If, while the air is thickened\r\nby this frosty drizzle, the calendar should happen to indicate that the\r\nblessed vernal season is already six weeks old, it will be admitted\r\nthat no depressing influence is absent from the scene. This fact was\r\nkeenly felt on a certain 12th of May, upwards of thirty years since, by\r\na lady who stood looking out of one of the windows of the best hotel in\r\nthe ancient city of Boston. She had stood there for half an hour\xe2\x80\x94stood\r\nthere, that is, at intervals; for from time to time she turned back\r\ninto the room and measured its length with a restless step. In the\r\nchimney-place was a red-hot fire which emitted a small blue flame; and\r\nin front of the fire, at a table, sat a young man who was busily plying\r\na pencil. He had a number of sheets of paper cut into small equal\r\nsquares, and he was apparently covering them with pictorial\r\ndesigns\xe2\x80\x94strange-looking figures. He worked rapidly and attentively,\r\nsometimes threw back his head and held out his drawing at arm\xe2\x80\x99s-length,\r\nand kept up a soft, gay-sounding humming and whistling. The lady\r\nbrushed past him in her walk; her much-trimmed skirts were voluminous.\r\nShe never dropped her eyes upon his work; she only turned them,\r\noccasionally, as she passed, to a mirror suspended above the\r\ntoilet-table on the other side of the room. Here she paused a moment,\r\ngave a pinch to her waist with her two hands, or raised these\r\nmembers\xe2\x80\x94they were very plump and pretty\xe2\x80\x94to the multifold braids of her\r\nhair, with a movement half caressing, half corrective. An attentive\r\nobserver might have fancied that during these periods of desultory\r\nself-inspection her face forgot its melancholy; but as soon as she\r\nneared the window again it began to proclaim that she was a very\r\nill-pleased woman. And indeed, in what met her eyes there was little to\r\nbe pleased with. The window-panes were battered by the sleet; the\r\nhead-stones in the grave-yard beneath seemed to be holding themselves\r\naskance to keep it out of their faces. A tall iron railing protected\r\nthem from the street, and on the other side of the railing an\r\nassemblage of Bostonians were trampling about in the liquid snow. Many\r\nof them were looking up and down; they appeared to be waiting for\r\nsomething. From time to time a strange vehicle drew near to the place\r\nwhere they stood,\xe2\x80\x94such a vehicle as the lady at the window, in spite of\r\na considerable acquaintance with human inventions, had never seen\r\nbefore: a huge, low omnibus, painted in brilliant colors, and decorated\r\napparently with jangling bells, attached to a species of groove in the\r\npavement, through which it was dragged, with a great deal of rumbling,\r\nbouncing and scratching, by a couple of remarkably small horses. When\r\nit reached a certain point the people in front of the grave-yard, of\r\nwhom much the greater number were women, carrying satchels and parcels,\r\nprojected themselves upon it in a compact body\xe2\x80\x94a movement suggesting\r\nthe scramble for places in a life-boat at sea\xe2\x80\x94and were engulfed in its\r\nlarge interior. Then the life-boat\xe2\x80\x94or the life-car, as the lady at the\r\nwindow of the hotel vaguely designated it\xe2\x80\x94went bumping and jingling\r\naway upon its invisible wheels, with the helmsman (the man at the\r\nwheel) guiding its course incongruously from the prow. This phenomenon\r\nwas repeated every three minutes, and the supply of eagerly-moving\r\nwomen in cloaks, bearing reticules and bundles, renewed itself in the\r\nmost liberal manner. On the other side of the grave-yard was a row of\r\nsmall red brick houses, showing a series of homely, domestic-looking\r\nbacks; at the end opposite the hotel a tall wooden church-spire,\r\npainted white, rose high into the vagueness of the snow-flakes. The\r\nlady at the window looked at it for some time; for reasons of her own\r\nshe thought it the ugliest thing she had ever seen. She hated it, she\r\ndespised it; it threw her into a state of irritation that was quite out\r\nof proportion to any sensible motive. She had never known herself to\r\ncare so much about church-spires.\r\n\r\nShe was not pretty; but even when it expressed perplexed irritation her\r\nface was most interesting and agreeable. Neither was she in her first\r\nyouth; yet, though slender, with a great deal of extremely\r\nwell-fashioned roundness of contour\xe2\x80\x94a suggestion both of maturity and\r\nflexibility\xe2\x80\x94she carried her three and thirty years as a light-wristed\r\nHebe might have carried a brimming wine-cup. Her complexion was\r\nfatigued, as the French say; her mouth was large, her lips too full,\r\nher teeth uneven, her chin rather commonly modeled; she had a thick\r\nnose, and when she smiled\xe2\x80\x94she was constantly smiling\xe2\x80\x94the lines beside\r\nit rose too high, toward her eyes. But these eyes were charming: gray\r\nin color, brilliant, quickly glancing, gently resting, full of\r\nintelligence. Her forehead was very low\xe2\x80\x94it was her only handsome\r\nfeature; and she had a great abundance of crisp dark hair, finely\r\nfrizzled, which was always braided in a manner that suggested some\r\nSouthern or Eastern, some remotely foreign, woman. She had a large\r\ncollection of ear-rings, and wore them in alternation; and they seemed\r\nto give a point to her Oriental or exotic aspect. A compliment had once\r\nbeen paid her, which, being repeated to her, gave her greater pleasure\r\nthan anything she had ever heard. \xe2\x80\x9cA pretty woman?\xe2\x80\x9d someone had said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, her features are very bad.\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about her features,\xe2\x80\x9d a\r\nvery discerning observer had answered; \xe2\x80\x9cbut she carries her head like a\r\npretty woman.\xe2\x80\x9d You may imagine whether, after this, she carried her\r\nhead less becomingly.\r\n\r\nShe turned away from the window at last, pressing her hands to her\r\neyes. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too horrible!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall go back\xe2\x80\x94I shall go\r\nback!\xe2\x80\x9d And she flung herself into a chair before the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait a little, dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man softly, sketching away\r\nat his little scraps of paper.\r\n\r\nThe lady put out her foot; it was very small, and there was an immense\r\nrosette on her slipper. She fixed her eyes for a while on this\r\nornament, and then she looked at the glowing bed of anthracite coal in\r\nthe grate. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever see anything so hideous as that fire?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\ndemanded. \xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever see anything so\xe2\x80\x94so _affreux_ as\xe2\x80\x94as everything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe spoke English with perfect purity; but she brought out this French\r\nepithet in a manner that indicated that she was accustomed to using\r\nFrench epithets.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the fire is very pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, glancing at it a\r\nmoment. \xe2\x80\x9cThose little blue tongues, dancing on top of the crimson\r\nembers, are extremely picturesque. They are like a fire in an\r\nalchemist\xe2\x80\x99s laboratory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too good-natured, my dear,\xe2\x80\x9d his companion declared.\r\n\r\nThe young man held out one of his drawings, with his head on one side.\r\nHis tongue was gently moving along his under-lip. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-natured\xe2\x80\x94yes.\r\nToo good-natured\xe2\x80\x94no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are irritating,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, looking at her slipper.\r\n\r\nHe began to retouch his sketch. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you mean simply that you are\r\nirritated.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, for that, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d said his companion, with a little bitter laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s the darkest day of my life\xe2\x80\x94and you know what that means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWait till tomorrow,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, we have made a great mistake. If there is any doubt about it\r\ntoday, there certainly will be none tomorrow. _Ce sera clair, au\r\nmoins!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man was silent a few moments, driving his pencil. Then at\r\nlast, \xe2\x80\x9cThere are no such things as mistakes,\xe2\x80\x9d he affirmed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery true\xe2\x80\x94for those who are not clever enough to perceive them. Not to\r\nrecognize one\xe2\x80\x99s mistakes\xe2\x80\x94that would be happiness in life,\xe2\x80\x9d the lady\r\nwent on, still looking at her pretty foot.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, always intent upon his\r\ndrawing, \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s the first time you have told me I am not clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, by your own theory I can\xe2\x80\x99t call it a mistake,\xe2\x80\x9d answered his\r\nsister, pertinently enough.\r\n\r\nThe young man gave a clear, fresh laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou, at least, are clever\r\nenough, dearest sister,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was not so when I proposed this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas it you who proposed it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked her brother.\r\n\r\nShe turned her head and gave him a little stare. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you desire the\r\ncredit of it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you like, I will take the blame,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking up with a smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d she rejoined in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cyou make no difference in these\r\nthings. You have no sense of property.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man gave his joyous laugh again. \xe2\x80\x9cIf that means I have no\r\nproperty, you are right!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t joke about your poverty,\xe2\x80\x9d said his sister. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is quite as\r\nvulgar as to boast about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poverty! I have just finished a drawing that will bring me fifty\r\nfrancs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cVoyons,\xe2\x80\x9d_ said the lady, putting out her hand.\r\n\r\nHe added a touch or two, and then gave her his sketch. She looked at\r\nit, but she went on with her idea of a moment before. \xe2\x80\x9cIf a woman were\r\nto ask you to marry her you would say, \xe2\x80\x98Certainly, my dear, with\r\npleasure!\xe2\x80\x99 And you would marry her and be ridiculously happy. Then at\r\nthe end of three months you would say to her, \xe2\x80\x98You know that blissful\r\nday when I begged you to be mine!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man had risen from the table, stretching his arms a little;\r\nhe walked to the window. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is a description of a charming nature,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, you have a charming nature; I regard that as our capital. If\r\nI had not been convinced of that I should never have taken the risk of\r\nbringing you to this dreadful country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis comical country, this delightful country!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the young\r\nman, and he broke into the most animated laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it those women scrambling into the omnibus?\xe2\x80\x9d asked his companion.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you suppose is the attraction?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose there is a very good-looking man inside,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young\r\nman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn each of them? They come along in hundreds, and the men in this\r\ncountry don\xe2\x80\x99t seem at all handsome. As for the women\xe2\x80\x94I have never seen\r\nso many at once since I left the convent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe women are very pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d her brother declared, \xe2\x80\x9cand the whole\r\naffair is very amusing. I must make a sketch of it.\xe2\x80\x9d And he came back\r\nto the table quickly, and picked up his utensils\xe2\x80\x94a small\r\nsketching-board, a sheet of paper, and three or four crayons. He took\r\nhis place at the window with these things, and stood there glancing\r\nout, plying his pencil with an air of easy skill. While he worked he\r\nwore a brilliant smile. Brilliant is indeed the word at this moment for\r\nhis strongly-lighted face. He was eight and twenty years old; he had a\r\nshort, slight, well-made figure. Though he bore a noticeable\r\nresemblance to his sister, he was a better favored person: fair-haired,\r\nclear-faced, witty-looking, with a delicate finish of feature and an\r\nexpression at once urbane and not at all serious, a warm blue eye, an\r\neyebrow finely drawn and excessively arched\xe2\x80\x94an eyebrow which, if ladies\r\nwrote sonnets to those of their lovers, might have been made the\r\nsubject of such a piece of verse\xe2\x80\x94and a light moustache that flourished\r\nupwards as if blown that way by the breath of a constant smile. There\r\nwas something in his physiognomy at once benevolent and picturesque.\r\nBut, as I have hinted, it was not at all serious. The young man\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\nwas, in this respect, singular; it was not at all serious, and yet it\r\ninspired the liveliest confidence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe sure you put in plenty of snow,\xe2\x80\x9d said his sister. \xe2\x80\x9c_Bont\xc3\xa9 divine_,\r\nwhat a climate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall leave the sketch all white, and I shall put in the little\r\nfigures in black,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man answered, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I shall call\r\nit\xe2\x80\x94what is that line in Keats?\xe2\x80\x94Mid-May\xe2\x80\x99s Eldest Child!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t remember,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady, \xe2\x80\x9cthat mamma ever told me it was like\r\nthis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMamma never told you anything disagreeable. And it\xe2\x80\x99s not like\r\nthis\xe2\x80\x94every day. You will see that tomorrow we shall have a splendid\r\nday.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Qu\xe2\x80\x99en savez-vous?_ Tomorrow I shall go away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere shall you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnywhere away from here. Back to Silberstadt. I shall write to the\r\nReigning Prince.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man turned a little and looked at her, with his crayon\r\npoised. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Eugenia,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cwere you so happy at sea?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia got up; she still held in her hand the drawing her brother had\r\ngiven her. It was a bold, expressive sketch of a group of miserable\r\npeople on the deck of a steamer, clinging together and clutching at\r\neach other, while the vessel lurched downward, at a terrific angle,\r\ninto the hollow of a wave. It was extremely clever, and full of a sort\r\nof tragi-comical power. Eugenia dropped her eyes upon it and made a sad\r\ngrimace. \xe2\x80\x9cHow can you draw such odious scenes?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI should\r\nlike to throw it into the fire!\xe2\x80\x9d And she tossed the paper away. Her\r\nbrother watched, quietly, to see where it went. It fluttered down to\r\nthe floor, where he let it lie. She came toward the window, pinching in\r\nher waist. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you reproach me\xe2\x80\x94abuse me?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I\r\nshould feel better then. Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you tell me that you hate me for\r\nbringing you here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you would not believe it. I adore you, dear sister! I am\r\ndelighted to be here, and I am charmed with the prospect.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what had taken possession of me. I had lost my head,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nEugenia went on.\r\n\r\nThe young man, on his side, went on plying his pencil. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is evidently\r\na most curious and interesting country. Here we are, and I mean to\r\nenjoy it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis companion turned away with an impatient step, but presently came\r\nback. \xe2\x80\x9cHigh spirits are doubtless an excellent thing,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut\r\nyou give one too much of them, and I can\xe2\x80\x99t see that they have done you\r\nany good.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man stared, with lifted eyebrows, smiling; he tapped his\r\nhandsome nose with his pencil. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have made me happy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was the least they could do; they have made you nothing else. You\r\nhave gone through life thanking fortune for such very small favors that\r\nshe has never put herself to any trouble for you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe must have put herself to a little, I think, to present me with so\r\nadmirable a sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe serious, Felix. You forget that I am your elder.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith a sister, then, so elderly!\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Felix, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI hoped\r\nwe had left seriousness in Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fancy you will find it here. Remember that you are nearly thirty\r\nyears old, and that you are nothing but an obscure Bohemian\xe2\x80\x94a penniless\r\ncorrespondent of an illustrated newspaper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cObscure as much as you please, but not so much of a Bohemian as you\r\nthink. And not at all penniless! I have a hundred pounds in my pocket.\r\nI have an engagement to make fifty sketches, and I mean to paint the\r\nportraits of all our cousins, and of all _their_ cousins, at a hundred\r\ndollars a head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not ambitious,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are, dear Baroness,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man replied.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness was silent a moment, looking out at the sleet-darkened\r\ngrave-yard and the bumping horse-cars. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am ambitious,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nat last. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd my ambition has brought me to this dreadful place!\xe2\x80\x9d She\r\nglanced about her\xe2\x80\x94the room had a certain vulgar nudity; the bed and the\r\nwindow were curtainless\xe2\x80\x94and she gave a little passionate sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cPoor\r\nold ambition!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. Then she flung herself down upon a sofa\r\nwhich stood near against the wall, and covered her face with her hands.\r\n\r\nHer brother went on with his drawing, rapidly and skillfully; after\r\nsome moments he sat down beside her and showed her his sketch. \xe2\x80\x9cNow,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t you think that\xe2\x80\x99s pretty good for an obscure Bohemian?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have knocked off another fifty francs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia glanced at the little picture as he laid it on her lap. \xe2\x80\x9cYes,\r\nit is very clever,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. And in a moment she added, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nsuppose our cousins do that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGet into those things, and look like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix meditated awhile. \xe2\x80\x9cI really can\xe2\x80\x99t say. It will be interesting to\r\ndiscover.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, the rich people can\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you very sure they are rich?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Felix, lightly.\r\n\r\nHis sister slowly turned in her place, looking at him. \xe2\x80\x9cHeavenly\r\npowers!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have a way of bringing out things!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt will certainly be much pleasanter if they are rich,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix\r\ndeclared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you suppose if I had not known they were rich I would ever have\r\ncome?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man met his sister\xe2\x80\x99s somewhat peremptory eye with his bright,\r\ncontented glance. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it certainly will be pleasanter,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is all I expect of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t count upon\r\ntheir being clever or friendly\xe2\x80\x94at first\xe2\x80\x94or elegant or interesting. But\r\nI assure you I insist upon their being rich.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix leaned his head upon the back of the sofa and looked awhile at\r\nthe oblong patch of sky to which the window served as frame. The snow\r\nwas ceasing; it seemed to him that the sky had begun to brighten. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ncount upon their being rich,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cand powerful, and\r\nclever, and friendly, and elegant, and interesting, and generally\r\ndelightful! _Tu vas voir_.\xe2\x80\x9d And he bent forward and kissed his sister.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook there!\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cAs a portent, even while I speak, the sky is\r\nturning the color of gold; the day is going to be splendid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd indeed, within five minutes the weather had changed. The sun broke\r\nout through the snow-clouds and jumped into the Baroness\xe2\x80\x99s room.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Bont\xc3\xa9 divine_,\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed this lady, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat a climate!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will go out and see the world,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\nAnd after a while they went out. The air had grown warm as well as\r\nbrilliant; the sunshine had dried the pavements. They walked about the\r\nstreets at hazard, looking at the people and the houses, the shops and\r\nthe vehicles, the blazing blue sky and the muddy crossings, the\r\nhurrying men and the slow-strolling maidens, the fresh red bricks and\r\nthe bright green trees, the extraordinary mixture of smartness and\r\nshabbiness. From one hour to another the day had grown vernal; even in\r\nthe bustling streets there was an odor of earth and blossom. Felix was\r\nimmensely entertained. He had called it a comical country, and he went\r\nabout laughing at everything he saw. You would have said that American\r\ncivilization expressed itself to his sense in a tissue of capital\r\njokes. The jokes were certainly excellent, and the young man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nmerriment was joyous and genial. He possessed what is called the\r\npictorial sense; and this first glimpse of democratic manners stirred\r\nthe same sort of attention that he would have given to the movements of\r\na lively young person with a bright complexion. Such attention would\r\nhave been demonstrative and complimentary; and in the present case\r\nFelix might have passed for an undispirited young exile revisiting the\r\nhaunts of his childhood. He kept looking at the violent blue of the\r\nsky, at the scintillating air, at the scattered and multiplied patches\r\nof color.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Comme c\xe2\x80\x99est bariol\xc3\xa9_, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d he said to his sister in that foreign\r\ntongue which they both appeared to feel a mysterious prompting\r\noccasionally to use.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is _bariol\xc3\xa9_ indeed,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like the\r\ncoloring; it hurts my eyes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt shows how extremes meet,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cInstead of\r\ncoming to the West we seem to have gone to the East. The way the sky\r\ntouches the house-tops is just like Cairo; and the red and blue\r\nsign-boards patched over the face of everything remind one of Mahometan\r\ndecorations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young women are not Mahometan,\xe2\x80\x9d said his companion. \xe2\x80\x9cThey can\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nsaid to hide their faces. I never saw anything so bold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank Heaven they don\xe2\x80\x99t hide their faces!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cTheir faces\r\nare uncommonly pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, their faces are often very pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, who was a\r\nvery clever woman. She was too clever a woman not to be capable of a\r\ngreat deal of just and fine observation. She clung more closely than\r\nusual to her brother\xe2\x80\x99s arm; she was not exhilarated, as he was; she\r\nsaid very little, but she noted a great many things and made her\r\nreflections. She was a little excited; she felt that she had indeed\r\ncome to a strange country, to make her fortune. Superficially, she was\r\nconscious of a good deal of irritation and displeasure; the Baroness\r\nwas a very delicate and fastidious person. Of old, more than once, she\r\nhad gone, for entertainment\xe2\x80\x99s sake and in brilliant company, to a fair\r\nin a provincial town. It seemed to her now that she was at an enormous\r\nfair\xe2\x80\x94that the entertainment and the _d\xc3\xa9sagr\xc3\xa9ments_ were very much the\r\nsame. She found herself alternately smiling and shrinking; the show was\r\nvery curious, but it was probable, from moment to moment, that one\r\nwould be jostled. The Baroness had never seen so many people walking\r\nabout before; she had never been so mixed up with people she did not\r\nknow. But little by little she felt that this fair was a more serious\r\nundertaking. She went with her brother into a large public garden,\r\nwhich seemed very pretty, but where she was surprised at seeing no\r\ncarriages. The afternoon was drawing to a close; the coarse, vivid\r\ngrass and the slender tree-boles were gilded by the level\r\nsunbeams\xe2\x80\x94gilded as with gold that was fresh from the mine. It was the\r\nhour at which ladies should come out for an airing and roll past a\r\nhedge of pedestrians, holding their parasols askance. Here, however,\r\nEugenia observed no indications of this custom, the absence of which\r\nwas more anomalous as there was a charming avenue of remarkably\r\ngraceful, arching elms in the most convenient contiguity to a large,\r\ncheerful street, in which, evidently, among the more prosperous members\r\nof the _bourgeoisie_, a great deal of pedestrianism went forward. Our\r\nfriends passed out into this well lighted promenade, and Felix noticed\r\na great many more pretty girls and called his sister\xe2\x80\x99s attention to\r\nthem. This latter measure, however, was superfluous; for the Baroness\r\nhad inspected, narrowly, these charming young ladies.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel an intimate conviction that our cousins are like that,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nFelix.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness hoped so, but this is not what she said. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are very\r\npretty,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut they are mere little girls. Where are the\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x94the women of thirty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf thirty-three, do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d her brother was going to ask; for he\r\nunderstood often both what she said and what she did not say. But he\r\nonly exclaimed upon the beauty of the sunset, while the Baroness, who\r\nhad come to seek her fortune, reflected that it would certainly be well\r\nfor her if the persons against whom she might need to measure herself\r\nshould all be mere little girls. The sunset was superb; they stopped to\r\nlook at it; Felix declared that he had never seen such a gorgeous\r\nmixture of colors. The Baroness also thought it splendid; and she was\r\nperhaps the more easily pleased from the fact that while she stood\r\nthere she was conscious of much admiring observation on the part of\r\nvarious nice-looking people who passed that way, and to whom a\r\ndistinguished, strikingly-dressed woman with a foreign air, exclaiming\r\nupon the beauties of nature on a Boston street corner in the French\r\ntongue, could not be an object of indifference. Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s spirits rose.\r\nShe surrendered herself to a certain tranquil gaiety. If she had come\r\nto seek her fortune, it seemed to her that her fortune would be easy to\r\nfind. There was a promise of it in the gorgeous purity of the western\r\nsky; there was an intimation in the mild, unimpertinent gaze of the\r\npassers of a certain natural facility in things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will not go back to Silberstadt, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot tomorrow,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNor write to the Reigning Prince?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall write to him that they evidently know nothing about him over\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will not believe you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cI advise you to let him\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix himself continued to be in high good humor. Brought up among\r\nancient customs and in picturesque cities, he yet found plenty of local\r\ncolor in the little Puritan metropolis. That evening, after dinner, he\r\ntold his sister that he should go forth early on the morrow to look up\r\ntheir cousins.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very impatient,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat can be more natural,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, \xe2\x80\x9cafter seeing all those pretty\r\ngirls today? If one\xe2\x80\x99s cousins are of that pattern, the sooner one knows\r\nthem the better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps they are not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cWe ought to have brought some\r\nletters\xe2\x80\x94to some other people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe other people would not be our kinsfolk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPossibly they would be none the worse for that,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness replied.\r\n\r\nHer brother looked at her with his eyebrows lifted. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was not what\r\nyou said when you first proposed to me that we should come out here and\r\nfraternize with our relatives. You said that it was the prompting of\r\nnatural affection; and when I suggested some reasons against it you\r\ndeclared that the _voix du sang_ should go before everything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou remember all that?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVividly! I was greatly moved by it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was walking up and down the room, as she had done in the morning;\r\nshe stopped in her walk and looked at her brother. She apparently was\r\ngoing to say something, but she checked herself and resumed her walk.\r\nThen, in a few moments, she said something different, which had the\r\neffect of an explanation of the suppression of her earlier thought.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will never be anything but a child, dear brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne would suppose that you, madam,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Felix, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cwere a\r\nthousand years old.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am\xe2\x80\x94sometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go, then, and announce to our cousins the arrival of a\r\npersonage so extraordinary. They will immediately come and pay you\r\ntheir respects.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia paced the length of the room again, and then she stopped before\r\nher brother, laying her hand upon his arm. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are not to come and\r\nsee me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are not to allow that. That is not the way I\r\nshall meet them first.\xe2\x80\x9d And in answer to his interrogative glance she\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will go and examine, and report. You will come back and\r\ntell me who they are and what they are; their number, gender, their\r\nrespective ages\xe2\x80\x94all about them. Be sure you observe everything; be\r\nready to describe to me the locality, the accessories\xe2\x80\x94how shall I say\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x94the _mise en sc\xc3\xa8ne_. Then, at my own time, at my own hour, under\r\ncircumstances of my own choosing, I will go to them. I will present\r\nmyself\xe2\x80\x94I will appear before them!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, this time\r\nphrasing her idea with a certain frankness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what message am I to take to them?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Felix, who had a lively\r\nfaith in the justness of his sister\xe2\x80\x99s arrangements.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him a moment\xe2\x80\x94at his expression of agreeable veracity;\r\nand, with that justness that he admired, she replied, \xe2\x80\x9cSay what you\r\nplease. Tell my story in the way that seems to you most\xe2\x80\x94natural.\xe2\x80\x9d And\r\nshe bent her forehead for him to kiss.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II\r\n\r\n\r\nThe next day was splendid, as Felix had prophesied; if the winter had\r\nsuddenly leaped into spring, the spring had for the moment as quickly\r\nleaped into summer. This was an observation made by a young girl who\r\ncame out of a large square house in the country, and strolled about in\r\nthe spacious garden which separated it from a muddy road. The flowering\r\nshrubs and the neatly-disposed plants were basking in the abundant\r\nlight and warmth; the transparent shade of the great elms\xe2\x80\x94they were\r\nmagnificent trees\xe2\x80\x94seemed to thicken by the hour; and the intensely\r\nhabitual stillness offered a submissive medium to the sound of a\r\ndistant church-bell. The young girl listened to the church-bell; but\r\nshe was not dressed for church. She was bare-headed; she wore a white\r\nmuslin waist, with an embroidered border, and the skirt of her dress\r\nwas of colored muslin. She was a young lady of some two or three and\r\ntwenty years of age, and though a young person of her sex walking\r\nbare-headed in a garden, of a Sunday morning in spring-time, can, in\r\nthe nature of things, never be a displeasing object, you would not have\r\npronounced this innocent Sabbath-breaker especially pretty. She was\r\ntall and pale, thin and a little awkward; her hair was fair and\r\nperfectly straight; her eyes were dark, and they had the singularity of\r\nseeming at once dull and restless\xe2\x80\x94differing herein, as you see, fatally\r\nfrom the ideal \xe2\x80\x9cfine eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d which we always imagine to be both\r\nbrilliant and tranquil. The doors and windows of the large square house\r\nwere all wide open, to admit the purifying sunshine, which lay in\r\ngenerous patches upon the floor of a wide, high, covered piazza\r\nadjusted to two sides of the mansion\xe2\x80\x94a piazza on which several\r\nstraw-bottomed rocking-chairs and half a dozen of those small\r\ncylindrical stools in green and blue porcelain, which suggest an\r\naffiliation between the residents and the Eastern trade, were\r\nsymmetrically disposed. It was an ancient house\xe2\x80\x94ancient in the sense of\r\nbeing eighty years old; it was built of wood, painted a clean, clear,\r\nfaded gray, and adorned along the front, at intervals, with flat wooden\r\npilasters, painted white. These pilasters appeared to support a kind of\r\nclassic pediment, which was decorated in the middle by a large triple\r\nwindow in a boldly carved frame, and in each of its smaller angles by a\r\nglazed circular aperture. A large white door, furnished with a\r\nhighly-polished brass knocker, presented itself to the rural-looking\r\nroad, with which it was connected by a spacious pathway, paved with\r\nworn and cracked, but very clean, bricks. Behind it there were meadows\r\nand orchards, a barn and a pond; and facing it, a short distance along\r\nthe road, on the opposite side, stood a smaller house, painted white,\r\nwith external shutters painted green, a little garden on one hand and\r\nan orchard on the other. All this was shining in the morning air,\r\nthrough which the simple details of the picture addressed themselves to\r\nthe eye as distinctly as the items of a \xe2\x80\x9csum\xe2\x80\x9d in addition.\r\n\r\nA second young lady presently came out of the house, across the piazza,\r\ndescended into the garden and approached the young girl of whom I have\r\nspoken. This second young lady was also thin and pale; but she was\r\nolder than the other; she was shorter; she had dark, smooth hair. Her\r\neyes, unlike the other\xe2\x80\x99s, were quick and bright; but they were not at\r\nall restless. She wore a straw bonnet with white ribbons, and a long,\r\nred, India scarf, which, on the front of her dress, reached to her\r\nfeet. In her hand she carried a little key.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9care you very sure you had better not go to\r\nchurch?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at her a moment, plucked a small sprig from a\r\nlilac-bush, smelled it and threw it away. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not very sure of\r\nanything!\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\nThe other young lady looked straight past her, at the distant pond,\r\nwhich lay shining between the long banks of fir trees. Then she said in\r\na very soft voice, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the key of the dining-room closet. I think\r\nyou had better have it, if anyone should want anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is there to want anything?\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall be all\r\nalone in the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomeone may come,\xe2\x80\x9d said her companion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean Mr. Brand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Gertrude. He may like a piece of cake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t like men that are always eating cake!\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude declared,\r\ngiving a pull at the lilac-bush.\r\n\r\nHer companion glanced at her, and then looked down on the ground. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink father expected you would come to church,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat shall\r\nI say to him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSay I have a bad headache.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWould that be true?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the elder lady, looking straight at the\r\npond again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, Charlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d said the younger one simply.\r\n\r\nCharlotte transferred her quiet eyes to her companion\xe2\x80\x99s face. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nafraid you are feeling restless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am feeling as I always feel,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude replied, in the same tone.\r\n\r\nCharlotte turned away; but she stood there a moment. Presently she\r\nlooked down at the front of her dress. \xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t it seem to you,\r\nsomehow, as if my scarf were too long?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nGertrude walked half round her, looking at the scarf. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think\r\nyou wear it right,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should I wear it, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; differently from that. You should draw it differently\r\nover your shoulders, round your elbows; you should look differently\r\nbehind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow should I look?\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I can tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, plucking out the scarf a\r\nlittle behind. \xe2\x80\x9cI could do it myself, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think I can explain\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte, by a movement of her elbows, corrected the laxity that had\r\ncome from her companion\xe2\x80\x99s touch. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, some day you must do it for me.\r\nIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t matter now. Indeed, I don\xe2\x80\x99t think it matters,\xe2\x80\x9d she added,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chow one looks behind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say it mattered more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cThen you don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwho may be observing you. You are not on your guard. You can\xe2\x80\x99t try to\r\nlook pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte received this declaration with extreme gravity. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink one should ever try to look pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d she rejoined, earnestly.\r\n\r\nHer companion was silent. Then she said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell, perhaps it\xe2\x80\x99s not of\r\nmuch use.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte looked at her a little, and then kissed her. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will\r\nbe better when we come back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sister, I am very well!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\nCharlotte went down the large brick walk to the garden gate; her\r\ncompanion strolled slowly toward the house. At the gate Charlotte met a\r\nyoung man, who was coming in\xe2\x80\x94a tall, fair young man, wearing a high hat\r\nand a pair of thread gloves. He was handsome, but rather too stout. He\r\nhad a pleasant smile. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Brand!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the young lady.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came to see whether your sister was not going to church,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nyoung man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe says she is not going; but I am very glad you have come. I think\r\nif you were to talk to her a little\xe2\x80\x9d.... And Charlotte lowered her\r\nvoice. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems as if she were restless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand smiled down on the young lady from his great height. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall\r\nbe very glad to talk to her. For that I should be willing to absent\r\nmyself from almost any occasion of worship, however attractive.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I suppose you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte, softly, as if positive\r\nacceptance of this proposition might be dangerous. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am afraid I\r\nshall be late.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you will have a pleasant sermon,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Mr. Gilman is always pleasant,\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte answered. And she went\r\non her way.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand went into the garden, where Gertrude, hearing the gate close\r\nbehind him, turned and looked at him. For a moment she watched him\r\ncoming; then she turned away. But almost immediately she corrected this\r\nmovement, and stood still, facing him. He took off his hat and wiped\r\nhis forehead as he approached. Then he put on his hat again and held\r\nout his hand. His hat being removed, you would have perceived that his\r\nforehead was very large and smooth, and his hair abundant but rather\r\ncolorless. His nose was too large, and his mouth and eyes were too\r\nsmall; but for all this he was, as I have said, a young man of striking\r\nappearance. The expression of his little clean-colored blue eyes was\r\nirresistibly gentle and serious; he looked, as the phrase is, as good\r\nas gold. The young girl, standing in the garden path, glanced, as he\r\ncame up, at his thread gloves.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hoped you were going to church,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI wanted to walk with\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very much obliged to you,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not going to\r\nchurch.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had shaken hands with him; he held her hand a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you any\r\nspecial reason for not going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Mr. Brand,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I ask what it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him smiling; and in her smile, as I have intimated, there\r\nwas a certain dullness. But mingled with this dullness was something\r\nsweet and suggestive. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause the sky is so blue!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe looked at the sky, which was magnificent, and then said, smiling\r\ntoo, \xe2\x80\x9cI have heard of young ladies staying at home for bad weather, but\r\nnever for good. Your sister, whom I met at the gate, tells me you are\r\ndepressed,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDepressed? I am never depressed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, surely, sometimes,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Brand, as if he thought this a\r\nregrettable account of one\xe2\x80\x99s self.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am never depressed,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I am sometimes wicked.\r\nWhen I am wicked I am in high spirits. I was wicked just now to my\r\nsister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat did you do to her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI said things that puzzled her\xe2\x80\x94on purpose.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy did you do that, Miss Gertrude?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the young man.\r\n\r\nShe began to smile again. \xe2\x80\x9cBecause the sky is so blue!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say things that puzzle _me_,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI always know when I do it,\xe2\x80\x9d proceeded Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cBut people puzzle me\r\nmore, I think. And they don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is very interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand observed, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou told me to tell you about my\xe2\x80\x94my struggles,\xe2\x80\x9d the young girl went\r\non.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet us talk about them. I have so many things to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude turned away a moment; and then, turning back, \xe2\x80\x9cYou had better\r\ngo to church,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man urged, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I have always one thing to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at him a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t say it now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are all alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, taking off his hat; \xe2\x80\x9call alone in\r\nthis beautiful Sunday stillness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked around her, at the breaking buds, the shining distance,\r\nthe blue sky to which she had referred as a pretext for her\r\nirregularities. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the reason,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cwhy I don\xe2\x80\x99t want you to\r\nspeak. Do me a favor; go to church.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I speak when I come back?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you are still disposed,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether you are wicked,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you are certainly\r\npuzzling.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had turned away; she raised her hands to her ears. He looked at her\r\na moment, and then he slowly walked to church.\r\n\r\nShe wandered for a while about the garden, vaguely and without purpose.\r\nThe church-bell had stopped ringing; the stillness was complete. This\r\nyoung lady relished highly, on occasions, the sense of being alone\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nabsence of the whole family and the emptiness of the house. Today,\r\napparently, the servants had also gone to church; there was never a\r\nfigure at the open windows; behind the house there was no stout negress\r\nin a red turban, lowering the bucket into the great shingle-hooded\r\nwell. And the front door of the big, unguarded home stood open, with\r\nthe trustfulness of the golden age; or what is more to the purpose,\r\nwith that of New England\xe2\x80\x99s silvery prime. Gertrude slowly passed\r\nthrough it, and went from one of the empty rooms to the other\xe2\x80\x94large,\r\nclear-colored rooms, with white wainscots, ornamented with thin-legged\r\nmahogany furniture, and, on the walls, with old-fashioned engravings,\r\nchiefly of scriptural subjects, hung very high. This agreeable sense of\r\nsolitude, of having the house to herself, of which I have spoken,\r\nalways excited Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s imagination; she could not have told you why,\r\nand neither can her humble historian. It always seemed to her that she\r\nmust do something particular\xe2\x80\x94that she must honor the occasion; and\r\nwhile she roamed about, wondering what she could do, the occasion\r\nusually came to an end. Today she wondered more than ever. At last she\r\ntook down a book; there was no library in the house, but there were\r\nbooks in all the rooms. None of them were forbidden books, and Gertrude\r\nhad not stopped at home for the sake of a chance to climb to the\r\ninaccessible shelves. She possessed herself of a very obvious\r\nvolume\xe2\x80\x94one of the series of the _Arabian Nights_\xe2\x80\x94and she brought it out\r\ninto the portico and sat down with it in her lap. There, for a quarter\r\nof an hour, she read the history of the loves of the Prince\r\nCamaralzaman and the Princess Badoura. At last, looking up, she beheld,\r\nas it seemed to her, the Prince Camaralzaman standing before her. A\r\nbeautiful young man was making her a very low bow\xe2\x80\x94a magnificent bow,\r\nsuch as she had never seen before. He appeared to have dropped from the\r\nclouds; he was wonderfully handsome; he smiled\xe2\x80\x94smiled as if he were\r\nsmiling on purpose. Extreme surprise, for a moment, kept Gertrude\r\nsitting still; then she rose, without even keeping her finger in her\r\nbook. The young man, with his hat in his hand, still looked at her,\r\nsmiling and smiling. It was very strange.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you kindly tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the mysterious visitor, at last,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cwhether I have the honor of speaking to Miss Wentworth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Gertrude Wentworth,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured the young woman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen\xe2\x80\x94then\xe2\x80\x94I have the honor\xe2\x80\x94the pleasure\xe2\x80\x94of being your cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man had so much the character of an apparition that this\r\nannouncement seemed to complete his unreality. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat cousin? Who are\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\nHe stepped back a few paces and looked up at the house; then glanced\r\nround him at the garden and the distant view. After this he burst out\r\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI see it must seem to you very strange,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. There was,\r\nafter all, something substantial in his laughter. Gertrude looked at\r\nhim from head to foot. Yes, he was remarkably handsome; but his smile\r\nwas almost a grimace. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is very still,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, coming nearer\r\nagain. And as she only looked at him, for reply, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cAre you all\r\nalone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEveryone has gone to church,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was afraid of that!\xe2\x80\x9d the young man exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I hope you are\r\nnot afraid of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to tell me who you are,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid of you!\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cI had a different plan. I\r\nexpected the servant would take in my card, and that you would put your\r\nheads together, before admitting me, and make out my identity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude had been wondering with a quick intensity which brought its\r\nresult; and the result seemed an answer\xe2\x80\x94a wondrous, delightful\r\nanswer\xe2\x80\x94to her vague wish that something would befall her. \xe2\x80\x9cI know\xe2\x80\x94I\r\nknow,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou come from Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe came two days ago. You have heard of us, then\xe2\x80\x94you believe in us?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have known, vaguely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, \xe2\x80\x9cthat we had relations in\r\nFrance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have you ever wanted to see us?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the young man.\r\n\r\nGertrude was silent a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cI have wanted to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad, then, it is you I have found. We wanted to see you, so we\r\ncame.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gertrude.\r\n\r\nThe young man looked round him, smiling still. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes; on purpose.\r\nDoes that sound as if we should bore you?\xe2\x80\x9d he added. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think we\r\nshall\xe2\x80\x94I really don\xe2\x80\x99t think we shall. We are rather fond of wandering,\r\ntoo; and we were glad of a pretext.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have just arrived?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Boston, two days ago. At the inn I asked for Mr. Wentworth. He must\r\nbe your father. They found out for me where he lived; they seemed often\r\nto have heard of him. I determined to come, without ceremony. So, this\r\nlovely morning, they set my face in the right direction, and told me to\r\nwalk straight before me, out of town. I came on foot because I wanted\r\nto see the country. I walked and walked, and here I am! It\xe2\x80\x99s a good\r\nmany miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is seven miles and a half,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, softly. Now that this\r\nhandsome young man was proving himself a reality she found herself\r\nvaguely trembling; she was deeply excited. She had never in her life\r\nspoken to a foreigner, and she had often thought it would be delightful\r\nto do so. Here was one who had suddenly been engendered by the Sabbath\r\nstillness for her private use; and such a brilliant, polite, smiling\r\none! She found time and means to compose herself, however: to remind\r\nherself that she must exercise a sort of official hospitality. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are\r\nvery\xe2\x80\x94very glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you come into the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd she moved toward the open door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are not afraid of me, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the young man again, with his\r\nlight laugh.\r\n\r\nShe wondered a moment, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cWe are not afraid\xe2\x80\x94here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cAh, comme vous devez avoir raison!\xe2\x80\x9d_ cried the young man, looking all\r\nround him, appreciatively. It was the first time that Gertrude had\r\nheard so many words of French spoken. They gave her something of a\r\nsensation. Her companion followed her, watching, with a certain\r\nexcitement of his own, this tall, interesting-looking girl, dressed in\r\nher clear, crisp muslin. He paused in the hall, where there was a broad\r\nwhite staircase with a white balustrade. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a pleasant house!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s lighter inside than it is out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s pleasanter here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, and she led the way into the\r\nparlor,\xe2\x80\x94a high, clean, rather empty-looking room. Here they stood\r\nlooking at each other,\xe2\x80\x94the young man smiling more than ever; Gertrude,\r\nvery serious, trying to smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you know my name,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI am called Felix Young.\r\nYour father is my uncle. My mother was his half sister, and older than\r\nhe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, \xe2\x80\x9cand she turned Roman Catholic and married in\r\nEurope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you know,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cShe married and she died. Your\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s family didn\xe2\x80\x99t like her husband. They called him a foreigner;\r\nbut he was not. My poor father was born in Sicily, but his parents were\r\nAmerican.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Sicily?\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix Young, \xe2\x80\x9cthat they had spent their lives in\r\nEurope. But they were very patriotic. And so are we.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you are Sicilian,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSicilian, no! Let\xe2\x80\x99s see. I was born at a little place\xe2\x80\x94a dear little\r\nplace\xe2\x80\x94in France. My sister was born at Vienna.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo you are French,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeaven forbid!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young man. Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were fixed upon\r\nhim almost insistently. He began to laugh again. \xe2\x80\x9cI can easily be\r\nFrench, if that will please you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are a foreigner of some sort,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf some sort\xe2\x80\x94yes; I suppose so. But who can say of what sort? I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink we have ever had occasion to settle the question. You know there\r\nare people like that. About their country, their religion, their\r\nprofession, they can\xe2\x80\x99t tell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude stood there gazing; she had not asked him to sit down. She had\r\nnever heard of people like that; she wanted to hear. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere do you\r\nlive?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey can\xe2\x80\x99t tell that, either!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid you will think\r\nthey are little better than vagabonds. I have lived\r\nanywhere\xe2\x80\x94everywhere. I really think I have lived in every city in\r\nEurope.\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude gave a little long soft exhalation. It made the young\r\nman smile at her again; and his smile made her blush a little. To take\r\nrefuge from blushing she asked him if, after his long walk, he was not\r\nhungry or thirsty. Her hand was in her pocket; she was fumbling with\r\nthe little key that her sister had given her. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, my dear young lady,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe said, clasping his hands a little, \xe2\x80\x9cif you could give me, in\r\ncharity, a glass of wine!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude gave a smile and a little nod, and went quickly out of the\r\nroom. Presently she came back with a very large decanter in one hand\r\nand a plate in the other, on which was placed a big, round cake with a\r\nfrosted top. Gertrude, in taking the cake from the closet, had had a\r\nmoment of acute consciousness that it composed the refection of which\r\nher sister had thought that Mr. Brand would like to partake. Her\r\nkinsman from across the seas was looking at the pale, high-hung\r\nengravings. When she came in he turned and smiled at her, as if they\r\nhad been old friends meeting after a separation. \xe2\x80\x9cYou wait upon me\r\nyourself?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI am served like the gods!\xe2\x80\x9d She had waited upon a\r\ngreat many people, but none of them had ever told her that. The\r\nobservation added a certain lightness to the step with which she went\r\nto a little table where there were some curious red glasses\xe2\x80\x94glasses\r\ncovered with little gold sprigs, which Charlotte used to dust every\r\nmorning with her own hands. Gertrude thought the glasses very handsome,\r\nand it was a pleasure to her to know that the wine was good; it was her\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s famous madeira. Felix Young thought it excellent; he wondered\r\nwhy he had been told that there was no wine in America. She cut him an\r\nimmense triangle out of the cake, and again she thought of Mr. Brand.\r\nFelix sat there, with his glass in one hand and his huge morsel of cake\r\nin the other\xe2\x80\x94eating, drinking, smiling, talking. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very hungry,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not at all tired; I am never tired. But I am very hungry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must stay to dinner,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cAt two o\xe2\x80\x99clock. They will\r\nall have come back from church; you will see the others.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho are the others?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cDescribe them all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou will see for yourself. It is you that must tell me; now, about\r\nyour sister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy sister is the Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\nOn hearing that his sister was a Baroness, Gertrude got up and walked\r\nabout slowly, in front of him. She was silent a moment. She was\r\nthinking of it. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t she come, too?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe did come; she is in Boston, at the hotel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will go and see her,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, looking at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe begs you will not!\xe2\x80\x9d the young man replied. \xe2\x80\x9cShe sends you her\r\nlove; she sent me to announce her. A Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster, who sent a\r\nbrilliant young man to \xe2\x80\x9cannounce\xe2\x80\x9d her; who was coming, as the Queen of\r\nSheba came to Solomon, to pay her \xe2\x80\x9crespects\xe2\x80\x9d to quiet Mr.\r\nWentworth\xe2\x80\x94such a personage presented herself to Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s vision with\r\na most effective unexpectedness. She wished to ask more about her; but\r\nshe hardly knew what could be predicated of a Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nshe\xe2\x80\x94is she\xe2\x80\x94married?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix had finished his cake and wine; he got up, fixing upon the young\r\ngirl his bright, expressive eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is married to a German\r\nprince\xe2\x80\x94Prince Adolf, of Silberstadt-Schreckenstein. He is not the\r\nreigning prince; he is a younger brother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude gazed at her informant; her lips were slightly parted. \xe2\x80\x9cIs she\r\na\xe2\x80\x94a _Princess_?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man; \xe2\x80\x9cher position is rather a singular one.\r\nIt\xe2\x80\x99s a morganatic marriage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMorganatic?\xe2\x80\x9d These were new names and new words to poor Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what they call a marriage, you know, contracted between a scion\r\nof a ruling house and\xe2\x80\x94and a common mortal. They made Eugenia a\r\nBaroness, poor woman; but that was all they could do. Now they want to\r\ndissolve the marriage. Prince Adolf, between ourselves, is a ninny; but\r\nhis brother, who is a clever man, has plans for him. Eugenia, naturally\r\nenough, makes difficulties; not, however, that I think she cares\r\nmuch\xe2\x80\x94she\xe2\x80\x99s a very clever woman; I\xe2\x80\x99m sure you\xe2\x80\x99ll like her\xe2\x80\x94but she wants\r\nto bother them. Just now everything is _en l\xe2\x80\x99air_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe cheerful, off-hand tone in which her visitor related this darkly\r\nromantic tale seemed to Gertrude very strange; but it seemed also to\r\nconvey a certain flattery to herself, a recognition of her wisdom and\r\ndignity. She felt a dozen impressions stirring within her, and\r\npresently the one that was uppermost found words. \xe2\x80\x9cThey want to\r\ndissolve her marriage?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it appears.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd against her will?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAgainst her right.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe must be very unhappy!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\nHer visitor looked at her, smiling; he raised his hand to the back of\r\nhis head and held it there a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cSo she says,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s her story. She told me to tell it you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me more,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I will leave that to her; she does it better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude gave her little excited sigh again. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, if she is unhappy,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am glad she has come to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had been so interested that she failed to notice the sound of a\r\nfootstep in the portico; and yet it was a footstep that she always\r\nrecognized. She heard it in the hall, and then she looked out of the\r\nwindow. They were all coming back from church\xe2\x80\x94her father, her sister\r\nand brother, and their cousins, who always came to dinner on Sunday.\r\nMr. Brand had come in first; he was in advance of the others, because,\r\napparently, he was still disposed to say what she had not wished him to\r\nsay an hour before. He came into the parlor, looking for Gertrude. He\r\nhad two little books in his hand. On seeing Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s companion he\r\nslowly stopped, looking at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs this a cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Felix.\r\n\r\nThen Gertrude saw that she must introduce him; but her ears, and, by\r\nsympathy, her lips, were full of all that he had been telling her.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is the Prince,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthe Prince of\r\nSilberstadt-Schreckenstein!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix burst out laughing, and Mr. Brand stood staring, while the\r\nothers, who had passed into the house, appeared behind him in the open\r\ndoorway.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III\r\n\r\n\r\nThat evening at dinner Felix Young gave his sister, the Baroness\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster, an account of his impressions. She saw that he had come back\r\nin the highest possible spirits; but this fact, to her own mind, was\r\nnot a reason for rejoicing. She had but a limited confidence in her\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s judgment; his capacity for taking rose-colored views was such\r\nas to vulgarize one of the prettiest of tints. Still, she supposed he\r\ncould be trusted to give her the mere facts; and she invited him with\r\nsome eagerness to communicate them. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose, at least, they didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nturn you out from the door;\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have been away some ten\r\nhours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurn me from the door!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThey took me to their\r\nhearts; they killed the fatted calf.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what you want to say: they are a collection of angels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExactly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are a collection of angels\xe2\x80\x94simply.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_C\xe2\x80\x99est bien vague_,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat are they like?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLike nothing you ever saw.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure I am much obliged; but that is hardly more definite.\r\nSeriously, they were glad to see you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnchanted. It has been the proudest day of my life. Never, never have\r\nI been so lionized! I assure you, I was cock of the walk. My dear\r\nsister,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, \xe2\x80\x9c_nous n\xe2\x80\x99avons qu\xe2\x80\x99\xc3\xa0 nous tenir_; we shall\r\nbe great swells!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster looked at him, and her eye exhibited a slight responsive\r\nspark. She touched her lips to a glass of wine, and then she said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDescribe them. Give me a picture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix drained his own glass. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, it\xe2\x80\x99s in the country, among the\r\nmeadows and woods; a wild sort of place, and yet not far from here.\r\nOnly, such a road, my dear! Imagine one of the Alpine glaciers\r\nreproduced in mud. But you will not spend much time on it, for they\r\nwant you to come and stay, once for all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, \xe2\x80\x9cthey want me to come and stay, once for all?\r\n_Bon_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s intensely rural, tremendously natural; and all overhung with this\r\nstrange white light, this far-away blue sky. There\xe2\x80\x99s a big wooden\r\nhouse\xe2\x80\x94a kind of three-story bungalow; it looks like a magnified\r\nNuremberg toy. There was a gentleman there that made a speech to me\r\nabout it and called it a \xe2\x80\x98venerable mansion;\xe2\x80\x99 but it looks as if it had\r\nbeen built last night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs it handsome\xe2\x80\x94is it elegant?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Baroness.\r\n\r\nFelix looked at her a moment, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s very clean! No splendors,\r\nno gilding, no troops of servants; rather straight-backed chairs. But\r\nyou might eat off the floors, and you can sit down on the stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat must be a privilege. And the inhabitants are straight-backed too,\r\nof course.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cthe inhabitants are charming.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn what style?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn a style of their own. How shall I describe it? It\xe2\x80\x99s primitive; it\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npatriarchal; it\xe2\x80\x99s the _ton_ of the golden age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd have they nothing golden but their _ton_? Are there no symptoms of\r\nwealth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say there was wealth without symptoms. A plain, homely way of\r\nlife: nothing for show, and very little for\xe2\x80\x94what shall I call it?\xe2\x80\x94for\r\nthe senses; but a great _aisance_, and a lot of money, out of sight,\r\nthat comes forward very quietly for subscriptions to institutions, for\r\nrepairing tenements, for paying doctor\xe2\x80\x99s bills; perhaps even for\r\nportioning daughters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the daughters?\xe2\x80\x9d Madame M\xc3\xbcnster demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cHow many are there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere are two, Charlotte and Gertrude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they pretty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of them,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich is that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man was silent, looking at his sister. \xe2\x80\x9cCharlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d he said\r\nat last.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him in return. \xe2\x80\x9cI see. You are in love with Gertrude.\r\nThey must be Puritans to their finger-tips; anything but gay!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, they are not gay,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix admitted. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are sober; they are even\r\nsevere. They are of a pensive cast; they take things hard. I think\r\nthere is something the matter with them; they have some melancholy\r\nmemory or some depressing expectation. It\xe2\x80\x99s not the epicurean\r\ntemperament. My uncle, Mr. Wentworth, is a tremendously high-toned old\r\nfellow; he looks as if he were undergoing martyrdom, not by fire, but\r\nby freezing. But we shall cheer them up; we shall do them good. They\r\nwill take a good deal of stirring up; but they are wonderfully kind and\r\ngentle. And they are appreciative. They think one clever; they think\r\none remarkable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very fine, so far as it goes,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cBut are we\r\nto be shut up to these three people, Mr. Wentworth and the two young\r\nwomen\xe2\x80\x94what did you say their names were\xe2\x80\x94Deborah and Hephzibah?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no; there is another little girl, a cousin of theirs, a very\r\npretty creature; a thorough little American. And then there is the son\r\nof the house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are coming to the gentlemen. What of the\r\nson of the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid he gets tipsy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe, then, has the epicurean temperament! How old is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a boy of twenty; a pretty young fellow, but I am afraid he has\r\nvulgar tastes. And then there is Mr. Brand\xe2\x80\x94a very tall young man, a\r\nsort of lay-priest. They seem to think a good deal of him, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nexactly make him out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is there nothing,\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Baroness, \xe2\x80\x9cbetween these\r\nextremes\xe2\x80\x94this mysterious ecclesiastic and that intemperate youth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, there is Mr. Acton. I think,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, with a nod\r\nat his sister, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you will like Mr. Acton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemember that I am very fastidious,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cHas he very\r\ngood manners?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe will have them with you. He is a man of the world; he has been to\r\nChina.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster gave a little laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cA man of the Chinese world! He\r\nmust be very interesting.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have an idea that he brought home a fortune,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is always interesting. Is he young, good-looking, clever?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is less than forty; he has a baldish head; he says witty things. I\r\nrather think,\xe2\x80\x9d added the young man, \xe2\x80\x9cthat he will admire the Baroness\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very possible,\xe2\x80\x9d said this lady. Her brother never knew how she\r\nwould take things; but shortly afterwards she declared that he had made\r\na very pretty description and that on the morrow she would go and see\r\nfor herself.\r\n\r\nThey mounted, accordingly, into a great barouche\xe2\x80\x94a vehicle as to which\r\nthe Baroness found nothing to criticise but the price that was asked\r\nfor it and the fact that the coachman wore a straw hat. (At Silberstadt\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster had had liveries of yellow and crimson.) They drove into\r\nthe country, and the Baroness, leaning far back and swaying her\r\nlace-fringed parasol, looked to right and to left and surveyed the\r\nway-side objects. After a while she pronounced them _affreux_. Her\r\nbrother remarked that it was apparently a country in which the\r\nforeground was inferior to the _plans recul\xc3\xa9s_; and the Baroness\r\nrejoined that the landscape seemed to be all foreground. Felix had\r\nfixed with his new friends the hour at which he should bring his\r\nsister; it was four o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the afternoon. The large, clean-faced\r\nhouse wore, to his eyes, as the barouche drove up to it, a very\r\nfriendly aspect; the high, slender elms made lengthening shadows in\r\nfront of it. The Baroness descended; her American kinsfolk were\r\nstationed in the portico. Felix waved his hat to them, and a tall, lean\r\ngentleman, with a high forehead and a clean shaven face, came forward\r\ntoward the garden gate. Charlotte Wentworth walked at his side.\r\nGertrude came behind, more slowly. Both of these young ladies wore\r\nrustling silk dresses. Felix ushered his sister into the gate. \xe2\x80\x9cBe very\r\ngracious,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to her. But he saw the admonition was superfluous.\r\nEugenia was prepared to be gracious as only Eugenia could be. Felix\r\nknew no keener pleasure than to be able to admire his sister\r\nunrestrictedly; for if the opportunity was frequent, it was not\r\ninveterate. When she desired to please she was to him, as to everyone\r\nelse, the most charming woman in the world. Then he forgot that she was\r\never anything else; that she was sometimes hard and perverse; that he\r\nwas occasionally afraid of her. Now, as she took his arm to pass into\r\nthe garden, he felt that she desired, that she proposed, to please, and\r\nthis situation made him very happy. Eugenia would please.\r\n\r\nThe tall gentleman came to meet her, looking very rigid and grave. But\r\nit was a rigidity that had no illiberal meaning. Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s manner\r\nwas pregnant, on the contrary, with a sense of grand responsibility, of\r\nthe solemnity of the occasion, of its being difficult to show\r\nsufficient deference to a lady at once so distinguished and so unhappy.\r\nFelix had observed on the day before his characteristic pallor; and now\r\nhe perceived that there was something almost cadaverous in his uncle\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhigh-featured white face. But so clever were this young man\xe2\x80\x99s quick\r\nsympathies and perceptions that he already learned that in these\r\nsemi-mortuary manifestations there was no cause for alarm. His light\r\nimagination had gained a glimpse of Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s spiritual\r\nmechanism, and taught him that, the old man being infinitely\r\nconscientious, the special operation of conscience within him announced\r\nitself by several of the indications of physical faintness.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness took her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s hand, and stood looking at him with her\r\nugly face and her beautiful smile. \xe2\x80\x9cHave I done right to come?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery right, very right,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, solemnly. He had arranged\r\nin his mind a little speech; but now it quite faded away. He felt\r\nalmost frightened. He had never been looked at in just that way\xe2\x80\x94with\r\njust that fixed, intense smile\xe2\x80\x94by any woman; and it perplexed and\r\nweighed upon him, now, that the woman who was smiling so and who had\r\ninstantly given him a vivid sense of her possessing other unprecedented\r\nattributes, was his own niece, the child of his own father\xe2\x80\x99s daughter.\r\nThe idea that his niece should be a German Baroness, married\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cmorganatically\xe2\x80\x9d to a Prince, had already given him much to think\r\nabout. Was it right, was it just, was it acceptable? He always slept\r\nbadly, and the night before he had lain awake much more even than\r\nusual, asking himself these questions. The strange word \xe2\x80\x9cmorganatic\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nwas constantly in his ears; it reminded him of a certain Mrs. Morgan\r\nwhom he had once known and who had been a bold, unpleasant woman. He\r\nhad a feeling that it was his duty, so long as the Baroness looked at\r\nhim, smiling in that way, to meet her glance with his own scrupulously\r\nadjusted, consciously frigid organs of vision; but on this occasion he\r\nfailed to perform his duty to the last. He looked away toward his\r\ndaughters. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are very glad to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d he had said. \xe2\x80\x9cAllow me to\r\nintroduce my daughters\xe2\x80\x94Miss Charlotte Wentworth, Miss Gertrude\r\nWentworth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness thought she had never seen people less demonstrative. But\r\nCharlotte kissed her and took her hand, looking at her sweetly and\r\nsolemnly. Gertrude seemed to her almost funereal, though Gertrude might\r\nhave found a source of gaiety in the fact that Felix, with his\r\nmagnificent smile, had been talking to her; he had greeted her as a\r\nvery old friend. When she kissed the Baroness she had tears in her\r\neyes. Madame M\xc3\xbcnster took each of these young women by the hand, and\r\nlooked at them all over. Charlotte thought her very strange-looking and\r\nsingularly dressed; she could not have said whether it was well or ill.\r\nShe was glad, at any rate, that they had put on their silk\r\ngowns\xe2\x80\x94especially Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cMy cousins are very pretty,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nBaroness, turning her eyes from one to the other. \xe2\x80\x9cYour daughters are\r\nvery handsome, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte blushed quickly; she had never yet heard her personal\r\nappearance alluded to in a loud, expressive voice. Gertrude looked\r\naway\xe2\x80\x94not at Felix; she was extremely pleased. It was not the compliment\r\nthat pleased her; she did not believe it; she thought herself very\r\nplain. She could hardly have told you the source of her satisfaction;\r\nit came from something in the way the Baroness spoke, and it was not\r\ndiminished\xe2\x80\x94it was rather deepened, oddly enough\xe2\x80\x94by the young girl\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ndisbelief. Mr. Wentworth was silent; and then he asked, formally,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWon\xe2\x80\x99t you come into the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese are not all; you have some other children,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a son,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why doesn\xe2\x80\x99t he come to meet me?\xe2\x80\x9d Eugenia cried. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid he is\r\nnot so charming as his sisters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know; I will see about it,\xe2\x80\x9d the old man declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is rather afraid of ladies,\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte said, softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, as loud as she could.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will go in and find him. We will draw him out of his _cachette_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nAnd the Baroness took Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s arm, who was not aware that he\r\nhad offered it to her, and who, as they walked toward the house,\r\nwondered whether he ought to have offered it and whether it was proper\r\nfor her to take it if it had not been offered. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to know you\r\nwell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, interrupting these meditations, \xe2\x80\x9cand I want\r\nyou to know me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems natural that we should know each other,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth\r\nrejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are near relatives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there comes a moment in life when one reverts, irresistibly, to\r\none\xe2\x80\x99s natural ties\xe2\x80\x94to one\xe2\x80\x99s natural affections.
