You must have found\r\nthat!\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth had been told the day before by Felix that Eugenia was\r\nvery clever, very brilliant, and the information had held him in some\r\nsuspense. This was the cleverness, he supposed; the brilliancy was\r\nbeginning. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, the natural affections are very strong,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn some people,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness declared. \xe2\x80\x9cNot in all.\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte was\r\nwalking beside her; she took hold of her hand again, smiling always.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you, _cousine_, where did you get that enchanting complexion?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nwent on; \xe2\x80\x9csuch lilies and roses?\xe2\x80\x9d The roses in poor Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncountenance began speedily to predominate over the lilies, and she\r\nquickened her step and reached the portico. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is the country of\r\ncomplexions,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness continued, addressing herself to Mr.\r\nWentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cI am convinced they are more delicate. There are very good\r\nones in England\xe2\x80\x94in Holland; but they are very apt to be coarse. There\r\nis too much red.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you will find,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, \xe2\x80\x9cthat this country is\r\nsuperior in many respects to those you mention. I have been to England\r\nand Holland.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have been to Europe?\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come\r\nand see me? But it\xe2\x80\x99s better, after all, this way,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. They were\r\nentering the house; she paused and looked round her. \xe2\x80\x9cI see you have\r\narranged your house\xe2\x80\x94your beautiful house\xe2\x80\x94in the\xe2\x80\x94in the Dutch taste!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe house is very old,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Mr. Wentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cGeneral Washington\r\nonce spent a week here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I have heard of Washington,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cMy father used\r\nto tell me of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth was silent a moment, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cI found he was very well\r\nknown in Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nFelix had lingered in the garden with Gertrude; he was standing before\r\nher and smiling, as he had done the day before. What had happened the\r\nday before seemed to her a kind of dream. He had been there and he had\r\nchanged everything; the others had seen him, they had talked with him;\r\nbut that he should come again, that he should be part of the future,\r\npart of her small, familiar, much-meditating life\xe2\x80\x94this needed, afresh,\r\nthe evidence of her senses. The evidence had come to her senses now;\r\nand her senses seemed to rejoice in it. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you think of Eugenia?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nFelix asked. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t she charming?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very brilliant,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I can\xe2\x80\x99t tell yet. She\r\nseems to me like a singer singing an air. You can\xe2\x80\x99t tell till the song\r\nis done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, the song will never be done!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the young man, laughing.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think her handsome?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude had been disappointed in the beauty of the Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster;\r\nshe had expected her, for mysterious reasons, to resemble a very pretty\r\nportrait of the Empress Josephine, of which there hung an engraving in\r\none of the parlors, and which the younger Miss Wentworth had always\r\ngreatly admired. But the Baroness was not at all like that\xe2\x80\x94not at all.\r\nThough different, however, she was very wonderful, and Gertrude felt\r\nherself most suggestively corrected. It was strange, nevertheless, that\r\nFelix should speak in that positive way about his sister\xe2\x80\x99s beauty. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink I _shall_ think her handsome,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt must be very\r\ninteresting to know her. I don\xe2\x80\x99t feel as if I ever could.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you will know her well; you will become great friends,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix\r\ndeclared, as if this were the easiest thing in the world.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is very graceful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, looking after the Baroness,\r\nsuspended to her father\xe2\x80\x99s arm. It was a pleasure to her to say that\r\nanyone was graceful.\r\n\r\nFelix had been looking about him. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd your little cousin, of\r\nyesterday,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cwho was so wonderfully pretty\xe2\x80\x94what has become of\r\nher?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is in the parlor,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude answered. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, she is very pretty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nShe felt as if it were her duty to take him straight into the house, to\r\nwhere he might be near her cousin. But after hesitating a moment she\r\nlingered still. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t believe you would come back,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot come back!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cYou didn\xe2\x80\x99t know, then, the\r\nimpression made upon this susceptible heart of mine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe wondered whether he meant the impression her cousin Lizzie had\r\nmade. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t think we should ever see you again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd pray what did you think would become of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know. I thought you would melt away.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a compliment to my solidity! I melt very often,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut there is always something left of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came and waited for you by the door, because the others did,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGertrude went on. \xe2\x80\x9cBut if you had never appeared I should not have been\r\nsurprised.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope,\xe2\x80\x9d declared Felix, looking at her, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would have been\r\ndisappointed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him a little, and shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cAh, par exemple!\xe2\x80\x9d_ cried the young man. \xe2\x80\x9cYou deserve that I should\r\nnever leave you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGoing into the parlor they found Mr. Wentworth performing\r\nintroductions. A young man was standing before the Baroness, blushing a\r\ngood deal, laughing a little, and shifting his weight from one foot to\r\nthe other\xe2\x80\x94a slim, mild-faced young man, with neatly-arranged features,\r\nlike those of Mr. Wentworth. Two other gentlemen, behind him, had risen\r\nfrom their seats, and a little apart, near one of the windows, stood a\r\nremarkably pretty young girl. The young girl was knitting a stocking;\r\nbut, while her fingers quickly moved, she looked with wide, brilliant\r\neyes at the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd what is your son\xe2\x80\x99s name?\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia, smiling at the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy name is Clifford Wentworth, ma\xe2\x80\x99am,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in a tremulous voice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy didn\xe2\x80\x99t you come out to meet me, Mr. Clifford Wentworth?\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nBaroness demanded, with her beautiful smile.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t think you would want me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the young man, slowly sidling\r\nabout.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne always wants a _beau cousin_,\xe2\x80\x94if one has one! But if you are very\r\nnice to me in future I won\xe2\x80\x99t remember it against you.\xe2\x80\x9d And Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster transferred her smile to the other persons present. It rested\r\nfirst upon the candid countenance and long-skirted figure of Mr. Brand,\r\nwhose eyes were intently fixed upon Mr. Wentworth, as if to beg him not\r\nto prolong an anomalous situation. Mr. Wentworth pronounced his name.\r\nEugenia gave him a very charming glance, and then looked at the other\r\ngentleman.\r\n\r\nThis latter personage was a man of rather less than the usual stature\r\nand the usual weight, with a quick, observant, agreeable dark eye, a\r\nsmall quantity of thin dark hair, and a small moustache. He had been\r\nstanding with his hands in his pockets; and when Eugenia looked at him\r\nhe took them out. But he did not, like Mr. Brand, look evasively and\r\nurgently at their host. He met Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s eyes; he appeared to\r\nappreciate the privilege of meeting them. Madame M\xc3\xbcnster instantly felt\r\nthat he was, intrinsically, the most important person present. She was\r\nnot unconscious that this impression was in some degree manifested in\r\nthe little sympathetic nod with which she acknowledged Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nannouncement, \xe2\x80\x9cMy cousin, Mr. Acton!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour cousin\xe2\x80\x94not mine?\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt only depends upon you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Acton declared, laughing.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness looked at him a moment, and noticed that he had very white\r\nteeth. \xe2\x80\x9cLet it depend upon your behavior,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI think I had\r\nbetter wait. I have cousins enough. Unless I can also claim\r\nrelationship,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9cwith that charming young lady,\xe2\x80\x9d and she\r\npointed to the young girl at the window.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s my sister,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Acton. And Gertrude Wentworth put her arm\r\nround the young girl and led her forward. It was not, apparently, that\r\nshe needed much leading. She came toward the Baroness with a light,\r\nquick step, and with perfect self-possession, rolling her stocking\r\nround its needles. She had dark blue eyes and dark brown hair; she was\r\nwonderfully pretty.\r\n\r\nEugenia kissed her, as she had kissed the other young women, and then\r\nheld her off a little, looking at her. \xe2\x80\x9cNow this is quite another\r\n_type_,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; she pronounced the word in the French manner. \xe2\x80\x9cThis\r\nis a different outline, my uncle, a different character, from that of\r\nyour own daughters. This, Felix,\xe2\x80\x9d she went on, \xe2\x80\x9cis very much more what\r\nwe have always thought of as the American type.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young girl, during this exposition, was smiling askance at everyone\r\nin turn, and at Felix out of turn. \xe2\x80\x9cI find only one type here!\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nFelix, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cThe type adorable!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis sally was received in perfect silence, but Felix, who learned all\r\nthings quickly, had already learned that the silences frequently\r\nobserved among his new acquaintances were not necessarily restrictive\r\nor resentful. It was, as one might say, the silence of expectation, of\r\nmodesty. They were all standing round his sister, as if they were\r\nexpecting her to acquit herself of the exhibition of some peculiar\r\nfaculty, some brilliant talent. Their attitude seemed to imply that she\r\nwas a kind of conversational mountebank, attired, intellectually, in\r\ngauze and spangles. This attitude gave a certain ironical force to\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s next words. \xe2\x80\x9cNow this is your circle,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to her\r\nuncle. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is your _salon_. These are your regular _habitu\xc3\xa9s_, eh? I\r\nam so glad to see you all together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, \xe2\x80\x9cthey are always dropping in and out. You\r\nmust do the same.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather,\xe2\x80\x9d interposed Charlotte Wentworth, \xe2\x80\x9cthey must do something\r\nmore.\xe2\x80\x9d And she turned her sweet, serious face, that seemed at once\r\ntimid and placid, upon their interesting visitor. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is your name?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEugenia-Camilla-Dolores,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you needn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsay all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will say Eugenia, if you will let me. You must come and stay with\r\nus.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness laid her hand upon Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s arm very tenderly; but she\r\nreserved herself. She was wondering whether it would be possible to\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cstay\xe2\x80\x9d with these people. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would be very charming\xe2\x80\x94very charming,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe said; and her eyes wandered over the company, over the room. She\r\nwished to gain time before committing herself. Her glance fell upon\r\nyoung Mr. Brand, who stood there, with his arms folded and his hand on\r\nhis chin, looking at her. \xe2\x80\x9cThe gentleman, I suppose, is a sort of\r\necclesiastic,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Mr. Wentworth, lowering her voice a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is a minister,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Protestant?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Eugenia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a Unitarian, madam,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Mr. Brand, impressively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, I see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cSomething new.\xe2\x80\x9d She had never heard of this\r\nform of worship.\r\n\r\nMr. Acton began to laugh, and Gertrude looked anxiously at Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have come very far,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery far\xe2\x80\x94very far,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness replied, with a graceful shake of her\r\nhead\xe2\x80\x94a shake that might have meant many different things.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a reason why you ought to settle down with us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nWentworth, with that dryness of utterance which, as Eugenia was too\r\nintelligent not to feel, took nothing from the delicacy of his meaning.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him, and for an instant, in his cold, still face, she\r\nseemed to see a far-away likeness to the vaguely remembered image of\r\nher mother. Eugenia was a woman of sudden emotions, and now,\r\nunexpectedly, she felt one rising in her heart. She kept looking round\r\nthe circle; she knew that there was admiration in all the eyes that\r\nwere fixed upon her. She smiled at them all.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came to look\xe2\x80\x94to try\xe2\x80\x94to ask,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me I have done\r\nwell. I am very tired; I want to rest.\xe2\x80\x9d There were tears in her eyes.\r\nThe luminous interior, the gentle, tranquil people, the simple, serious\r\nlife\xe2\x80\x94the sense of these things pressed upon her with an overmastering\r\nforce, and she felt herself yielding to one of the most genuine\r\nemotions she had ever known. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to stay here,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray take me in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThough she was smiling, there were tears in her voice as well as in her\r\neyes. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear niece,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, softly. And Charlotte put\r\nout her arms and drew the Baroness toward her; while Robert Acton\r\nturned away, with his hands stealing into his pockets.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV\r\n\r\n\r\nA few days after the Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster had presented herself to her\r\nAmerican kinsfolk she came, with her brother, and took up her abode in\r\nthat small white house adjacent to Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s own dwelling of\r\nwhich mention has already been made. It was on going with his daughters\r\nto return her visit that Mr. Wentworth placed this comfortable cottage\r\nat her service; the offer being the result of a domestic colloquy,\r\ndiffused through the ensuing twenty-four hours, in the course of which\r\nthe two foreign visitors were discussed and analyzed with a great deal\r\nof earnestness and subtlety. The discussion went forward, as I say, in\r\nthe family circle; but that circle on the evening following Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s return to town, as on many other occasions, included Robert\r\nActon and his pretty sister. If you had been present, it would probably\r\nnot have seemed to you that the advent of these brilliant strangers was\r\ntreated as an exhilarating occurrence, a pleasure the more in this\r\ntranquil household, a prospective source of entertainment. This was not\r\nMr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s way of treating any human occurrence. The sudden\r\nirruption into the well-ordered consciousness of the Wentworths of an\r\nelement not allowed for in its scheme of usual obligations required a\r\nreadjustment of that sense of responsibility which constituted its\r\nprincipal furniture. To consider an event, crudely and baldly, in the\r\nlight of the pleasure it might bring them was an intellectual exercise\r\nwith which Felix Young\xe2\x80\x99s American cousins were almost wholly\r\nunacquainted, and which they scarcely supposed to be largely pursued in\r\nany section of human society. The arrival of Felix and his sister was a\r\nsatisfaction, but it was a singularly joyless and inelastic\r\nsatisfaction. It was an extension of duty, of the exercise of the more\r\nrecondite virtues; but neither Mr. Wentworth, nor Charlotte, nor Mr.\r\nBrand, who, among these excellent people, was a great promoter of\r\nreflection and aspiration, frankly adverted to it as an extension of\r\nenjoyment. This function was ultimately assumed by Gertrude Wentworth,\r\nwho was a peculiar girl, but the full compass of whose peculiarities\r\nhad not been exhibited before they very ingeniously found their pretext\r\nin the presence of these possibly too agreeable foreigners. Gertrude,\r\nhowever, had to struggle with a great accumulation of obstructions,\r\nboth of the subjective, as the metaphysicians say, and of the\r\nobjective, order; and indeed it is no small part of the purpose of this\r\nlittle history to set forth her struggle. What seemed paramount in this\r\nabrupt enlargement of Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s sympathies and those of his\r\ndaughters was an extension of the field of possible mistakes; and the\r\ndoctrine, as it may almost be called, of the oppressive gravity of\r\nmistakes was one of the most cherished traditions of the Wentworth\r\nfamily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe she wants to come and stay in this house,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGertrude; Madame M\xc3\xbcnster, from this time forward, receiving no other\r\ndesignation than the personal pronoun. Charlotte and Gertrude acquired\r\nconsiderable facility in addressing her, directly, as \xe2\x80\x9cEugenia;\xe2\x80\x9d but in\r\nspeaking of her to each other they rarely called her anything but\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cshe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoesn\xe2\x80\x99t she think it good enough for her?\xe2\x80\x9d cried little Lizzie Acton,\r\nwho was always asking unpractical questions that required, in\r\nstrictness, no answer, and to which indeed she expected no other answer\r\nthan such as she herself invariably furnished in a small,\r\ninnocently-satirical laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe certainly expressed a willingness to come,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was only politeness,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, she is very polite\xe2\x80\x94very polite,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is too polite,\xe2\x80\x9d his son declared, in a softly growling tone which\r\nwas habitual to him, but which was an indication of nothing worse than\r\na vaguely humorous intention. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is very embarrassing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is more than can be said of you, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lizzie Acton, with\r\nher little laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean to encourage her,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m sure I don\xe2\x80\x99t care if you do!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lizzie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will not think of you, Clifford,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope not!\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will think of Robert,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude continued, in the same tone.\r\n\r\nRobert Acton began to blush; but there was no occasion for it, for\r\neveryone was looking at Gertrude\xe2\x80\x94everyone, at least, save Lizzie, who,\r\nwith her pretty head on one side, contemplated her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you attribute motives, Gertrude?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t attribute motives, father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cI only say she\r\nwill think of Robert; and she will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude judges by herself!\xe2\x80\x9d Acton exclaimed, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you,\r\nGertrude? Of course the Baroness will think of me. She will think of me\r\nfrom morning till night.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will be very comfortable here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte, with something of\r\na housewife\xe2\x80\x99s pride. \xe2\x80\x9cShe can have the large northeast room. And the\r\nFrench bedstead,\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte added, with a constant sense of the lady\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nforeignness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will not like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude; \xe2\x80\x9cnot even if you pin little\r\ntidies all over the chairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy not, dear?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Charlotte, perceiving a touch of irony here, but\r\nnot resenting it.\r\n\r\nGertrude had left her chair; she was walking about the room; her stiff\r\nsilk dress, which she had put on in honor of the Baroness, made a sound\r\nupon the carpet. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d she replied. \xe2\x80\x9cShe will want something\r\nmore\xe2\x80\x94more private.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she wants to be private she can stay in her room,\xe2\x80\x9d Lizzie Acton\r\nremarked.\r\n\r\nGertrude paused in her walk, looking at her. \xe2\x80\x9cThat would not be\r\npleasant,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cShe wants privacy and pleasure together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRobert Acton began to laugh again. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear cousin, what a picture!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte had fixed her serious eyes upon her sister; she wondered\r\nwhence she had suddenly derived these strange notions. Mr. Wentworth\r\nalso observed his younger daughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know what her manner of life may have been,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut she\r\ncertainly never can have enjoyed a more refined and salubrious home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude stood there looking at them all. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is the wife of a\r\nPrince,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are all princes here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth; \xe2\x80\x9cand I don\xe2\x80\x99t know of any\r\npalace in this neighborhood that is to let.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCousin William,\xe2\x80\x9d Robert Acton interposed, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you want to do something\r\nhandsome? Make them a present, for three months, of the little house\r\nover the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very generous with other people\xe2\x80\x99s things!\xe2\x80\x9d cried his sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRobert is very generous with his own things,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth observed\r\ndispassionately, and looking, in cold meditation, at his kinsman.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d Lizzie went on, \xe2\x80\x9cI had an idea you were so fond of your new\r\ncousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhich new cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean the Baroness!\xe2\x80\x9d the young girl rejoined, with her laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thought you expected to see so much of him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf Felix? I hope to see a great deal of him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, simply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why do you want to keep him out of the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at Lizzie Acton, and then looked away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you want me to live in the house with you, Lizzie?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nClifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope you never will. I hate you!\xe2\x80\x9d Such was this young lady\xe2\x80\x99s reply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFather,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, stopping before Mr. Wentworth and smiling,\r\nwith a smile the sweeter, as her smile always was, for its rarity; \xe2\x80\x9cdo\r\nlet them live in the little house over the way. It will be lovely!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nRobert Acton had been watching her. \xe2\x80\x9cGertrude is right,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude is the cleverest girl in the world. If I might take the\r\nliberty, I should strongly recommend their living there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is nothing there so pretty as the northeast room,\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte\r\nurged.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will make it pretty. Leave her alone!\xe2\x80\x9d Acton exclaimed.\r\n\r\nGertrude, at his compliment, had blushed and looked at him: it was as\r\nif someone less familiar had complimented her. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure she will make\r\nit pretty. It will be very interesting. It will be a place to go to. It\r\nwill be a foreign house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre we very sure that we need a foreign house?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth\r\ninquired. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you think it desirable to establish a foreign house\xe2\x80\x94in\r\nthis quiet place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou speak,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cas if it were a question of the\r\npoor Baroness opening a wine-shop or a gaming-table.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be too lovely!\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude declared again, laying her hand on\r\nthe back of her father\xe2\x80\x99s chair.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat she should open a gaming-table?\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte asked, with great\r\ngravity.\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at her a moment, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, Charlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d she said,\r\nsimply.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude is growing pert,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford Wentworth observed, with his\r\nhumorous young growl. \xe2\x80\x9cThat comes of associating with foreigners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth looked up at his daughter, who was standing beside him;\r\nhe drew her gently forward. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must be careful,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must\r\nkeep watch. Indeed, we must all be careful. This is a great change; we\r\nare to be exposed to peculiar influences. I don\xe2\x80\x99t say they are bad. I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t judge them in advance. But they may perhaps make it necessary\r\nthat we should exercise a great deal of wisdom and self-control. It\r\nwill be a different tone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude was silent a moment, in deference to her father\xe2\x80\x99s speech; then\r\nshe spoke in a manner that was not in the least an answer to it. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwant to see how they will live. I am sure they will have different\r\nhours. She will do all kinds of little things differently. When we go\r\nover there it will be like going to Europe. She will have a boudoir.\r\nShe will invite us to dinner\xe2\x80\x94very late. She will breakfast in her\r\nroom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte gazed at her sister again. Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s imagination seemed to\r\nher to be fairly running riot. She had always known that Gertrude had a\r\ngreat deal of imagination\xe2\x80\x94she had been very proud of it. But at the\r\nsame time she had always felt that it was a dangerous and irresponsible\r\nfaculty; and now, to her sense, for the moment, it seemed to threaten\r\nto make her sister a strange person who should come in suddenly, as\r\nfrom a journey, talking of the peculiar and possibly unpleasant things\r\nshe had observed. Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s imagination took no journeys whatever;\r\nshe kept it, as it were, in her pocket, with the other furniture of\r\nthis receptacle\xe2\x80\x94a thimble, a little box of peppermint, and a morsel of\r\ncourt-plaster. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe she would have any dinner\xe2\x80\x94or any\r\nbreakfast,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miss Wentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t believe she knows how to do\r\nanything herself. I should have to get her ever so many servants, and\r\nshe wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has a maid,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude; \xe2\x80\x9ca French maid. She mentioned her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder if the maid has a little fluted cap and red slippers,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nLizzie Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cThere was a French maid in that play that Robert took me\r\nto see. She had pink stockings; she was very wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was a _soubrette_,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude announced, who had never seen a play\r\nin her life. \xe2\x80\x9cThey call that a soubrette. It will be a great chance to\r\nlearn French.\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte gave a little soft, helpless groan. She had a\r\nvision of a wicked, theatrical person, clad in pink stockings and red\r\nshoes, and speaking, with confounding volubility, an incomprehensible\r\ntongue, flitting through the sacred penetralia of that large, clean\r\nhouse. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is one reason in favor of their coming here,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we can make Eugenia speak French to us, and Felix. I mean\r\nto begin\xe2\x80\x94the next time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth had kept her standing near him, and he gave her his\r\nearnest, thin, unresponsive glance again. \xe2\x80\x9cI want you to make me a\r\npromise, Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot to get excited. Not to allow these\xe2\x80\x94these occurrences to be an\r\noccasion for excitement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked down at him a moment, and then she shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I can promise that, father. I am excited already.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth was silent a while; they all were silent, as if in\r\nrecognition of something audacious and portentous.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think they had better go to the other house,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte,\r\nquietly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall keep them in the other house,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth subjoined, more\r\npregnantly.\r\n\r\nGertrude turned away; then she looked across at Robert Acton. Her\r\ncousin Robert was a great friend of hers; she often looked at him this\r\nway instead of saying things. Her glance on this occasion, however,\r\nstruck him as a substitute for a larger volume of diffident utterance\r\nthan usual, inviting him to observe, among other things, the\r\ninefficiency of her father\xe2\x80\x99s design\xe2\x80\x94if design it was\xe2\x80\x94for diminishing,\r\nin the interest of quiet nerves, their occasions of contact with their\r\nforeign relatives. But Acton immediately complimented Mr. Wentworth\r\nupon his liberality. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a very nice thing to do,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cgiving\r\nthem the little house. You will have treated them handsomely, and,\r\nwhatever happens, you will be glad of it.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth was liberal,\r\nand he knew he was liberal. It gave him pleasure to know it, to feel\r\nit, to see it recorded; and this pleasure is the only palpable form of\r\nself-indulgence with which the narrator of these incidents will be able\r\nto charge him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA three days\xe2\x80\x99 visit at most, over there, is all I should have found\r\npossible,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame M\xc3\xbcnster remarked to her brother, after they had taken\r\npossession of the little white house. \xe2\x80\x9cIt would have been too\r\n_intime_\xe2\x80\x94decidedly too _intime_. Breakfast, dinner, and tea _en\r\nfamille_\xe2\x80\x94it would have been the end of the world if I could have\r\nreached the third day.\xe2\x80\x9d And she made the same observation to her maid\r\nAugustine, an intelligent person, who enjoyed a liberal share of her\r\nconfidence. Felix declared that he would willingly spend his life in\r\nthe bosom of the Wentworth family; that they were the kindest,\r\nsimplest, most amiable people in the world, and that he had taken a\r\nprodigious fancy to them all. The Baroness quite agreed with him that\r\nthey were simple and kind; they were thoroughly nice people, and she\r\nliked them extremely. The girls were perfect ladies; it was impossible\r\nto be more of a lady than Charlotte Wentworth, in spite of her little\r\nvillage air. \xe2\x80\x9cBut as for thinking them the best company in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid the Baroness, \xe2\x80\x9cthat is another thing; and as for wishing to live\r\n_porte \xc3\xa0 porte_ with them, I should as soon think of wishing myself\r\nback in the convent again, to wear a bombazine apron and sleep in a\r\ndormitory.\xe2\x80\x9d And yet the Baroness was in high good humor; she had been\r\nvery much pleased. With her lively perception and her refined\r\nimagination, she was capable of enjoying anything that was\r\ncharacteristic, anything that was good of its kind. The Wentworth\r\nhousehold seemed to her very perfect in its kind\xe2\x80\x94wonderfully peaceful\r\nand unspotted; pervaded by a sort of dove-colored freshness that had\r\nall the quietude and benevolence of what she deemed to be Quakerism,\r\nand yet seemed to be founded upon a degree of material abundance for\r\nwhich, in certain matters of detail, one might have looked in vain at\r\nthe frugal little court of Silberstadt-Schreckenstein. She perceived\r\nimmediately that her American relatives thought and talked very little\r\nabout money; and this of itself made an impression upon Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nimagination. She perceived at the same time that if Charlotte or\r\nGertrude should ask their father for a very considerable sum he would\r\nat once place it in their hands; and this made a still greater\r\nimpression. The greatest impression of all, perhaps, was made by\r\nanother rapid induction. The Baroness had an immediate conviction that\r\nRobert Acton would put his hand into his pocket every day in the week\r\nif that rattle-pated little sister of his should bid him. The men in\r\nthis country, said the Baroness, are evidently very obliging. Her\r\ndeclaration that she was looking for rest and retirement had been by no\r\nmeans wholly untrue; nothing that the Baroness said was wholly untrue.\r\nIt is but fair to add, perhaps, that nothing that she said was wholly\r\ntrue. She wrote to a friend in Germany that it was a return to nature;\r\nit was like drinking new milk, and she was very fond of new milk. She\r\nsaid to herself, of course, that it would be a little dull; but there\r\ncan be no better proof of her good spirits than the fact that she\r\nthought she should not mind its being a little dull. It seemed to her,\r\nwhen from the piazza of her eleemosynary cottage she looked out over\r\nthe soundless fields, the stony pastures, the clear-faced ponds, the\r\nrugged little orchards, that she had never been in the midst of so\r\npeculiarly intense a stillness; it was almost a delicate sensual\r\npleasure. It was all very good, very innocent and safe, and out of it\r\nsomething good must come. Augustine, indeed, who had an unbounded faith\r\nin her mistress\xe2\x80\x99s wisdom and far-sightedness, was a great deal\r\nperplexed and depressed. She was always ready to take her cue when she\r\nunderstood it; but she liked to understand it, and on this occasion\r\ncomprehension failed. What, indeed, was the Baroness doing _dans cette\r\ngal\xc3\xa8re_? what fish did she expect to land out of these very stagnant\r\nwaters? The game was evidently a deep one. Augustine could trust her;\r\nbut the sense of walking in the dark betrayed itself in the physiognomy\r\nof this spare, sober, sallow, middle-aged person, who had nothing in\r\ncommon with Gertrude Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s conception of a soubrette, by the most\r\nironical scowl that had ever rested upon the unpretending tokens of the\r\npeace and plenty of the Wentworths. Fortunately, Augustine could quench\r\nskepticism in action. She quite agreed with her mistress\xe2\x80\x94or rather she\r\nquite out-stripped her mistress\xe2\x80\x94in thinking that the little white house\r\nwas pitifully bare. _\xe2\x80\x9cIl faudra,\xe2\x80\x9d_ said Augustine, _\xe2\x80\x9clui faire un peu\r\nde toilette.\xe2\x80\x9d_ And she began to hang up _porti\xc3\xa8res_ in the doorways; to\r\nplace wax candles, procured after some research, in unexpected\r\nsituations; to dispose anomalous draperies over the arms of sofas and\r\nthe backs of chairs. The Baroness had brought with her to the New World\r\na copious provision of the element of costume; and the two Miss\r\nWentworths, when they came over to see her, were somewhat bewildered by\r\nthe obtrusive distribution of her wardrobe. There were India shawls\r\nsuspended, curtain-wise, in the parlor door, and curious fabrics,\r\ncorresponding to Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s metaphysical vision of an opera-cloak,\r\ntumbled about in the sitting-places. There were pink silk blinds in the\r\nwindows, by which the room was strangely bedimmed; and along the\r\nchimney-piece was disposed a remarkable band of velvet, covered with\r\ncoarse, dirty-looking lace. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been making myself a little\r\ncomfortable,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, much to the confusion of Charlotte,\r\nwho had been on the point of proposing to come and help her put her\r\nsuperfluous draperies away. But what Charlotte mistook for an almost\r\nculpably delayed subsidence Gertrude very presently perceived to be the\r\nmost ingenious, the most interesting, the most romantic intention.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is life, indeed, without curtains?\xe2\x80\x9d she secretly asked herself;\r\nand she appeared to herself to have been leading hitherto an existence\r\nsingularly garish and totally devoid of festoons.\r\n\r\nFelix was not a young man who troubled himself greatly about\r\nanything\xe2\x80\x94least of all about the conditions of enjoyment. His faculty of\r\nenjoyment was so large, so unconsciously eager, that it may be said of\r\nit that it had a permanent advance upon embarrassment and sorrow. His\r\nsentient faculty was intrinsically joyous, and novelty and change were\r\nin themselves a delight to him. As they had come to him with a great\r\ndeal of frequency, his life had been more agreeable than appeared.\r\nNever was a nature more perfectly fortunate. It was not a restless,\r\napprehensive, ambitious spirit, running a race with the tyranny of\r\nfate, but a temper so unsuspicious as to put Adversity off her guard,\r\ndodging and evading her with the easy, natural motion of a wind-shifted\r\nflower. Felix extracted entertainment from all things, and all his\r\nfaculties\xe2\x80\x94his imagination, his intelligence, his affections, his\r\nsenses\xe2\x80\x94had a hand in the game. It seemed to him that Eugenia and he had\r\nbeen very well treated; there was something absolutely touching in that\r\ncombination of paternal liberality and social considerateness which\r\nmarked Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s deportment. It was most uncommonly kind of him,\r\nfor instance, to have given them a house. Felix was positively amused\r\nat having a house of his own; for the little white cottage among the\r\napple trees\xe2\x80\x94the chalet, as Madame M\xc3\xbcnster always called it\xe2\x80\x94was much\r\nmore sensibly his own than any domiciliary _quatri\xc3\xa8me_, looking upon a\r\ncourt, with the rent overdue. Felix had spent a good deal of his life\r\nin looking into courts, with a perhaps slightly tattered pair of elbows\r\nresting upon the ledge of a high-perched window, and the thin smoke of\r\na cigarette rising into an atmosphere in which street-cries died away\r\nand the vibration of chimes from ancient belfries became sensible. He\r\nhad never known anything so infinitely rural as these New England\r\nfields; and he took a great fancy to all their pastoral roughnesses. He\r\nhad never had a greater sense of luxurious security; and at the risk of\r\nmaking him seem a rather sordid adventurer I must declare that he found\r\nan irresistible charm in the fact that he might dine every day at his\r\nuncle\xe2\x80\x99s. The charm was irresistible, however, because his fancy flung a\r\nrosy light over this homely privilege. He appreciated highly the fare\r\nthat was set before him. There was a kind of fresh-looking abundance\r\nabout it which made him think that people must have lived so in the\r\nmythological era, when they spread their tables upon the grass,\r\nreplenished them from cornucopias, and had no particular need of\r\nkitchen stoves. But the great thing that Felix enjoyed was having found\r\na family\xe2\x80\x94sitting in the midst of gentle, generous people whom he might\r\ncall by their first names. He had never known anything more charming\r\nthan the attention they paid to what he said. It was like a large sheet\r\nof clean, fine-grained drawing-paper, all ready to be washed over with\r\neffective splashes of water-color. He had never had any cousins, and he\r\nhad never before found himself in contact so unrestricted with young\r\nunmarried ladies. He was extremely fond of the society of ladies, and\r\nit was new to him that it might be enjoyed in just this manner. At\r\nfirst he hardly knew what to make of his state of mind. It seemed to\r\nhim that he was in love, indiscriminately, with three girls at once. He\r\nsaw that Lizzie Acton was more brilliantly pretty than Charlotte and\r\nGertrude; but this was scarcely a superiority. His pleasure came from\r\nsomething they had in common\xe2\x80\x94a part of which was, indeed, that physical\r\ndelicacy which seemed to make it proper that they should always dress\r\nin thin materials and clear colors. But they were delicate in other\r\nways, and it was most agreeable to him to feel that these latter\r\ndelicacies were appreciable by contact, as it were. He had known,\r\nfortunately, many virtuous gentlewomen, but it now appeared to him that\r\nin his relations with them (especially when they were unmarried) he had\r\nbeen looking at pictures under glass. He perceived at present what a\r\nnuisance the glass had been\xe2\x80\x94how it perverted and interfered, how it\r\ncaught the reflection of other objects and kept you walking from side\r\nto side. He had no need to ask himself whether Charlotte and Gertrude,\r\nand Lizzie Acton, were in the right light; they were always in the\r\nright light. He liked everything about them: he was, for instance, not\r\nat all above liking the fact that they had very slender feet and high\r\ninsteps. He liked their pretty noses; he liked their surprised eyes and\r\ntheir hesitating, not at all positive way of speaking; he liked so much\r\nknowing that he was perfectly at liberty to be alone for hours,\r\nanywhere, with either of them; that preference for one to the other, as\r\na companion of solitude, remained a minor affair. Charlotte Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsweetly severe features were as agreeable as Lizzie Acton\xe2\x80\x99s wonderfully\r\nexpressive blue eyes; and Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s air of being always ready to walk\r\nabout and listen was as charming as anything else, especially as she\r\nwalked very gracefully. After a while Felix began to distinguish; but\r\neven then he would often wish, suddenly, that they were not all so sad.\r\nEven Lizzie Acton, in spite of her fine little chatter and laughter,\r\nappeared sad. Even Clifford Wentworth, who had extreme youth in his\r\nfavor, and kept a buggy with enormous wheels and a little sorrel mare\r\nwith the prettiest legs in the world\xe2\x80\x94even this fortunate lad was apt to\r\nhave an averted, uncomfortable glance, and to edge away from you at\r\ntimes, in the manner of a person with a bad conscience. The only person\r\nin the circle with no sense of oppression of any kind was, to Felix\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nperception, Robert Acton.\r\n\r\nIt might perhaps have been feared that after the completion of those\r\ngraceful domiciliary embellishments which have been mentioned Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster would have found herself confronted with alarming possibilities\r\nof _ennui_. But as yet she had not taken the alarm. The Baroness was a\r\nrestless soul, and she projected her restlessness, as it may be said,\r\ninto any situation that lay before her. Up to a certain point her\r\nrestlessness might be counted upon to entertain her. She was always\r\nexpecting something to happen, and, until it was disappointed,\r\nexpectancy itself was a delicate pleasure. What the Baroness expected\r\njust now it would take some ingenuity to set forth; it is enough that\r\nwhile she looked about her she found something to occupy her\r\nimagination. She assured herself that she was enchanted with her new\r\nrelatives; she professed to herself that, like her brother, she felt it\r\na sacred satisfaction to have found a family. It is certain that she\r\nenjoyed to the utmost the gentleness of her kinsfolk\xe2\x80\x99s deference. She\r\nhad, first and last, received a great deal of admiration, and her\r\nexperience of well-turned compliments was very considerable; but she\r\nknew that she had never been so real a power, never counted for so\r\nmuch, as now when, for the first time, the standard of comparison of\r\nher little circle was a prey to vagueness. The sense, indeed, that the\r\ngood people about her had, as regards her remarkable self, no standard\r\nof comparison at all gave her a feeling of almost illimitable power. It\r\nwas true, as she said to herself, that if for this reason they would be\r\nable to discover nothing against her, so they would perhaps neglect to\r\nperceive some of her superior points; but she always wound up her\r\nreflections by declaring that she would take care of that.\r\n\r\nCharlotte and Gertrude were in some perplexity between their desire to\r\nshow all proper attention to Madame M\xc3\xbcnster and their fear of being\r\nimportunate. The little house in the orchard had hitherto been occupied\r\nduring the summer months by intimate friends of the family, or by poor\r\nrelations who found in Mr. Wentworth a landlord attentive to repairs\r\nand oblivious of quarter-day. Under these circumstances the open door\r\nof the small house and that of the large one, facing each other across\r\ntheir homely gardens, levied no tax upon hourly visits. But the Misses\r\nWentworth received an impression that Eugenia was no friend to the\r\nprimitive custom of \xe2\x80\x9cdropping in;\xe2\x80\x9d she evidently had no idea of living\r\nwithout a door-keeper. \xe2\x80\x9cOne goes into your house as into an inn\xe2\x80\x94except\r\nthat there are no servants rushing forward,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Charlotte. And\r\nshe added that that was very charming. Gertrude explained to her sister\r\nthat she meant just the reverse; she didn\xe2\x80\x99t like it at all. Charlotte\r\ninquired why she should tell an untruth, and Gertrude answered that\r\nthere was probably some very good reason for it which they should\r\ndiscover when they knew her better. \xe2\x80\x9cThere can surely be no good reason\r\nfor telling an untruth,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope she does not think\r\nso.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had of course desired, from the first, to do everything in the way\r\nof helping her to arrange herself. It had seemed to Charlotte that\r\nthere would be a great many things to talk about; but the Baroness was\r\napparently inclined to talk about nothing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWrite her a note, asking her leave to come and see her. I think that\r\nis what she will like,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I give her the trouble of answering me?\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte asked.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe will have to write a note and send it over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think she will take any trouble,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, profoundly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat then will she do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what I am curious to see,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, leaving her sister\r\nwith an impression that her curiosity was morbid.\r\n\r\nThey went to see the Baroness without preliminary correspondence; and\r\nin the little salon which she had already created, with its becoming\r\nlight and its festoons, they found Robert Acton.\r\n\r\nEugenia was intensely gracious, but she accused them of neglecting her\r\ncruelly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see Mr. Acton has had to take pity upon me,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy brother goes off sketching, for hours; I can never depend upon him.\r\nSo I was to send Mr. Acton to beg you to come and give me the benefit\r\nof your wisdom.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at her sister. She wanted to say, \xe2\x80\x9c_That_ is what she\r\nwould have done.\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte said that they hoped the Baroness would\r\nalways come and dine with them; it would give them so much pleasure;\r\nand, in that case, she would spare herself the trouble of having a\r\ncook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but I must have a cook!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cAn old negress in a\r\nyellow turban. I have set my heart upon that. I want to look out of my\r\nwindow and see her sitting there on the grass, against the background\r\nof those crooked, dusky little apple trees, pulling the husks off a\r\nlapful of Indian corn. That will be local color, you know. There isn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nmuch of it here\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t mind my saying that, do you?\xe2\x80\x94so one must make\r\nthe most of what one can get. I shall be most happy to dine with you\r\nwhenever you will let me; but I want to be able to ask you sometimes.\r\nAnd I want to be able to ask Mr. Acton,\xe2\x80\x9d added the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must come and ask me at home,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must come and see\r\nme; you must dine with me first. I want to show you my place; I want to\r\nintroduce you to my mother.\xe2\x80\x9d He called again upon Madame M\xc3\xbcnster, two\r\ndays later. He was constantly at the other house; he used to walk\r\nacross the fields from his own place, and he appeared to have fewer\r\nscruples than his cousins with regard to dropping in. On this occasion\r\nhe found that Mr. Brand had come to pay his respects to the charming\r\nstranger; but after Acton\xe2\x80\x99s arrival the young theologian said nothing.\r\nHe sat in his chair with his two hands clasped, fixing upon his hostess\r\na grave, fascinated stare. The Baroness talked to Robert Acton, but, as\r\nshe talked, she turned and smiled at Mr. Brand, who never took his eyes\r\noff her. The two men walked away together; they were going to Mr.\r\nWentworth\xe2\x80\x99s. Mr. Brand still said nothing; but after they had passed\r\ninto Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s garden he stopped and looked back for some time at\r\nthe little white house. Then, looking at his companion, with his head\r\nbent a little to one side and his eyes somewhat contracted, \xe2\x80\x9cNow I\r\nsuppose that\xe2\x80\x99s what is called conversation,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9creal\r\nconversation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s what I call a very clever woman,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is most interesting,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI only wish she would\r\nspeak French; it would seem more in keeping. It must be quite the style\r\nthat we have heard about, that we have read about\xe2\x80\x94the style of\r\nconversation of Madame de Sta\xc3\xabl, of Madame R\xc3\xa9camier.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon also looked at Madame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s residence among its hollyhocks\r\nand apple trees. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat I should like to know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cis\r\njust what has brought Madame R\xc3\xa9camier to live in that place!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V\r\n\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth, with his cane and his gloves in his hand, went every\r\nafternoon to call upon his niece. A couple of hours later she came over\r\nto the great house to tea. She had let the proposal that she should\r\nregularly dine there fall to the ground; she was in the enjoyment of\r\nwhatever satisfaction was to be derived from the spectacle of an old\r\nnegress in a crimson turban shelling peas under the apple trees.\r\nCharlotte, who had provided the ancient negress, thought it must be a\r\nstrange household, Eugenia having told her that Augustine managed\r\neverything, the ancient negress included\xe2\x80\x94Augustine who was naturally\r\ndevoid of all acquaintance with the expurgatory English tongue. By far\r\nthe most immoral sentiment which I shall have occasion to attribute to\r\nCharlotte Wentworth was a certain emotion of disappointment at finding\r\nthat, in spite of these irregular conditions, the domestic arrangements\r\nat the small house were apparently not\xe2\x80\x94from Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s peculiar point of\r\nview\xe2\x80\x94strikingly offensive. The Baroness found it amusing to go to tea;\r\nshe dressed as if for dinner. The tea-table offered an anomalous and\r\npicturesque repast; and on leaving it they all sat and talked in the\r\nlarge piazza, or wandered about the garden in the starlight, with their\r\nears full of those sounds of strange insects which, though they are\r\nsupposed to be, all over the world, a part of the magic of summer\r\nnights, seemed to the Baroness to have beneath these western skies an\r\nincomparable resonance.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth, though, as I say, he went punctiliously to call upon\r\nher, was not able to feel that he was getting used to his niece. It\r\ntaxed his imagination to believe that she was really his half-sister\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchild. His sister was a figure of his early years; she had been only\r\ntwenty when she went abroad, never to return, making in foreign parts a\r\nwillful and undesirable marriage. His aunt, Mrs. Whiteside, who had\r\ntaken her to Europe for the benefit of the tour, gave, on her return,\r\nso lamentable an account of Mr. Adolphus Young, to whom the headstrong\r\ngirl had united her destiny, that it operated as a chill upon family\r\nfeeling\xe2\x80\x94especially in the case of the half-brothers. Catherine had done\r\nnothing subsequently to propitiate her family; she had not even written\r\nto them in a way that indicated a lucid appreciation of their suspended\r\nsympathy; so that it had become a tradition in Boston circles that the\r\nhighest charity, as regards this young lady, was to think it well to\r\nforget her, and to abstain from conjecture as to the extent to which\r\nher aberrations were reproduced in her descendants. Over these young\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x94a vague report of their existence had come to his ears\xe2\x80\x94Mr.\r\nWentworth had not, in the course of years, allowed his imagination to\r\nhover. It had plenty of occupation nearer home, and though he had many\r\ncares upon his conscience the idea that he had been an unnatural uncle\r\nwas, very properly, never among the number. Now that his nephew and\r\nniece had come before him, he perceived that they were the fruit of\r\ninfluences and circumstances very different from those under which his\r\nown familiar progeny had reached a vaguely-qualified maturity. He felt\r\nno provocation to say that these influences had been exerted for evil;\r\nbut he was sometimes afraid that he should not be able to like his\r\ndistinguished, delicate, lady-like niece. He was paralyzed and\r\nbewildered by her foreignness. She spoke, somehow, a different\r\nlanguage. There was something strange in her words. He had a feeling\r\nthat another man, in his place, would accommodate himself to her tone;\r\nwould ask her questions and joke with her, reply to those pleasantries\r\nof her own which sometimes seemed startling as addressed to an uncle.\r\nBut Mr. Wentworth could not do these things. He could not even bring\r\nhimself to attempt to measure her position in the world. She was the\r\nwife of a foreign nobleman who desired to repudiate her. This had a\r\nsingular sound, but the old man felt himself destitute of the materials\r\nfor a judgment. It seemed to him that he ought to find them in his own\r\nexperience, as a man of the world and an almost public character; but\r\nthey were not there, and he was ashamed to confess to himself\xe2\x80\x94much more\r\nto reveal to Eugenia by interrogations possibly too innocent\xe2\x80\x94the\r\nunfurnished condition of this repository.\r\n\r\nIt appeared to him that he could get much nearer, as he would have\r\nsaid, to his nephew; though he was not sure that Felix was altogether\r\nsafe. He was so bright and handsome and talkative that it was\r\nimpossible not to think well of him; and yet it seemed as if there were\r\nsomething almost impudent, almost vicious\xe2\x80\x94or as if there ought to be\xe2\x80\x94in\r\na young man being at once so joyous and so positive. It was to be\r\nobserved that while Felix was not at all a serious young man there was\r\nsomehow more of him\xe2\x80\x94he had more weight and volume and resonance\xe2\x80\x94than a\r\nnumber of young men who were distinctly serious. While Mr. Wentworth\r\nmeditated upon this anomaly his nephew was admiring him unrestrictedly.\r\nHe thought him a most delicate, generous, high-toned old gentleman,\r\nwith a very handsome head, of the ascetic type, which he promised\r\nhimself the profit of sketching. Felix was far from having made a\r\nsecret of the fact that he wielded the paint-brush, and it was not his\r\nown fault if it failed to be generally understood that he was prepared\r\nto execute the most striking likenesses on the most reasonable terms.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is an artist\xe2\x80\x94my cousin is an artist,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude; and she\r\noffered this information to everyone who would receive it. She offered\r\nit to herself, as it were, by way of admonition and reminder; she\r\nrepeated to herself at odd moments, in lonely places, that Felix was\r\ninvested with this sacred character. Gertrude had never seen an artist\r\nbefore; she had only read about such people. They seemed to her a\r\nromantic and mysterious class, whose life was made up of those\r\nagreeable accidents that never happened to other persons. And it merely\r\nquickened her meditations on this point that Felix should declare, as\r\nhe repeatedly did, that he was really not an artist. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never gone\r\ninto the thing seriously,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have never studied; I have had\r\nno training. I do a little of everything, and nothing well. I am only\r\nan amateur.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt pleased Gertrude even more to think that he was an amateur than to\r\nthink that he was an artist; the former word, to her fancy, had an even\r\nsubtler connotation. She knew, however, that it was a word to use more\r\nsoberly. Mr. Wentworth used it freely; for though he had not been\r\nexactly familiar with it, he found it convenient as a help toward\r\nclassifying Felix, who, as a young man extremely clever and active and\r\napparently respectable and yet not engaged in any recognized business,\r\nwas an importunate anomaly. Of course the Baroness and her brother\xe2\x80\x94she\r\nwas always spoken of first\xe2\x80\x94were a welcome topic of conversation between\r\nMr. Wentworth and his daughters and their occasional visitors.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the young man, your nephew, what is his profession?\xe2\x80\x9d asked an old\r\ngentleman\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Broderip, of Salem\xe2\x80\x94who had been Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s classmate\r\nat Harvard College in the year 1809, and who came into his office in\r\nDevonshire Street. (Mr. Wentworth, in his later years, used to go but\r\nthree times a week to his office, where he had a large amount of highly\r\nconfidential trust-business to transact.)\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, he\xe2\x80\x99s an amateur,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix\xe2\x80\x99s uncle, with folded hands, and\r\nwith a certain satisfaction in being able to say it. And Mr. Broderip\r\nhad gone back to Salem with a feeling that this was probably a\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEuropean\xe2\x80\x9d expression for a broker or a grain exporter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to do your head, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix to his uncle one\r\nevening, before them all\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Brand and Robert Acton being also present.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I should make a very fine thing of it. It\xe2\x80\x99s an interesting\r\nhead; it\xe2\x80\x99s very mediaeval.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth looked grave; he felt awkwardly, as if all the company\r\nhad come in and found him standing before the looking-glass. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Lord\r\nmade it,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it is for man to make it over again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertainly the Lord made it,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Felix, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cand he made it\r\nvery well. But life has been touching up the work. It is a very\r\ninteresting type of head. It\xe2\x80\x99s delightfully wasted and emaciated. The\r\ncomplexion is wonderfully bleached.\xe2\x80\x9d And Felix looked round at the\r\ncircle, as if to call their attention to these interesting points. Mr.\r\nWentworth grew visibly paler. \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to do you as an old\r\nprelate, an old cardinal, or the prior of an order.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA prelate, a cardinal?\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Mr. Wentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you refer to the\r\nRoman Catholic priesthood?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean an old ecclesiastic who should have led a very pure, abstinent\r\nlife. Now I take it that has been the case with you, sir; one sees it\r\nin your face,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix proceeded. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have been very\xe2\x80\x94a very moderate.\r\nDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think one always sees that in a man\xe2\x80\x99s face?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou see more in a man\xe2\x80\x99s face than I should think of looking for,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nMr. Wentworth coldly.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness rattled her fan, and gave her brilliant laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is a\r\nrisk to look so close!\xe2\x80\x9d she exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cMy uncle has some peccadilloes\r\non his conscience.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth looked at her, painfully at a loss;\r\nand in so far as the signs of a pure and abstinent life were visible in\r\nhis face they were then probably peculiarly manifest. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are a _beau\r\nvieillard_, dear uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame M\xc3\xbcnster, smiling with her foreign\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are paying me a compliment,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSurely, I am not the first woman that ever did so!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the\r\nBaroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you are,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth gravely. And turning to Felix he\r\nadded, in the same tone, \xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t take my likeness. My children\r\nhave my daguerreotype. That is quite satisfactory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t promise,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cnot to work your head into something!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth looked at him and then at all the others; then he got up\r\nand slowly walked away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFelix,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, in the silence that followed, \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would\r\npaint my portrait.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte wondered whether Gertrude was right in wishing this; and she\r\nlooked at Mr. Brand as the most legitimate way of ascertaining.\r\nWhatever Gertrude did or said, Charlotte always looked at Mr. Brand. It\r\nwas a standing pretext for looking at Mr. Brand\xe2\x80\x94always, as Charlotte\r\nthought, in the interest of Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s welfare. It is true that she\r\nfelt a tremulous interest in Gertrude being right; for Charlotte, in\r\nher small, still way, was an heroic sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe should be glad to have your portrait, Miss Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nBrand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be delighted to paint so charming a model,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think you are so lovely, my dear?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Lizzie Acton, with her\r\nlittle inoffensive pertness, biting off a knot in her knitting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not because I think I am beautiful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, looking all\r\nround. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think I am beautiful, at all.\xe2\x80\x9d She spoke with a sort of\r\nconscious deliberateness; and it seemed very strange to Charlotte to\r\nhear her discussing this question so publicly. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is because I think\r\nit would be amusing to sit and be painted. I have always thought that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry you have not had better things to think about, my\r\ndaughter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are very beautiful, cousin Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a compliment,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cI put all the compliments I\r\nreceive into a little money-jug that has a slit in the side. I shake\r\nthem up and down, and they rattle. There are not many yet\xe2\x80\x94only two or\r\nthree.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it\xe2\x80\x99s not a compliment,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cSee; I am careful not to\r\ngive it the form of a compliment. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t think you were beautiful at\r\nfirst. But you have come to seem so little by little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care, now, your jug doesn\xe2\x80\x99t burst!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Lizzie.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think sitting for one\xe2\x80\x99s portrait is only one of the various forms of\r\nidleness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cTheir name is legion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sir,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can\xe2\x80\x99t be said to be idle when you are\r\nmaking a man work so!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne might be painted while one is asleep,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Mr. Brand, as a\r\ncontribution to the discussion.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, do paint me while I am asleep,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude to Felix, smiling.\r\nAnd she closed her eyes a little. It had by this time become a matter\r\nof almost exciting anxiety to Charlotte what Gertrude would say or\r\nwould do next.\r\n\r\nShe began to sit for her portrait on the following day\xe2\x80\x94in the open air,\r\non the north side of the piazza. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would tell me what you\r\nthink of us\xe2\x80\x94how we seem to you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to Felix, as he sat before\r\nhis easel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to me the best people in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou say that,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude resumed, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause it saves you the trouble of\r\nsaying anything else.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe young man glanced at her over the top of his canvas. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat else\r\nshould I say? It would certainly be a great deal of trouble to say\r\nanything different.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have seen people before that you have\r\nliked, have you not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed I have, thank Heaven!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd they have been very different from us,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude went on.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat only proves,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cthat there are a thousand different\r\nways of being good company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think us good company?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCompany for a king!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude was silent a moment; and then, \xe2\x80\x9cThere must be a thousand\r\ndifferent ways of being dreary,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cand sometimes I think we\r\nmake use of them all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix stood up quickly, holding up his hand. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you could only keep\r\nthat look on your face for half an hour\xe2\x80\x94while I catch it!\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nis uncommonly handsome.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo look handsome for half an hour\xe2\x80\x94that is a great deal to ask of me,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would be the portrait of a young woman who has taken some vow, some\r\npledge, that she repents of,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cand who is thinking it over\r\nat leisure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have taken no vow, no pledge,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, very gravely; \xe2\x80\x9cI have\r\nnothing to repent of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear cousin, that was only a figure of speech. I am very sure that\r\nno one in your excellent family has anything to repent of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd yet we are always repenting!\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is what I\r\nmean by our being dreary. You know it perfectly well; you only pretend\r\nthat you don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix gave a quick laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cThe half hour is going on, and yet you are\r\nhandsomer than ever. One must be careful what one says, you see.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, \xe2\x80\x9cyou can say anything.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix looked at her, as an artist might, and painted for some time in\r\nsilence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you seem to me different from your father and sister\xe2\x80\x94from most of\r\nthe people you have lived with,\xe2\x80\x9d he observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo say that one\xe2\x80\x99s self,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude went on, \xe2\x80\x9cis like saying\xe2\x80\x94by\r\nimplication, at least\xe2\x80\x94that one is better. I am not better; I am much\r\nworse. But they say themselves that I am different. It makes them\r\nunhappy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince you accuse me of concealing my real impressions, I may admit\r\nthat I think the tendency\xe2\x80\x94among you generally\xe2\x80\x94is to be made unhappy too\r\neasily.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would tell that to my father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might make him more unhappy!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed, laughing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt certainly would. I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you have seen people like that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my dear cousin, how do you know what I have seen?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix demanded.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou might tell me a great many things, if you only would. You have\r\nseen people like yourself\xe2\x80\x94people who are bright and gay and fond of\r\namusement. We are not fond of amusement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cI confess that rather strikes me. You don\xe2\x80\x99t seem to\r\nme to get all the pleasure out of life that you might. You don\xe2\x80\x99t seem\r\nto me to enjoy..... Do you mind my saying this?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, pausing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease go on,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl, earnestly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to me very well placed for enjoying. You have money and\r\nliberty and what is called in Europe a \xe2\x80\x98position.\xe2\x80\x99 But you take a\r\npainful view of life, as one may say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne ought to think it bright and charming and delightful, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d asked\r\nGertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should say so\xe2\x80\x94if one can. It is true it all depends upon that,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nFelix added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know there is a great deal of misery in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d said his\r\nmodel.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have seen a little of it,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it was all\r\nover there\xe2\x80\x94beyond the sea. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see any here. This is a paradise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude said nothing; she sat looking at the dahlias and the\r\ncurrant-bushes in the garden, while Felix went on with his work. \xe2\x80\x9cTo\r\n\xe2\x80\x98enjoy,\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d she began at last, \xe2\x80\x9cto take life\xe2\x80\x94not painfully, must one do\r\nsomething wrong?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix gave his long, light laugh again. \xe2\x80\x9cSeriously, I think not. And\r\nfor this reason, among others: you strike me as very capable of\r\nenjoying, if the chance were given you, and yet at the same time as\r\nincapable of wrong-doing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sure,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are very wrong in telling a\r\nperson that she is incapable of that. We are never nearer to evil than\r\nwhen we believe that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are handsomer than ever,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Felix, irrelevantly.\r\n\r\nGertrude had got used to hearing him say this. There was not so much\r\nexcitement in it as at first. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat ought one to do?\xe2\x80\x9d she continued.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo give parties, to go to the theatre, to read novels, to keep late\r\nhours?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think it\xe2\x80\x99s what one does or one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t do that promotes\r\nenjoyment,\xe2\x80\x9d her companion answered. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the general way of looking\r\nat life.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey look at it as a discipline\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what they do here. I have often\r\nbeen told that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\xe2\x80\x99s very good. But there is another way,\xe2\x80\x9d added Felix,\r\nsmiling: \xe2\x80\x9cto look at it as an opportunity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn opportunity\xe2\x80\x94yes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cOne would get more pleasure that\r\nway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t attempt to say anything better for it than that it has been my\r\nown way\xe2\x80\x94and that is not saying much!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix had laid down his palette\r\nand brushes; he was leaning back, with his arms folded, to judge the\r\neffect of his work. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you know,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cI am a very petty\r\npersonage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have a great deal of talent,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo\xe2\x80\x94no,\xe2\x80\x9d the young man rejoined, in a tone of cheerful impartiality, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave not a great deal of talent. It is nothing at all remarkable. I\r\nassure you I should know if it were. I shall always be obscure. The\r\nworld will never hear of me.\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude looked at him with a strange\r\nfeeling. She was thinking of the great world which he knew and which\r\nshe did not, and how full of brilliant talents it must be, since it\r\ncould afford to make light of his abilities. \xe2\x80\x9cYou needn\xe2\x80\x99t in general\r\nattach much importance to anything I tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued; \xe2\x80\x9cbut you\r\nmay believe me when I say this,\xe2\x80\x94that I am little better than a\r\ngood-natured feather-head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA feather-head?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am a species of Bohemian.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Bohemian?\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude had never heard this term before, save as a\r\ngeographical denomination; and she quite failed to understand the\r\nfigurative meaning which her companion appeared to attach to it. But it\r\ngave her pleasure.\r\n\r\nFelix had pushed back his chair and risen to his feet; he slowly came\r\ntoward her, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI am a sort of adventurer,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, looking down\r\nat her.\r\n\r\nShe got up, meeting his smile. \xe2\x80\x9cAn adventurer?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cI should\r\nlike to hear your adventures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor an instant she believed that he was going to take her hand; but he\r\ndropped his own hands suddenly into the pockets of his painting-jacket.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no reason why you shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been an\r\nadventurer, but my adventures have been very innocent. They have all\r\nbeen happy ones; I don\xe2\x80\x99t think there are any I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t tell. They\r\nwere very pleasant and very pretty; I should like to go over them in\r\nmemory. Sit down again, and I will begin,\xe2\x80\x9d he added in a moment, with\r\nhis naturally persuasive smile.\r\n\r\nGertrude sat down again on that day, and she sat down on several other\r\ndays. Felix, while he plied his brush, told her a great many stories,\r\nand she listened with charmed avidity. Her eyes rested upon his lips;\r\nshe was very serious; sometimes, from her air of wondering gravity, he\r\nthought she was displeased. But Felix never believed for more than a\r\nsingle moment in any displeasure of his own producing. This would have\r\nbeen fatuity if the optimism it expressed had not been much more a hope\r\nthan a prejudice. It is beside the matter to say that he had a good\r\nconscience; for the best conscience is a sort of self-reproach, and\r\nthis young man\xe2\x80\x99s brilliantly healthy nature spent itself in objective\r\ngood intentions which were ignorant of any test save exactness in\r\nhitting their mark. He told Gertrude how he had walked over France and\r\nItaly with a painter\xe2\x80\x99s knapsack on his back, paying his way often by\r\nknocking off a flattering portrait of his host or hostess. He told her\r\nhow he had played the violin in a little band of musicians\xe2\x80\x94not of high\r\ncelebrity\xe2\x80\x94who traveled through foreign lands giving provincial\r\nconcerts. He told her also how he had been a momentary ornament of a\r\ntroupe of strolling actors, engaged in the arduous task of interpreting\r\nShakespeare to French and German, Polish and Hungarian audiences.\r\n\r\nWhile this periodical recital was going on, Gertrude lived in a\r\nfantastic world; she seemed to herself to be reading a romance that\r\ncame out in daily numbers. She had known nothing so delightful since\r\nthe perusal of _Nicholas Nickleby_. One afternoon she went to see her\r\ncousin, Mrs. Acton, Robert\xe2\x80\x99s mother, who was a great invalid, never\r\nleaving the house. She came back alone, on foot, across the fields\xe2\x80\x94this\r\nbeing a short way which they often used. Felix had gone to Boston with\r\nher father, who desired to take the young man to call upon some of his\r\nfriends, old gentlemen who remembered his mother\xe2\x80\x94remembered her, but\r\nsaid nothing about her\xe2\x80\x94and several of whom, with the gentle ladies\r\ntheir wives, had driven out from town to pay their respects at the\r\nlittle house among the apple trees, in vehicles which reminded the\r\nBaroness, who received her visitors with discriminating civility, of\r\nthe large, light, rattling barouche in which she herself had made her\r\njourney to this neighborhood. The afternoon was waning; in the western\r\nsky the great picture of a New England sunset, painted in crimson and\r\nsilver, was suspended from the zenith; and the stony pastures, as\r\nGertrude traversed them, thinking intently to herself, were covered\r\nwith a light, clear glow. At the open gate of one of the fields she saw\r\nfrom the distance a man\xe2\x80\x99s figure; he stood there as if he were waiting\r\nfor her, and as she came nearer she recognized Mr. Brand. She had a\r\nfeeling as of not having seen him for some time; she could not have\r\nsaid for how long, for it yet seemed to her that he had been very\r\nlately at the house.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay I walk back with you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. And when she had said that he\r\nmight if he wanted, he observed that he had seen her and recognized her\r\nhalf a mile away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must have very good eyes,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have very good eyes, Miss Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand. She\r\nperceived that he meant something; but for a long time past Mr. Brand\r\nhad constantly meant something, and she had almost got used to it.
