She\r\nfelt, however, that what he meant had now a renewed power to disturb\r\nher, to perplex and agitate her. He walked beside her in silence for a\r\nmoment, and then he added, \xe2\x80\x9cI have had no trouble in seeing that you\r\nare beginning to avoid me. But perhaps,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cone needn\xe2\x80\x99t have\r\nhad very good eyes to see that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not avoided you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, without looking at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you have been unconscious that you were avoiding me,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nBrand replied. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have not even known that I was there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you are here now, Mr. Brand!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, with a little\r\nlaugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI know that very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made no rejoinder. He simply walked beside her slowly, as they were\r\nobliged to walk over the soft grass. Presently they came to another\r\ngate, which was closed. Mr. Brand laid his hand upon it, but he made no\r\nmovement to open it; he stood and looked at his companion. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are\r\nvery much interested\xe2\x80\x94very much absorbed,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\nGertrude glanced at him; she saw that he was pale and that he looked\r\nexcited. She had never seen Mr. Brand excited before, and she felt that\r\nthe spectacle, if fully carried out, would be impressive, almost\r\npainful. \xe2\x80\x9cAbsorbed in what?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. Then she looked away at the\r\nilluminated sky. She felt guilty and uncomfortable, and yet she was\r\nvexed with herself for feeling so. But Mr. Brand, as he stood there\r\nlooking at her with his small, kind, persistent eyes, represented an\r\nimmense body of half-obliterated obligations, that were rising again\r\ninto a certain distinctness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have new interests, new occupations,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nthat I can say that you have new duties. We have always old ones,\r\nGertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease open the gate, Mr. Brand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and she felt as if, in\r\nsaying so, she were cowardly and petulant. But he opened the gate, and\r\nallowed her to pass; then he closed it behind himself. Before she had\r\ntime to turn away he put out his hand and held her an instant by the\r\nwrist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to say something to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what you want to say,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. And she was on the point\r\nof adding, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I know just how you will say it;\xe2\x80\x9d but these words she\r\nkept back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI love you, Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI love you very much; I love you more\r\nthan ever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said the words just as she had known he would; she had heard them\r\nbefore. They had no charm for her; she had said to herself before that\r\nit was very strange. It was supposed to be delightful for a woman to\r\nlisten to such words; but these seemed to her flat and mechanical. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwish you would forget that,\xe2\x80\x9d she declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can I\xe2\x80\x94why should I?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have made you no promise\xe2\x80\x94given you no pledge,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, looking at\r\nhim, with her voice trembling a little.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have let me feel that I have an influence over you. You have\r\nopened your mind to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI never opened my mind to you, Mr. Brand!\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude cried, with some\r\nvehemence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you were not so frank as I thought\xe2\x80\x94as we all thought.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see what anyone else had to do with it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the girl.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean your father and your sister. You know it makes them happy to\r\nthink you will listen to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe gave a little laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cIt doesn\xe2\x80\x99t make them happy,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNothing makes them happy. No one is happy here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think your cousin is very happy\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Young,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Mr. Brand, in a\r\nsoft, almost timid tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much the better for him!\xe2\x80\x9d And Gertrude gave her little laugh again.\r\n\r\nThe young man looked at her a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are very much changed,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am glad to hear it,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not. I have known you a long time, and I have loved you as you\r\nwere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cI must be going home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe on his side, gave a little laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly do avoid me\xe2\x80\x94you see!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAvoid me, then,\xe2\x80\x9d said the girl.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her again; and then, very gently, \xe2\x80\x9cNo I will not avoid\r\nyou,\xe2\x80\x9d he replied; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I will leave you, for the present, to yourself.\r\nI think you will remember\xe2\x80\x94after a while\xe2\x80\x94some of the things you have\r\nforgotten. I think you will come back to me; I have great faith in\r\nthat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis time his voice was very touching; there was a strong, reproachful\r\nforce in what he said, and Gertrude could answer nothing. He turned\r\naway and stood there, leaning his elbows on the gate and looking at the\r\nbeautiful sunset. Gertrude left him and took her way home again; but\r\nwhen she reached the middle of the next field she suddenly burst into\r\ntears. Her tears seemed to her to have been a long time gathering, and\r\nfor some moments it was a kind of glee to shed them. But they presently\r\npassed away. There was something a little hard about Gertrude; and she\r\nnever wept again.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI\r\n\r\n\r\nGoing of an afternoon to call upon his niece, Mr. Wentworth more than\r\nonce found Robert Acton sitting in her little drawing-room. This was in\r\nno degree, to Mr. Wentworth, a perturbing fact, for he had no sense of\r\ncompeting with his young kinsman for Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s good graces. Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s uncle had the highest opinion of Robert Acton, who, indeed,\r\nin the family at large, was the object of a great deal of\r\nundemonstrative appreciation. They were all proud of him, in so far as\r\nthe charge of being proud may be brought against people who were,\r\nhabitually, distinctly guiltless of the misdemeanor known as \xe2\x80\x9ctaking\r\ncredit.\xe2\x80\x9d They never boasted of Robert Acton, nor indulged in\r\nvainglorious reference to him; they never quoted the clever things he\r\nhad said, nor mentioned the generous things he had done. But a sort of\r\nfrigidly-tender faith in his unlimited goodness was a part of their\r\npersonal sense of right; and there can, perhaps, be no better proof of\r\nthe high esteem in which he was held than the fact that no explicit\r\njudgment was ever passed upon his actions. He was no more praised than\r\nhe was blamed; but he was tacitly felt to be an ornament to his circle.\r\nHe was the man of the world of the family. He had been to China and\r\nbrought home a collection of curiosities; he had made a fortune\xe2\x80\x94or\r\nrather he had quintupled a fortune already considerable; he was\r\ndistinguished by that combination of celibacy, \xe2\x80\x9cproperty,\xe2\x80\x9d and good\r\nhumor which appeals to even the most subdued imaginations; and it was\r\ntaken for granted that he would presently place these advantages at the\r\ndisposal of some well-regulated young woman of his own \xe2\x80\x9cset.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr.\r\nWentworth was not a man to admit to himself that\xe2\x80\x94his paternal duties\r\napart\xe2\x80\x94he liked any individual much better than all other individuals;\r\nbut he thought Robert Acton extremely judicious; and this was perhaps\r\nas near an approach as he was capable of to the eagerness of\r\npreference, which his temperament repudiated as it would have\r\ndisengaged itself from something slightly unchaste. Acton was, in fact,\r\nvery judicious\xe2\x80\x94and something more beside; and indeed it must be claimed\r\nfor Mr. Wentworth that in the more illicit parts of his preference\r\nthere hovered the vague adumbration of a belief that his cousin\xe2\x80\x99s final\r\nmerit was a certain enviable capacity for whistling, rather gallantly,\r\nat the sanctions of mere judgment\xe2\x80\x94for showing a larger courage, a finer\r\nquality of pluck, than common occasion demanded. Mr. Wentworth would\r\nnever have risked the intimation that Acton was made, in the smallest\r\ndegree, of the stuff of a hero; but this is small blame to him, for\r\nRobert would certainly never have risked it himself. Acton certainly\r\nexercised great discretion in all things\xe2\x80\x94beginning with his estimate of\r\nhimself. He knew that he was by no means so much of a man of the world\r\nas he was supposed to be in local circles; but it must be added that he\r\nknew also that his natural shrewdness had a reach of which he had never\r\nquite given local circles the measure. He was addicted to taking the\r\nhumorous view of things, and he had discovered that even in the\r\nnarrowest circles such a disposition may find frequent opportunities.\r\nSuch opportunities had formed for some time\xe2\x80\x94that is, since his return\r\nfrom China, a year and a half before\xe2\x80\x94the most active element in this\r\ngentleman\xe2\x80\x99s life, which had just now a rather indolent air. He was\r\nperfectly willing to get married. He was very fond of books, and he had\r\na handsome library; that is, his books were much more numerous than Mr.\r\nWentworth\xe2\x80\x99s. He was also very fond of pictures; but it must be\r\nconfessed, in the fierce light of contemporary criticism, that his\r\nwalls were adorned with several rather abortive masterpieces. He had\r\ngot his learning\xe2\x80\x94and there was more of it than commonly appeared\xe2\x80\x94at\r\nHarvard College; and he took a pleasure in old associations, which made\r\nit a part of his daily contentment to live so near this institution\r\nthat he often passed it in driving to Boston. He was extremely\r\ninterested in the Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster.\r\n\r\nShe was very frank with him; or at least she intended to be. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure\r\nyou find it very strange that I should have settled down in this\r\nout-of-the-way part of the world!\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him three or four weeks\r\nafter she had installed herself. \xe2\x80\x9cI am certain you are wondering about\r\nmy motives. They are very pure.\xe2\x80\x9d The Baroness by this time was an old\r\ninhabitant; the best society in Boston had called upon her, and\r\nClifford Wentworth had taken her several times to drive in his buggy.\r\n\r\nRobert Acton was seated near her, playing with a fan; there were always\r\nseveral fans lying about her drawing-room, with long ribbons of\r\ndifferent colors attached to them, and Acton was always playing with\r\none. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I don\xe2\x80\x99t find it at all strange,\xe2\x80\x9d he said slowly, smiling.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat a clever woman should turn up in Boston, or its suburbs\xe2\x80\x94that does\r\nnot require so much explanation. Boston is a very nice place.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you wish to make me contradict you,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, \xe2\x80\x9c_vous vous\r\ny prenez mal_. In certain moods there is nothing I am not capable of\r\nagreeing to. Boston is a paradise, and we are in the suburbs of\r\nParadise.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust now I am not at all in the suburbs; I am in the place itself,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nrejoined Acton, who was lounging a little in his chair. He was,\r\nhowever, not always lounging; and when he was he was not quite so\r\nrelaxed as he pretended. To a certain extent, he sought refuge from\r\nshyness in this appearance of relaxation; and like many persons in the\r\nsame circumstances he somewhat exaggerated the appearance. Beyond this,\r\nthe air of being much at his ease was a cover for vigilant observation.\r\nHe was more than interested in this clever woman, who, whatever he\r\nmight say, was clever not at all after the Boston fashion; she plunged\r\nhim into a kind of excitement, held him in vague suspense. He was\r\nobliged to admit to himself that he had never yet seen a woman just\r\nlike this\xe2\x80\x94not even in China. He was ashamed, for inscrutable reasons,\r\nof the vivacity of his emotion, and he carried it off, superficially,\r\nby taking, still superficially, the humorous view of Madame M\xc3\xbcnster. It\r\nwas not at all true that he thought it very natural of her to have made\r\nthis pious pilgrimage. It might have been said of him in advance that\r\nhe was too good a Bostonian to regard in the light of an eccentricity\r\nthe desire of even the remotest alien to visit the New England\r\nmetropolis. This was an impulse for which, surely, no apology was\r\nneeded; and Madame M\xc3\xbcnster was the fortunate possessor of several New\r\nEngland cousins. In fact, however, Madame M\xc3\xbcnster struck him as out of\r\nkeeping with her little circle; she was at the best a very agreeable, a\r\ngracefully mystifying anomaly. He knew very well that it would not do\r\nto address these reflections too crudely to Mr. Wentworth; he would\r\nnever have remarked to the old gentleman that he wondered what the\r\nBaroness was up to. And indeed he had no great desire to share his\r\nvague mistrust with anyone. There was a personal pleasure in it; the\r\ngreatest pleasure he had known at least since he had come from China.\r\nHe would keep the Baroness, for better or worse, to himself; he had a\r\nfeeling that he deserved to enjoy a monopoly of her, for he was\r\ncertainly the person who had most adequately gauged her capacity for\r\nsocial intercourse. Before long it became apparent to him that the\r\nBaroness was disposed to lay no tax upon such a monopoly.\r\n\r\nOne day (he was sitting there again and playing with a fan) she asked\r\nhim to apologize, should the occasion present itself, to certain people\r\nin Boston for her not having returned their calls. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are half a\r\ndozen places,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9ca formidable list. Charlotte Wentworth has\r\nwritten it out for me, in a terrifically distinct hand. There is no\r\nambiguity on the subject; I know perfectly where I must go. Mr.\r\nWentworth informs me that the carriage is always at my disposal, and\r\nCharlotte offers to go with me, in a pair of tight gloves and a very\r\nstiff petticoat. And yet for three days I have been putting it off.\r\nThey must think me horribly vicious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ask me to apologize,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, \xe2\x80\x9cbut you don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me what\r\nexcuse I can offer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is more,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness declared, \xe2\x80\x9cthan I am held to. It would be\r\nlike my asking you to buy me a bouquet and giving you the money. I have\r\nno reason except that\xe2\x80\x94somehow\xe2\x80\x94it\xe2\x80\x99s too violent an effort. It is not\r\ninspiring. Wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t that serve as an excuse, in Boston? I am told they\r\nare very sincere; they don\xe2\x80\x99t tell fibs. And then Felix ought to go with\r\nme, and he is never in readiness. I don\xe2\x80\x99t see him. He is always roaming\r\nabout the fields and sketching old barns, or taking ten-mile walks, or\r\npainting someone\xe2\x80\x99s portrait, or rowing on the pond, or flirting with\r\nGertrude Wentworth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should think it would amuse you to go and see a few people,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nActon. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are having a very quiet time of it here. It\xe2\x80\x99s a dull life\r\nfor you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, the quiet,\xe2\x80\x94the quiet!\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness exclaimed. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what I\r\nlike. It\xe2\x80\x99s rest. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I came here for. Amusement? I have had\r\namusement. And as for seeing people\xe2\x80\x94I have already seen a great many in\r\nmy life. If it didn\xe2\x80\x99t sound ungracious I should say that I wish very\r\nhumbly your people here would leave me alone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon looked at her a moment, and she looked at him. She was a woman\r\nwho took being looked at remarkably well. \xe2\x80\x9cSo you have come here for\r\nrest?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo I may say. I came for many of those reasons that are no\r\nreasons\xe2\x80\x94don\xe2\x80\x99t you know?\xe2\x80\x94and yet that are really the best: to come away,\r\nto change, to break with everything. When once one comes away one must\r\narrive somewhere, and I asked myself why I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t arrive here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou certainly had time on the way!\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, laughing.\r\n\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster looked at him again; and then, smiling: \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I have\r\ncertainly had time, since I got here, to ask myself why I came.\r\nHowever, I never ask myself idle questions. Here I am, and it seems to\r\nme you ought only to thank me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen you go away you will see the difficulties I shall put in your\r\npath.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean to put difficulties in my path?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, rearranging the\r\nrosebud in her corsage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe greatest of all\xe2\x80\x94that of having been so agreeable\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat I shall be unable to depart? Don\xe2\x80\x99t be too sure. I have left some\r\nvery agreeable people over there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it was to come here, where I am!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t know of your existence. Excuse me for saying anything so\r\nrude; but, honestly speaking, I did not. No,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness pursued, \xe2\x80\x9cit\r\nwas precisely not to see you\xe2\x80\x94such people as you\xe2\x80\x94that I came.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch people as me?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had a sort of longing to come into those natural relations which I\r\nknew I should find here. Over there I had only, as I may say,\r\nartificial relations. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see the difference?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe difference tells against me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I am an\r\nartificial relation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cConventional,\xe2\x80\x9d declared the Baroness; \xe2\x80\x9cvery conventional.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, there is one way in which the relation of a lady and a gentleman\r\nmay always become natural,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean by their becoming lovers? That may be natural or not. And at\r\nany rate,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Eugenia, _\xe2\x80\x9cnous n\xe2\x80\x99en sommes pas l\xc3\xa0!\xe2\x80\x9d_\r\n\r\nThey were not, as yet; but a little later, when she began to go with\r\nhim to drive, it might almost have seemed that they were. He came for\r\nher several times, alone, in his high \xe2\x80\x9cwagon,\xe2\x80\x9d drawn by a pair of\r\ncharming light-limbed horses. It was different, her having gone with\r\nClifford Wentworth, who was her cousin, and so much younger. It was not\r\nto be imagined that she should have a flirtation with Clifford, who was\r\na mere shame-faced boy, and whom a large section of Boston society\r\nsupposed to be \xe2\x80\x9cengaged\xe2\x80\x9d to Lizzie Acton. Not, indeed, that it was to\r\nbe conceived that the Baroness was a possible party to any flirtation\r\nwhatever; for she was undoubtedly a married lady. It was generally\r\nknown that her matrimonial condition was of the \xe2\x80\x9cmorganatic\xe2\x80\x9d order; but\r\nin its natural aversion to suppose that this meant anything less than\r\nabsolute wedlock, the conscience of the community took refuge in the\r\nbelief that it implied something even more.\r\n\r\nActon wished her to think highly of American scenery, and he drove her\r\nto great distances, picking out the prettiest roads and the largest\r\npoints of view. If we are good when we are contented, Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s virtues\r\nshould now certainly have been uppermost; for she found a charm in the\r\nrapid movement through a wild country, and in a companion who from time\r\nto time made the vehicle dip, with a motion like a swallow\xe2\x80\x99s flight,\r\nover roads of primitive construction, and who, as she felt, would do a\r\ngreat many things that she might ask him. Sometimes, for a couple of\r\nhours together, there were almost no houses; there were nothing but\r\nwoods and rivers and lakes and horizons adorned with bright-looking\r\nmountains. It seemed to the Baroness very wild, as I have said, and\r\nlovely; but the impression added something to that sense of the\r\nenlargement of opportunity which had been born of her arrival in the\r\nNew World.\r\n\r\nOne day\xe2\x80\x94it was late in the afternoon\xe2\x80\x94Acton pulled up his horses on the\r\ncrest of a hill which commanded a beautiful prospect. He let them stand\r\na long time to rest, while he sat there and talked with Madame M\xc3\xbcnster.\r\nThe prospect was beautiful in spite of there being nothing human within\r\nsight. There was a wilderness of woods, and the gleam of a distant\r\nriver, and a glimpse of half the hill-tops in Massachusetts. The road\r\nhad a wide, grassy margin, on the further side of which there flowed a\r\ndeep, clear brook; there were wild flowers in the grass, and beside the\r\nbrook lay the trunk of a fallen tree. Acton waited a while; at last a\r\nrustic wayfarer came trudging along the road. Acton asked him to hold\r\nthe horses\xe2\x80\x94a service he consented to render, as a friendly turn to a\r\nfellow-citizen. Then he invited the Baroness to descend, and the two\r\nwandered away, across the grass, and sat down on the log beside the\r\nbrook.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI imagine it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t remind you of Silberstadt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. It was\r\nthe first time that he had mentioned Silberstadt to her, for particular\r\nreasons. He knew she had a husband there, and this was disagreeable to\r\nhim; and, furthermore, it had been repeated to him that this husband\r\nwished to put her away\xe2\x80\x94a state of affairs to which even indirect\r\nreference was to be deprecated. It was true, nevertheless, that the\r\nBaroness herself had often alluded to Silberstadt; and Acton had often\r\nwondered why her husband wished to get rid of her. It was a curious\r\nposition for a lady\xe2\x80\x94this being known as a repudiated wife; and it is\r\nworthy of observation that the Baroness carried it off with exceeding\r\ngrace and dignity. She had made it felt, from the first, that there\r\nwere two sides to the question, and that her own side, when she should\r\nchoose to present it, would be replete with touching interest.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt does not remind me of the town, of course,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cof the\r\nsculptured gables and the Gothic churches, of the wonderful Schloss,\r\nwith its moat and its clustering towers. But it has a little look of\r\nsome other parts of the principality. One might fancy one\xe2\x80\x99s self among\r\nthose grand old German forests, those legendary mountains; the sort of\r\ncountry one sees from the windows at Schreckenstein.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is Schreckenstein?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a great castle,\xe2\x80\x94the summer residence of the Reigning Prince.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever lived there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have stayed there,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. Acton was silent; he looked a\r\nwhile at the uncastled landscape before him. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the first time you\r\nhave ever asked me about Silberstadt,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI should think you\r\nwould want to know about my marriage; it must seem to you very\r\nstrange.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon looked at her a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cNow you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like me to say that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou Americans have such odd ways!\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness declared. \xe2\x80\x9cYou never\r\nask anything outright; there seem to be so many things you can\xe2\x80\x99t talk\r\nabout.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe Americans are very polite,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, whose national\r\nconsciousness had been complicated by a residence in foreign lands, and\r\nwho yet disliked to hear Americans abused. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t like to tread upon\r\npeople\xe2\x80\x99s toes,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I should like very much to hear about\r\nyour marriage. Now tell me how it came about.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Prince fell in love with me,\xe2\x80\x9d replied the Baroness simply. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\npressed his suit very hard. At first he didn\xe2\x80\x99t wish me to marry him; on\r\nthe contrary. But on that basis I refused to listen to him. So he\r\noffered me marriage\xe2\x80\x94in so far as he might. I was young, and I confess I\r\nwas rather flattered. But if it were to be done again now, I certainly\r\nshould not accept him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long ago was this?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh\xe2\x80\x94several years,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cYou should never ask a woman for\r\ndates.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, I should think that when a woman was relating history \xe2\x80\x9c Acton\r\nanswered. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now he wants to break it off?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey want him to make a political marriage. It is his brother\xe2\x80\x99s idea.\r\nHis brother is very clever.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey must be a precious pair!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Robert Acton.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness gave a little philosophic shrug. \xe2\x80\x9c_Que voulez-vous?_ They\r\nare princes. They think they are treating me very well. Silberstadt is\r\na perfectly despotic little state, and the Reigning Prince may annul\r\nthe marriage by a stroke of his pen. But he has promised me,\r\nnevertheless, not to do so without my formal consent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this you have refused?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHitherto. It is an indignity, and I have wished at least to make it\r\ndifficult for them. But I have a little document in my writing-desk\r\nwhich I have only to sign and send back to the Prince.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen it will be all over?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness lifted her hand, and dropped it again. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course I shall\r\nkeep my title; at least, I shall be at liberty to keep it if I choose.\r\nAnd I suppose I shall keep it. One must have a name. And I shall keep\r\nmy pension. It is very small\xe2\x80\x94it is wretchedly small; but it is what I\r\nlive on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you have only to sign that paper?\xe2\x80\x9d Acton asked.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness looked at him a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you urge it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe got up slowly, and stood with his hands in his pockets. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you\r\ngain by not doing it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am supposed to gain this advantage\xe2\x80\x94that if I delay, or temporize,\r\nthe Prince may come back to me, may make a stand against his brother.\r\nHe is very fond of me, and his brother has pushed him only little by\r\nlittle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he were to come back to you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, \xe2\x80\x9cwould you\xe2\x80\x94would you take\r\nhim back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness met his eyes; she colored just a little. Then she rose. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshould have the satisfaction of saying, \xe2\x80\x98Now it is my turn. I break\r\nwith your Serene Highness!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey began to walk toward the carriage. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Robert Acton,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s a curious story! How did you make his acquaintance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was staying with an old lady\xe2\x80\x94an old Countess\xe2\x80\x94in Dresden. She had\r\nbeen a friend of my father\xe2\x80\x99s. My father was dead; I was very much\r\nalone. My brother was wandering about the world in a theatrical\r\ntroupe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour brother ought to have stayed with you,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton observed, \xe2\x80\x9cand kept\r\nyou from putting your trust in princes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness was silent a moment, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cHe did what he could,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cHe sent me money. The old Countess encouraged the Prince; she\r\nwas even pressing. It seems to me,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame M\xc3\xbcnster added, gently,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x94under the circumstances\xe2\x80\x94I behaved very well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon glanced at her, and made the observation\xe2\x80\x94he had made it\r\nbefore\xe2\x80\x94that a woman looks the prettier for having unfolded her wrongs\r\nor her sufferings. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he reflected, audibly, \xe2\x80\x9cI should like to see\r\nyou send his Serene Highness\xe2\x80\x94somewhere!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster stooped and plucked a daisy from the grass. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd not\r\nsign my renunciation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\xe2\x80\x94I don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn one case I should have my revenge; in another case I should have my\r\nliberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon gave a little laugh as he helped her into the carriage. \xe2\x80\x9cAt any\r\nrate,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9ctake good care of that paper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA couple of days afterward he asked her to come and see his house. The\r\nvisit had already been proposed, but it had been put off in consequence\r\nof his mother\xe2\x80\x99s illness. She was a constant invalid, and she had passed\r\nthese recent years, very patiently, in a great flowered arm-chair at\r\nher bedroom window. Lately, for some days, she had been unable to see\r\nanyone; but now she was better, and she sent the Baroness a very civil\r\nmessage. Acton had wished their visitor to come to dinner; but Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster preferred to begin with a simple call. She had reflected that\r\nif she should go to dinner Mr. Wentworth and his daughters would also\r\nbe asked, and it had seemed to her that the peculiar character of the\r\noccasion would be best preserved in a _t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_ with her host. Why\r\nthe occasion should have a peculiar character she explained to no one.\r\nAs far as anyone could see, it was simply very pleasant. Acton came for\r\nher and drove her to his door, an operation which was rapidly\r\nperformed. His house the Baroness mentally pronounced a very good one;\r\nmore articulately, she declared that it was enchanting. It was large\r\nand square and painted brown; it stood in a well-kept shrubbery, and\r\nwas approached, from the gate, by a short drive. It was, moreover, a\r\nmuch more modern dwelling than Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s, and was more\r\nredundantly upholstered and expensively ornamented. The Baroness\r\nperceived that her entertainer had analyzed material comfort to a\r\nsufficiently fine point. And then he possessed the most delightful\r\n_chinoiseries_\xe2\x80\x94trophies of his sojourn in the Celestial Empire: pagodas\r\nof ebony and cabinets of ivory; sculptured monsters, grinning and\r\nleering on chimney-pieces, in front of beautifully figured\r\nhand-screens; porcelain dinner-sets, gleaming behind the glass doors of\r\nmahogany buffets; large screens, in corners, covered with tense silk\r\nand embroidered with mandarins and dragons. These things were scattered\r\nall over the house, and they gave Eugenia a pretext for a complete\r\ndomiciliary visit. She liked it, she enjoyed it; she thought it a very\r\nnice place. It had a mixture of the homely and the liberal, and though\r\nit was almost a museum, the large, little-used rooms were as fresh and\r\nclean as a well-kept dairy. Lizzie Acton told her that she dusted all\r\nthe pagodas and other curiosities every day with her own hands; and the\r\nBaroness answered that she was evidently a household fairy. Lizzie had\r\nnot at all the look of a young lady who dusted things; she wore such\r\npretty dresses and had such delicate fingers that it was difficult to\r\nimagine her immersed in sordid cares. She came to meet Madame M\xc3\xbcnster\r\non her arrival, but she said nothing, or almost nothing, and the\r\nBaroness again reflected\xe2\x80\x94she had had occasion to do so before\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nAmerican girls had no manners. She disliked this little American girl,\r\nand she was quite prepared to learn that she had failed to commend\r\nherself to Miss Acton. Lizzie struck her as positive and explicit\r\nalmost to pertness; and the idea of her combining the apparent\r\nincongruities of a taste for housework and the wearing of fresh,\r\nParisian-looking dresses suggested the possession of a dangerous\r\nenergy. It was a source of irritation to the Baroness that in this\r\ncountry it should seem to matter whether a little girl were a trifle\r\nless or a trifle more of a nonentity; for Eugenia had hitherto been\r\nconscious of no moral pressure as regards the appreciation of\r\ndiminutive virgins. It was perhaps an indication of Lizzie\xe2\x80\x99s pertness\r\nthat she very soon retired and left the Baroness on her brother\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhands. Acton talked a great deal about his _chinoiseries_; he knew a\r\ngood deal about porcelain and bric-\xc3\xa0-brac. The Baroness, in her\r\nprogress through the house, made, as it were, a great many stations.\r\nShe sat down everywhere, confessed to being a little tired, and asked\r\nabout the various objects with a curious mixture of alertness and\r\ninattention. If there had been anyone to say it to she would have\r\ndeclared that she was positively in love with her host; but she could\r\nhardly make this declaration\xe2\x80\x94even in the strictest confidence\xe2\x80\x94to Acton\r\nhimself. It gave her, nevertheless, a pleasure that had some of the\r\ncharm of unwontedness to feel, with that admirable keenness with which\r\nshe was capable of feeling things, that he had a disposition without\r\nany edges; that even his humorous irony always expanded toward the\r\npoint. One\xe2\x80\x99s impression of his honesty was almost like carrying a bunch\r\nof flowers; the perfume was most agreeable, but they were occasionally\r\nan inconvenience. One could trust him, at any rate, round all the\r\ncorners of the world; and, withal, he was not absolutely simple, which\r\nwould have been excess; he was only relatively simple, which was quite\r\nenough for the Baroness.\r\n\r\nLizzie reappeared to say that her mother would now be happy to receive\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster; and the Baroness followed her to Mrs. Acton\xe2\x80\x99s\r\napartment. Eugenia reflected, as she went, that it was not the\r\naffectation of impertinence that made her dislike this young lady, for\r\non that ground she could easily have beaten her. It was not an\r\naspiration on the girl\xe2\x80\x99s part to rivalry, but a kind of laughing,\r\nchildishly-mocking indifference to the results of comparison. Mrs.\r\nActon was an emaciated, sweet-faced woman of five and fifty, sitting\r\nwith pillows behind her, and looking out on a clump of hemlocks. She\r\nwas very modest, very timid, and very ill; she made Eugenia feel\r\ngrateful that she herself was not like that\xe2\x80\x94neither so ill, nor,\r\npossibly, so modest. On a chair, beside her, lay a volume of Emerson\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nEssays. It was a great occasion for poor Mrs. Acton, in her helpless\r\ncondition, to be confronted with a clever foreign lady, who had more\r\nmanner than any lady\xe2\x80\x94any dozen ladies\xe2\x80\x94that she had ever seen.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have heard a great deal about you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, softly, to the\r\nBaroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom your son, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d Eugenia asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has talked to me immensely of\r\nyou. Oh, he talks of you as you would like,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness declared; \xe2\x80\x9cas\r\nsuch a son _must_ talk of such a mother!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Acton sat gazing; this was part of Madame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9cmanner.\xe2\x80\x9d But\r\nRobert Acton was gazing too, in vivid consciousness that he had barely\r\nmentioned his mother to their brilliant guest. He never talked of this\r\nstill maternal presence,\xe2\x80\x94a presence refined to such delicacy that it\r\nhad almost resolved itself, with him, simply into the subjective\r\nemotion of gratitude. And Acton rarely talked of his emotions. The\r\nBaroness turned her smile toward him, and she instantly felt that she\r\nhad been observed to be fibbing. She had struck a false note. But who\r\nwere these people to whom such fibbing was not pleasing? If they were\r\nannoyed, the Baroness was equally so; and after the exchange of a few\r\ncivil inquiries and low-voiced responses she took leave of Mrs. Acton.\r\nShe begged Robert not to come home with her; she would get into the\r\ncarriage alone; she preferred that. This was imperious, and she thought\r\nhe looked disappointed. While she stood before the door with him\xe2\x80\x94the\r\ncarriage was turning in the gravel-walk\xe2\x80\x94this thought restored her\r\nserenity.\r\n\r\nWhen she had given him her hand in farewell she looked at him a moment.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have almost decided to dispatch that paper,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\nHe knew that she alluded to the document that she had called her\r\nrenunciation; and he assisted her into the carriage without saying\r\nanything. But just before the vehicle began to move he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\r\nwhen you have in fact dispatched it, I hope you will let me know!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII\r\n\r\n\r\nFelix Young finished Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s portrait, and he afterwards transferred\r\nto canvas the features of many members of that circle of which it may\r\nbe said that he had become for the time the pivot and the centre. I am\r\nafraid it must be confessed that he was a decidedly flattering painter,\r\nand that he imparted to his models a romantic grace which seemed easily\r\nand cheaply acquired by the payment of a hundred dollars to a young man\r\nwho made \xe2\x80\x9csitting\xe2\x80\x9d so entertaining. For Felix was paid for his\r\npictures, making, as he did, no secret of the fact that in guiding his\r\nsteps to the Western world affectionate curiosity had gone hand in hand\r\nwith a desire to better his condition. He took his uncle\xe2\x80\x99s portrait\r\nquite as if Mr. Wentworth had never averted himself from the\r\nexperiment; and as he compassed his end only by the exercise of gentle\r\nviolence, it is but fair to add that he allowed the old man to give him\r\nnothing but his time. He passed his arm into Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s one summer\r\nmorning\xe2\x80\x94very few arms indeed had ever passed into Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nled him across the garden and along the road into the studio which he\r\nhad extemporized in the little house among the apple trees. The grave\r\ngentleman felt himself more and more fascinated by his clever nephew,\r\nwhose fresh, demonstrative youth seemed a compendium of experiences so\r\nstrangely numerous. It appeared to him that Felix must know a great\r\ndeal; he would like to learn what he thought about some of those things\r\nas regards which his own conversation had always been formal, but his\r\nknowledge vague. Felix had a confident, gayly trenchant way of judging\r\nhuman actions which Mr. Wentworth grew little by little to envy; it\r\nseemed like criticism made easy. Forming an opinion\xe2\x80\x94say on a person\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nconduct\xe2\x80\x94was, with Mr. Wentworth, a good deal like fumbling in a lock\r\nwith a key chosen at hazard. He seemed to himself to go about the world\r\nwith a big bunch of these ineffectual instruments at his girdle. His\r\nnephew, on the other hand, with a single turn of the wrist, opened any\r\ndoor as adroitly as a horse-thief. He felt obliged to keep up the\r\nconvention that an uncle is always wiser than a nephew, even if he\r\ncould keep it up no otherwise than by listening in serious silence to\r\nFelix\xe2\x80\x99s quick, light, constant discourse. But there came a day when he\r\nlapsed from consistency and almost asked his nephew\xe2\x80\x99s advice.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you ever entertained the idea of settling in the United States?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe asked one morning, while Felix brilliantly plied his brush.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cexcuse me if your question makes me smile\r\na little. To begin with, I have never entertained an idea. Ideas often\r\nentertain _me_; but I am afraid I have never seriously made a plan. I\r\nknow what you are going to say; or rather, I know what you think, for I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t think you will say it\xe2\x80\x94that this is very frivolous and\r\nloose-minded on my part. So it is; but I am made like that; I take\r\nthings as they come, and somehow there is always some new thing to\r\nfollow the last. In the second place, I should never propose to\r\n_settle_. I can\xe2\x80\x99t settle, my dear uncle; I\xe2\x80\x99m not a settler. I know that\r\nis what strangers are supposed to do here; they always settle. But I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t\xe2\x80\x94to answer your question\xe2\x80\x94entertained that idea.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou intend to return to Europe and resume your irregular manner of\r\nlife?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth inquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t say I intend. But it\xe2\x80\x99s very likely I shall go back to Europe.\r\nAfter all, I am a European. I feel that, you know. It will depend a\r\ngood deal upon my sister. She\xe2\x80\x99s even more of a European than I; here,\r\nyou know, she\xe2\x80\x99s a picture out of her setting. And as for \xe2\x80\x98resuming,\xe2\x80\x99\r\ndear uncle, I really have never given up my irregular manner of life.\r\nWhat, for me, could be more irregular than this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThan what?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Wentworth, with his pale gravity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, than everything! Living in the midst of you, this way; this\r\ncharming, quiet, serious family life; fraternizing with Charlotte and\r\nGertrude; calling upon twenty young ladies and going out to walk with\r\nthem; sitting with you in the evening on the piazza and listening to\r\nthe crickets, and going to bed at ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour description is very animated,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth; \xe2\x80\x9cbut I see\r\nnothing improper in what you describe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither do I, dear uncle. It is extremely delightful; I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like\r\nit if it were improper. I assure you I don\xe2\x80\x99t like improper things;\r\nthough I dare say you think I do,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix went on, painting away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never accused you of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause, you see, at bottom I am a terrible\r\nPhilistine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA Philistine?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean, as one may say, a plain, God-fearing man.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth\r\nlooked at him reservedly, like a mystified sage, and Felix continued,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI trust I shall enjoy a venerable and venerated old age. I mean to\r\nlive long. I can hardly call that a plan, perhaps; but it\xe2\x80\x99s a keen\r\ndesire\xe2\x80\x94a rosy vision. I shall be a lively, perhaps even a frivolous old\r\nman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is natural,\xe2\x80\x9d said his uncle, sententiously, \xe2\x80\x9cthat one should desire\r\nto prolong an agreeable life. We have perhaps a selfish indisposition\r\nto bring our pleasure to a close. But I presume,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you\r\nexpect to marry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat too, dear uncle, is a hope, a desire, a vision,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. It\r\noccurred to him for an instant that this was possibly a preface to the\r\noffer of the hand of one of Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s admirable daughters. But in\r\nthe name of decent modesty and a proper sense of the hard realities of\r\nthis world, Felix banished the thought. His uncle was the incarnation\r\nof benevolence, certainly; but from that to accepting\xe2\x80\x94much more\r\npostulating\xe2\x80\x94the idea of a union between a young lady with a dowry\r\npresumptively brilliant and a penniless artist with no prospect of\r\nfame, there was a very long way. Felix had lately become conscious of a\r\nluxurious preference for the society\xe2\x80\x94if possible unshared with\r\nothers\xe2\x80\x94of Gertrude Wentworth; but he had relegated this young lady, for\r\nthe moment, to the coldly brilliant category of unattainable\r\npossessions. She was not the first woman for whom he had entertained an\r\nunpractical admiration. He had been in love with duchesses and\r\ncountesses, and he had made, once or twice, a perilously near approach\r\nto cynicism in declaring that the disinterestedness of women had been\r\noverrated. On the whole, he had tempered audacity with modesty; and it\r\nis but fair to him now to say explicitly that he would have been\r\nincapable of taking advantage of his present large allowance of\r\nfamiliarity to make love to the younger of his handsome cousins. Felix\r\nhad grown up among traditions in the light of which such a proceeding\r\nlooked like a grievous breach of hospitality. I have said that he was\r\nalways happy, and it may be counted among the present sources of his\r\nhappiness that he had as regards this matter of his relations with\r\nGertrude a deliciously good conscience. His own deportment seemed to\r\nhim suffused with the beauty of virtue\xe2\x80\x94a form of beauty that he admired\r\nwith the same vivacity with which he admired all other forms.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that if you marry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth presently, \xe2\x80\x9cit will\r\nconduce to your happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cSicurissimo!\xe2\x80\x9d_ Felix exclaimed; and then, arresting his brush, he\r\nlooked at his uncle with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is something I feel tempted to\r\nsay to you. May I risk it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth drew himself up a little. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very safe; I don\xe2\x80\x99t repeat\r\nthings.\xe2\x80\x9d But he hoped Felix would not risk too much.\r\n\r\nFelix was laughing at his answer.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s odd to hear you telling me how to be happy. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you\r\nknow yourself, dear uncle. Now, does that sound brutal?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man was silent a moment, and then, with a dry dignity that\r\nsuddenly touched his nephew: \xe2\x80\x9cWe may sometimes point out a road we are\r\nunable to follow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me you have had any sorrows,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nsuppose it, and I didn\xe2\x80\x99t mean to allude to them. I simply meant that\r\nyou all don\xe2\x80\x99t amuse yourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmuse ourselves? We are not children.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely not! You have reached the proper age. But you don\xe2\x80\x99t catch me at it again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth maintained for a while a silence more expressive than a\r\ndeep-drawn sigh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have no children,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t tell me,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cthat your charming young people are\r\na source of grief to you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of Charlotte.\xe2\x80\x9d And then, after a pause, Mr. Wentworth\r\ncontinued, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t speak of Gertrude. But I feel considerable anxiety\r\nabout Clifford. I will tell you another time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next time he gave Felix a sitting his nephew reminded him that he\r\nhad taken him into his confidence. \xe2\x80\x9cHow is Clifford today?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix\r\nasked. \xe2\x80\x9cHe has always seemed to me a young man of remarkable\r\ndiscretion. Indeed, he is only too discreet; he seems on his guard\r\nagainst me\xe2\x80\x94as if he thought me rather light company. The other day he\r\ntold his sister\xe2\x80\x94Gertrude repeated it to me\xe2\x80\x94that I was always laughing\r\nat him. If I laugh it is simply from the impulse to try and inspire him\r\nwith confidence. That is the only way I have.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClifford\xe2\x80\x99s situation is no laughing matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nis very peculiar, as I suppose you have guessed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you mean his love affair with his cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth stared, blushing a little. \xe2\x80\x9cI mean his absence from\r\ncollege. He has been suspended. We have decided not to speak of it\r\nunless we are asked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuspended?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix repeated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has been requested by the Harvard authorities to absent himself for\r\nsix months. Meanwhile he is studying with Mr. Brand. We think Mr. Brand\r\nwill help him; at least we hope so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat befell him at college?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHe was too fond of\r\npleasure? Mr. Brand certainly will not teach him any of those secrets!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe was too fond of something of which he should not have been fond. I\r\nsuppose it is considered a pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix gave his light laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear uncle, is there any doubt about\r\nits being a pleasure? _C\xe2\x80\x99est de son \xc3\xa2ge_, as they say in France.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should have said rather it was a vice of later life\xe2\x80\x94of disappointed\r\nold age.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix glanced at his uncle, with his lifted eyebrows, and then, \xe2\x80\x9cOf\r\nwhat are you speaking?\xe2\x80\x9d he demanded, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf the situation in which Clifford was found.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, he was found\xe2\x80\x94he was caught?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNecessarily, he was caught. Lizzie\r\nActon, then, would not be jealous of another woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI certainly hope not,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, with a vague sense of\r\njealousy being an even lower vice than a love of liquor.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe best thing for Clifford, then,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix propounded, \xe2\x80\x9cis to become\r\ninterested in some clever, charming woman.\xe2\x80\x9d And he paused in his\r\npainting, and, with his elbows on his knees, looked with bright\r\ncommunicativeness at his uncle. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, I believe greatly in the\r\ninfluence of women. The murmur of the air in the far off\r\ntree-tops had a strange distinctness; it was almost articulate. One\r\nafternoon the young man came out of his painting-room and passed the\r\nopen door of Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s little salon. He had evidently just presented the bouquet to the\r\nBaroness, whose fine eyes, as she glanced at him over the big roses and\r\ngeraniums, wore a conversational smile. That civilizing process to which he\r\nhad suggested that Clifford should be subjected appeared to have come\r\non of itself. The idea in prospect had seemed of\r\nthe happiest, but in operation it made him a trifle uneasy. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat if\r\nEugenia\xe2\x80\x94what if Eugenia\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he asked himself softly; the question dying\r\naway in his sense of Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s undetermined capacity. Acton could be left to play the part of Providence\r\nand interrupt\xe2\x80\x94if interruption were needed\xe2\x80\x94Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s entanglement with\r\nEugenia.\r\n\r\nFelix passed through the garden toward the house and toward a postern\r\ngate which opened upon a path leading across the fields, beside a\r\nlittle wood, to the lake. He stopped and looked up at the house; his\r\neyes rested more particularly upon a certain open window, on the shady\r\nside. Presently Gertrude appeared there, looking out into the summer\r\nlight. He took off his hat to her and bade her good-day; he remarked\r\nthat he was going to row across the pond, and begged that she would do\r\nhim the honor to accompany him. But she soon reappeared below\r\nin one of those quaint and charming Leghorn hats, tied with white satin\r\nbows, that were worn at that period; she also carried a green parasol.\r\nShe went with him to the edge of the lake, where a couple of boats were\r\nalways moored; they got into one of them, and Felix, with gentle\r\nstrokes, propelled it to the opposite shore.
