I wished simply to\r\nexplain to you about Charlotte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to marry Mr. Brand, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, gravely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd does your father wish it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery much.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd you don\xe2\x80\x99t like him\xe2\x80\x94you have refused him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to marry him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour father and sister think you ought to, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a long story,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cThey think there are good\r\nreasons. I can\xe2\x80\x99t explain it. They think I have obligations, and that I\r\nhave encouraged him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix smiled at her, as if she had been telling him an amusing story\r\nabout someone else. \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t tell you how this interests me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you don\xe2\x80\x99t recognize these reasons\xe2\x80\x94these obligations?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure; it is not easy.\xe2\x80\x9d And she picked up her parasol and\r\nturned away, as if to descend the slope.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me this,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix went on, going with her: \xe2\x80\x9care you likely to give\r\nin\xe2\x80\x94to let them persuade you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at him with the serious face that she had constantly\r\nworn, in opposition to his almost eager smile. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall never marry Mr.\r\nBrand,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined. And they slowly descended the hill together,\r\nsaying nothing till they reached the margin of the pond. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is your\r\nown affair,\xe2\x80\x9d he then resumed; \xe2\x80\x9cbut do you know, I am not altogether\r\nglad? If it were settled that you were to marry Mr. Brand I should take\r\na certain comfort in the arrangement. I should feel more free. I have\r\nno right to make love to you myself, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d And he paused, lightly\r\npressing his argument upon her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone whatever,\xe2\x80\x9d replied Gertrude quickly\xe2\x80\x94too quickly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour father would never hear of it; I haven\xe2\x80\x99t a penny. Mr. Brand, of\r\ncourse, has property of his own, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe he has some property; but that has nothing to do with it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith you, of course not; but with your father and sister it must have.\r\nSo, as I say, if this were settled, I should feel more at liberty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMore at liberty?\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease unfasten the boat.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix untwisted the rope and stood holding it. \xe2\x80\x9cI should be able to say\r\nthings to you that I can\xe2\x80\x99t give myself the pleasure of saying now,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nwent on. \xe2\x80\x9cI could tell you how much I admire you, without seeming to\r\npretend to that which I have no right to pretend to. I should make\r\nviolent love to you,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, laughing, \xe2\x80\x9cif I thought you were so\r\nplaced as not to be offended by it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou mean if I were engaged to another man? That is strange reasoning!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGertrude exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn that case you would not take me seriously.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI take everyone seriously,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. And without his help she\r\nstepped lightly into the boat.\r\n\r\nFelix took up the oars and sent it forward. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, this is what you have\r\nbeen thinking about? It seemed to me you had something on your mind. I\r\nwish very much,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would tell me some of these\r\nso-called reasons\xe2\x80\x94these obligations.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are not real reasons\xe2\x80\x94good reasons,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, looking at the\r\npink and yellow gleams in the water.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI can understand that! Because a handsome girl has had a spark of\r\ncoquetry, that is no reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean me, it\xe2\x80\x99s not that. I have not done that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is something that troubles you, at any rate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot so much as it used to,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude rejoined.\r\n\r\nHe looked at her, smiling always. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is not saying much, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d But\r\nshe only rested her eyes, very gravely, on the lighted water. She\r\nseemed to him to be trying to hide the signs of the trouble of which\r\nshe had just told him. Felix felt, at all times, much the same impulse\r\nto dissipate visible melancholy that a good housewife feels to brush\r\naway dust. There was something he wished to brush away now; suddenly he\r\nstopped rowing and poised his oars. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should Mr. Brand have\r\naddressed himself to you, and not to your sister?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sure\r\nshe would listen to him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude, in her family, was thought capable of a good deal of levity;\r\nbut her levity had never gone so far as this. It moved her greatly,\r\nhowever, to hear Felix say that he was sure of something; so that,\r\nraising her eyes toward him, she tried intently, for some moments, to\r\nconjure up this wonderful image of a love-affair between her own sister\r\nand her own suitor. We know that Gertrude had an imaginative mind; so\r\nthat it is not impossible that this effort should have been partially\r\nsuccessful. But she only murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cAh, Felix! ah, Felix!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t they marry? Try and make them marry!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTry and make them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTurn the tables on them. Then they will leave you alone. I will help\r\nyou as far as I can.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude\xe2\x80\x99s heart began to beat; she was greatly excited; she had never\r\nhad anything so interesting proposed to her before. Felix had begun to\r\nrow again, and he now sent the boat home with long strokes. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe\r\nshe _does_ care for him!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, after they had disembarked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course she does, and we will marry them off. It will make them\r\nhappy; it will make everyone happy. We shall have a wedding and I will\r\nwrite an epithalamium.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems as if it would make _me_ happy,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo get rid of Mr. Brand, eh? To recover your liberty?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude walked on. \xe2\x80\x9cTo see my sister married to so good a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix gave his light laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou always put things on those grounds;\r\nyou will never say anything for yourself. You are all so afraid, here,\r\nof being selfish. I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you know how,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cLet me show\r\nyou! It will make me happy for myself, and for just the reverse of what\r\nI told you a while ago. After that, when I make love to you, you will\r\nhave to think I mean it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall never think you mean anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are too\r\nfantastic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cthat\xe2\x80\x99s a license to say everything! Gertrude, I\r\nadore you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII\r\n\r\n\r\nCharlotte and Mr. Brand had not returned when they reached the house;\r\nbut the Baroness had come to tea, and Robert Acton also, who now\r\nregularly asked for a place at this generous repast or made his\r\nappearance later in the evening. Clifford Wentworth, with his juvenile\r\ngrowl, remarked upon it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are always coming to tea nowadays, Robert,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI should\r\nthink you had drunk enough tea in China.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince when is Mr. Acton more frequent?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince you came,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems as if you were a kind of\r\nattraction.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose I am a curiosity,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cGive me time and I\r\nwill make you a salon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would fall to pieces after you go!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t talk about her going, in that familiar way,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\nmakes me feel gloomy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth glanced at his son, and taking note of these words,\r\nwondered if Felix had been teaching him, according to the programme he\r\nhad sketched out, to make love to the wife of a German prince.\r\n\r\nCharlotte came in late with Mr. Brand; but Gertrude, to whom, at least,\r\nFelix had taught something, looked in vain, in her face, for the traces\r\nof a guilty passion. Mr. Brand sat down by Gertrude, and she presently\r\nasked him why they had not crossed the pond to join Felix and herself.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is cruel of you to ask me that,\xe2\x80\x9d he answered, very softly. He had a\r\nlarge morsel of cake before him; but he fingered it without eating it.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sometimes think you are growing cruel,\xe2\x80\x9d he added.\r\n\r\nGertrude said nothing; she was afraid to speak. There was a kind of\r\nrage in her heart; she felt as if she could easily persuade herself\r\nthat she was persecuted. She said to herself that it was quite right\r\nthat she should not allow him to make her believe she was wrong. She\r\nthought of what Felix had said to her; she wished indeed Mr. Brand\r\nwould marry Charlotte. She looked away from him and spoke no more. Mr.\r\nBrand ended by eating his cake, while Felix sat opposite, describing to\r\nMr. Wentworth the students\xe2\x80\x99 duels at Heidelberg. After tea they all\r\ndispersed themselves, as usual, upon the piazza and in the garden; and\r\nMr. Brand drew near to Gertrude again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t come to you this afternoon because you were not alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nbegan; \xe2\x80\x9cbecause you were with a newer friend.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFelix? He is an old friend by this time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand looked at the ground for some moments. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought I was\r\nprepared to hear you speak in that way,\xe2\x80\x9d he resumed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I find it\r\nvery painful.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see what else I can say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand walked beside her for a while in silence; Gertrude wished he\r\nwould go away. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is certainly very accomplished. But I think I ought\r\nto advise you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo advise me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think I know your nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think you don\xe2\x80\x99t,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, with a soft laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou make yourself out worse than you are\xe2\x80\x94to please him,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand\r\nsaid, gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWorse\xe2\x80\x94to please him? What do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gertrude, stopping.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand stopped also, and with the same soft straight-forwardness,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care for the things you care for\xe2\x80\x94the great questions of\r\nlife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude, with her eyes on his, shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care for the\r\ngreat questions of life. They are much beyond me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a time when you didn\xe2\x80\x99t say that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Gertrude, \xe2\x80\x9cI think you made me talk a great deal of\r\nnonsense. And it depends,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9cupon what you call the great\r\nquestions of life. There are some things I care for.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre they the things you talk about with your cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should not say things to me against my cousin, Mr. Brand,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nGertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is dishonorable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe listened to this respectfully; then he answered, with a little\r\nvibration of the voice, \xe2\x80\x9cI should be very sorry to do anything\r\ndishonorable. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t see why it is dishonorable to say that your\r\ncousin is frivolous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo and say it to himself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think he would admit it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is the tone he would\r\ntake. He would not be ashamed of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I am not ashamed of it!\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude declared. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is probably\r\nwhat I like him for. I am frivolous myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are trying, as I said just now, to lower yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am trying for once to be natural!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Gertrude passionately. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nhave been pretending, all my life; I have been dishonest; it is you\r\nthat have made me so!\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand stood gazing at her, and she went on,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I be frivolous, if I want? One has a right to be\r\nfrivolous, if it\xe2\x80\x99s one\xe2\x80\x99s nature. No, I don\xe2\x80\x99t care for the great\r\nquestions. I care for pleasure\xe2\x80\x94for amusement. Perhaps I am fond of\r\nwicked things; it is very possible!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand remained staring; he was even a little pale, as if he had\r\nbeen frightened. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t think you know what you are saying!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nexclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps not. Perhaps I am talking nonsense. But it is only with you\r\nthat I talk nonsense. I never do so with my cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will speak to you again, when you are less excited,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am always excited when you speak to me. I must tell you that\xe2\x80\x94even if\r\nit prevents you altogether, in future. Your speaking to me irritates\r\nme. With my cousin it is very different. That seems quiet and natural.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked at her, and then he looked away, with a kind of helpless\r\ndistress, at the dusky garden and the faint summer stars. After which,\r\nsuddenly turning back, \xe2\x80\x9cGertrude, Gertrude!\xe2\x80\x9d he softly groaned. \xe2\x80\x9cAm I\r\nreally losing you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe was touched\xe2\x80\x94she was pained; but it had already occurred to her that\r\nshe might do something better than say so. It would not have alleviated\r\nher companion\xe2\x80\x99s distress to perceive, just then, whence she had\r\nsympathetically borrowed this ingenuity. \xe2\x80\x9cI am not sorry for you,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nGertrude said; \xe2\x80\x9cfor in paying so much attention to me you are following\r\na shadow\xe2\x80\x94you are wasting something precious. There is something else\r\nyou might have that you don\xe2\x80\x99t look at\xe2\x80\x94something better than I am. That\r\nis a reality!\xe2\x80\x9d And then, with intention, she looked at him and tried to\r\nsmile a little. He thought this smile of hers very strange; but she\r\nturned away and left him.\r\n\r\nShe wandered about alone in the garden wondering what Mr. Brand would\r\nmake of her words, which it had been a singular pleasure for her to\r\nutter. Shortly after, passing in front of the house, she saw at a\r\ndistance two persons standing near the garden gate. It was Mr. Brand\r\ngoing away and bidding good-night to Charlotte, who had walked down\r\nwith him from the house. Gertrude saw that the parting was prolonged.\r\nThen she turned her back upon it. She had not gone very far, however,\r\nwhen she heard her sister slowly following her. She neither turned\r\nround nor waited for her; she knew what Charlotte was going to say.\r\nCharlotte, who at last overtook her, in fact presently began; she had\r\npassed her arm into Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill you listen to me, dear, if I say something very particular?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what you are going to say,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Brand feels\r\nvery badly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Gertrude, how can you treat him so?\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte demanded. And as\r\nher sister made no answer she added, \xe2\x80\x9cAfter all he has done for you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat has he done for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wonder you can ask, Gertrude. He has helped you so. You told me so\r\nyourself, a great many times. You told me that he helped you to\r\nstruggle with your\xe2\x80\x94your peculiarities. You told me that he had taught\r\nyou how to govern your temper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFor a moment Gertrude said nothing. Then, \xe2\x80\x9cWas my temper very bad?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not accusing you, Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat are you doing, then?\xe2\x80\x9d her sister demanded, with a short laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am pleading for Mr. Brand\xe2\x80\x94reminding you of all you owe him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have given it all back,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, still with her little laugh.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe can take back the virtue he imparted! I want to be wicked again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHer sister made her stop in the path, and fixed upon her, in the\r\ndarkness, a sweet, reproachful gaze. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you talk this way I shall\r\nalmost believe it. Think of all we owe Mr. Brand. Think of how he has\r\nalways expected something of you. Think how much he has been to us.\r\nThink of his beautiful influence upon Clifford.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very good,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, looking at her sister. \xe2\x80\x9cI know he is\r\nvery good. But he shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t speak against Felix.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFelix is good,\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte answered, softly but promptly. \xe2\x80\x9cFelix is\r\nvery wonderful. Only he is so different. Mr. Brand is much nearer to\r\nus. I should never think of going to Felix with a trouble\xe2\x80\x94with a\r\nquestion. Mr. Brand is much more to us, Gertrude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is very\xe2\x80\x94very good,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is more to you; yes,\r\nmuch more. Charlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d she added suddenly, \xe2\x80\x9cyou are in love with him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Gertrude!\xe2\x80\x9d cried poor Charlotte; and her sister saw her blushing\r\nin the darkness.\r\n\r\nGertrude put her arm round her. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish he would marry you!\xe2\x80\x9d she went\r\non.\r\n\r\nCharlotte shook herself free. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must not say such things!\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nexclaimed, beneath her breath.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou like him more than you say, and he likes you more than he knows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is very cruel of you!\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte Wentworth murmured.\r\n\r\nBut if it was cruel Gertrude continued pitiless. \xe2\x80\x9cNot if it\xe2\x80\x99s true,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe answered. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish he would marry you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease don\xe2\x80\x99t say that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI mean to tell him so!\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, Gertrude, Gertrude!\xe2\x80\x9d her sister almost moaned.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, if he speaks to me again about myself. I will say, \xe2\x80\x98Why don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nmarry Charlotte? She\xe2\x80\x99s a thousand times better than I.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou _are_ wicked; you _are_ changed!\xe2\x80\x9d cried her sister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you don\xe2\x80\x99t like it you can prevent it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cYou can\r\nprevent it by keeping him from speaking to me!\xe2\x80\x9d And with this she\r\nwalked away, very conscious of what she had done; measuring it and\r\nfinding a certain joy and a quickened sense of freedom in it.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth was rather wide of the mark in suspecting that Clifford\r\nhad begun to pay unscrupulous compliments to his brilliant cousin; for\r\nthe young man had really more scruples than he received credit for in\r\nhis family. He had a certain transparent shamefacedness which was in\r\nitself a proof that he was not at his ease in dissipation. His\r\ncollegiate peccadilloes had aroused a domestic murmur as disagreeable\r\nto the young man as the creaking of his boots would have been to a\r\nhouse-breaker. Only, as the house-breaker would have simplified matters\r\nby removing his _chaussures_, it had seemed to Clifford that the\r\nshortest cut to comfortable relations with people\xe2\x80\x94relations which\r\nshould make him cease to think that when they spoke to him they meant\r\nsomething improving\xe2\x80\x94was to renounce all ambition toward a nefarious\r\ndevelopment. And, in fact, Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s ambition took the most\r\ncommendable form. He thought of himself in the future as the well-known\r\nand much-liked Mr. Wentworth, of Boston, who should, in the natural\r\ncourse of prosperity, have married his pretty cousin, Lizzie Acton;\r\nshould live in a wide-fronted house, in view of the Common; and should\r\ndrive, behind a light wagon, over the damp autumn roads, a pair of\r\nbeautifully matched sorrel horses. Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s vision of the coming\r\nyears was very simple; its most definite features were this element of\r\nfamiliar matrimony and the duplication of his resources for trotting.\r\nHe had not yet asked his cousin to marry him; but he meant to do so as\r\nsoon as he had taken his degree. Lizzie was serenely conscious of his\r\nintention, and she had made up her mind that he would improve. Her\r\nbrother, who was very fond of this light, quick, competent little\r\nLizzie, saw on his side no reason to interpose. It seemed to him a\r\ngraceful social law that Clifford and his sister should become engaged;\r\nhe himself was not engaged, but everyone else, fortunately, was not\r\nsuch a fool as he. He was fond of Clifford, as well, and had his own\r\nway\xe2\x80\x94of which it must be confessed he was a little ashamed\xe2\x80\x94of looking at\r\nthose aberrations which had led to the young man\xe2\x80\x99s compulsory\r\nretirement from the neighboring seat of learning. Acton had seen the\r\nworld, as he said to himself; he had been to China and had knocked\r\nabout among men. He had learned the essential difference between a nice\r\nyoung fellow and a mean young fellow, and was satisfied that there was\r\nno harm in Clifford. He believed\xe2\x80\x94although it must be added that he had\r\nnot quite the courage to declare it\xe2\x80\x94in the doctrine of wild oats, and\r\nthought it a useful preventive of superfluous fears. If Mr. Wentworth\r\nand Charlotte and Mr. Brand would only apply it in Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s case,\r\nthey would be happier; and Acton thought it a pity they should not be\r\nhappier. They took the boy\xe2\x80\x99s misdemeanors too much to heart; they\r\ntalked to him too solemnly; they frightened and bewildered him. Of\r\ncourse there was the great standard of morality, which forbade that a\r\nman should get tipsy, play at billiards for money, or cultivate his\r\nsensual consciousness; but what fear was there that poor Clifford was\r\ngoing to run a tilt at any great standard? It had, however, never\r\noccurred to Acton to dedicate the Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster to the redemption of\r\na refractory collegian. The instrument, here, would have seemed to him\r\nquite too complex for the operation. Felix, on the other hand, had\r\nspoken in obedience to the belief that the more charming a woman is the\r\nmore numerous, literally, are her definite social uses.\r\n\r\nEugenia herself, as we know, had plenty of leisure to enumerate her\r\nuses. As I have had the honor of intimating, she had come four thousand\r\nmiles to seek her fortune; and it is not to be supposed that after this\r\ngreat effort she could neglect any apparent aid to advancement. It is\r\nmy misfortune that in attempting to describe in a short compass the\r\ndeportment of this remarkable woman I am obliged to express things\r\nrather brutally. I feel this to be the case, for instance, when I say\r\nthat she had primarily detected such an aid to advancement in the\r\nperson of Robert Acton, but that she had afterwards remembered that a\r\nprudent archer has always a second bowstring. Eugenia was a woman of\r\nfinely-mingled motive, and her intentions were never sensibly gross.\r\nShe had a sort of aesthetic ideal for Clifford which seemed to her a\r\ndisinterested reason for taking him in hand. It was very well for a\r\nfresh-colored young gentleman to be ingenuous; but Clifford, really,\r\nwas crude. With such a pretty face he ought to have prettier manners.\r\nShe would teach him that, with a beautiful name, the expectation of a\r\nlarge property, and, as they said in Europe, a social position, an only\r\nson should know how to carry himself.\r\n\r\nOnce Clifford had begun to come and see her by himself and for himself,\r\nhe came very often. He hardly knew why he should come; he saw her\r\nalmost every evening at his father\xe2\x80\x99s house; he had nothing particular\r\nto say to her. She was not a young girl, and fellows of his age called\r\nonly upon young girls. He exaggerated her age; she seemed to him an old\r\nwoman; it was happy that the Baroness, with all her intelligence, was\r\nincapable of guessing this. But gradually it struck Clifford that\r\nvisiting old women might be, if not a natural, at least, as they say of\r\nsome articles of diet, an acquired taste. The Baroness was certainly a\r\nvery amusing old woman; she talked to him as no lady\xe2\x80\x94and indeed no\r\ngentleman\xe2\x80\x94had ever talked to him before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou should go to Europe and make the tour,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to him one\r\nafternoon. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course, on leaving college you will go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford declared. \xe2\x80\x9cI know some fellows who have\r\nbeen to Europe. They say you can have better fun here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat depends. It depends upon your idea of fun. Your friends probably\r\nwere not introduced.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIntroduced?\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey had no opportunity of going into society; they formed no\r\n_relations_.\xe2\x80\x9d This was one of a certain number of words that the\r\nBaroness often pronounced in the French manner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey went to a ball, in Paris; I know that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there are balls and balls; especially in Paris. No, you must go,\r\nyou know; it is not a thing from which you can dispense yourself. You\r\nneed it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m very well,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99m not sick.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t mean for your health, my poor child. I mean for your manners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI haven\xe2\x80\x99t got any manners!\xe2\x80\x9d growled Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrecisely. You don\xe2\x80\x99t mind my assenting to that, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the\r\nBaroness with a smile. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must go to Europe and get a few. You can\r\nget them better there. It is a pity you might not have come while I was\r\nliving in\xe2\x80\x94in Germany. I would have introduced you; I had a charming\r\nlittle circle. You would perhaps have been rather young; but the\r\nyounger one begins, I think, the better. Now, at any rate, you have no\r\ntime to lose, and when I return you must immediately come to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this, to Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s apprehension, was a great mixture\xe2\x80\x94his beginning\r\nyoung, Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s return to Europe, his being introduced to her charming\r\nlittle circle. What was he to begin, and what was her little circle?\r\nHis ideas about her marriage had a good deal of vagueness; but they\r\nwere in so far definite as that he felt it to be a matter not to be\r\nfreely mentioned. He sat and looked all round the room; he supposed she\r\nwas alluding in some way to her marriage.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to go to Germany,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; it seemed to him the most\r\nconvenient thing to say.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him a while, smiling with her lips, but not with her\r\neyes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have scruples?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cScruples?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou young people, here, are very singular; one doesn\xe2\x80\x99t know where to\r\nexpect you. When you are not extremely improper you are so terribly\r\nproper. I dare say you think that, owing to my irregular marriage, I\r\nlive with loose people. You were never more mistaken. I have been all\r\nthe more particular.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, honestly distressed. \xe2\x80\x9cI never thought such a\r\nthing as that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you very sure? I am convinced that your father does, and your\r\nsisters. They say to each other that here I am on my good behavior, but\r\nthat over there\xe2\x80\x94married by the left hand\xe2\x80\x94I associate with light women.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clifford, energetically, \xe2\x80\x9cthey don\xe2\x80\x99t say such things as\r\nthat to each other!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf they think them they had better say them,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness rejoined.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen they can be contradicted. Please contradict that whenever you\r\nhear it, and don\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid of coming to see me on account of the\r\ncompany I keep. I have the honor of knowing more distinguished men, my\r\npoor child, than you are likely to see in a life-time. I see very few\r\nwomen; but those are women of rank. So, my dear young Puritan, you\r\nneedn\xe2\x80\x99t be afraid. I am not in the least one of those who think that\r\nthe society of women who have lost their place in the _vrai monde_ is\r\nnecessary to form a young man. I have never taken that tone. I have\r\nkept my place myself, and I think we are a much better school than the\r\nothers. Trust me, Clifford, and I will prove that to you,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness\r\ncontinued, while she made the agreeable reflection that she could not,\r\nat least, be accused of perverting her young kinsman. \xe2\x80\x9cSo if you ever\r\nfall among thieves don\xe2\x80\x99t go about saying I sent you to them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford thought it so comical that he should know\xe2\x80\x94in spite of her\r\nfigurative language\xe2\x80\x94what she meant, and that she should mean what he\r\nknew, that he could hardly help laughing a little, although he tried\r\nhard. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, no! oh, no!\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLaugh out, laugh out, if I amuse you!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cI am here\r\nfor that!\xe2\x80\x9d And Clifford thought her a very amusing person indeed. \xe2\x80\x9cBut\r\nremember,\xe2\x80\x9d she said on this occasion, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are coming\xe2\x80\x94next year\xe2\x80\x94to\r\npay me a visit over there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAbout a week afterwards she said to him, point-blank, \xe2\x80\x9cAre you\r\nseriously making love to your little cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeriously making love\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94these words, on Madame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s lips, had to\r\nClifford\xe2\x80\x99s sense a portentous and embarrassing sound; he hesitated\r\nabout assenting, lest he should commit himself to more than he\r\nunderstood. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say it if I was!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you say it?\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness demanded. \xe2\x80\x9cThose things ought\r\nto be known.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care whether it is known or not,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t want people looking at me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA young man of your importance ought to learn to bear observation\xe2\x80\x94to\r\ncarry himself as if he were quite indifferent to it. I won\xe2\x80\x99t say,\r\nexactly, unconscious,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness explained. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, he must seem to\r\nknow he is observed, and to think it natural he should be; but he must\r\nappear perfectly used to it. Now you haven\xe2\x80\x99t that, Clifford; you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t that at all. You must have that, you know. Don\xe2\x80\x99t tell me you\r\nare not a young man of importance,\xe2\x80\x9d Eugenia added. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t say anything\r\nso flat as that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, you don\xe2\x80\x99t catch me saying that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you must come to Germany,\xe2\x80\x9d Madame M\xc3\xbcnster continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI will show\r\nyou how people can be talked about, and yet not seem to know it. You\r\nwill be talked about, of course, with me; it will be said you are my\r\nlover. I will show you how little one may mind that\xe2\x80\x94how little I shall\r\nmind it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford sat staring, blushing and laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall mind it a good\r\ndeal!\xe2\x80\x9d he declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, not too much, you know; that would be uncivil. But I give you\r\nleave to mind it a little; especially if you have a passion for Miss\r\nActon. _Voyons_; as regards that, you either have or you have not. It\r\nis very simple to say it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see why you want to know,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou ought to want me to know. If one is arranging a marriage, one\r\ntells one\xe2\x80\x99s friends.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I\xe2\x80\x99m not arranging anything,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou don\xe2\x80\x99t intend to marry your cousin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I expect I shall do as I choose!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness leaned her head upon the back of her chair and closed her\r\neyes, as if she were tired. Then opening them again, \xe2\x80\x9cYour cousin is\r\nvery charming!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is the prettiest girl in this place,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford rejoined.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98In this place\xe2\x80\x99 is saying little; she would be charming anywhere. I am\r\nafraid you are entangled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, no, I\xe2\x80\x99m not entangled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you engaged? At your age that is the same thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford looked at the Baroness with some audacity. \xe2\x80\x9cWill you tell no\r\none?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it\xe2\x80\x99s as sacred as that\xe2\x80\x94no.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, then\xe2\x80\x94we are not!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s the great secret\xe2\x80\x94that you are not, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Baroness,\r\nwith a quick laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very glad to hear it. You are altogether too\r\nyoung. A young man in your position must choose and compare; he must\r\nsee the world first. Depend upon it,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9cyou should not settle\r\nthat matter before you have come abroad and paid me that visit. There\r\nare several things I should like to call your attention to first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I am rather afraid of that visit,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to\r\nme it will be rather like going to school again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness looked at him a moment.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear child,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cthere is no agreeable man who has not, at\r\nsome moment, been to school to a clever woman\xe2\x80\x94probably a little older\r\nthan himself. And you must be thankful when you get your instructions\r\ngratis. With me you would get it gratis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next day Clifford told Lizzie Acton that the Baroness thought her\r\nthe most charming girl she had ever seen.\r\n\r\nLizzie shook her head. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, she doesn\xe2\x80\x99t!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you think everything she says,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Clifford, \xe2\x80\x9cis to be taken the\r\nopposite way?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that is!\xe2\x80\x9d said Lizzie.\r\n\r\nClifford was going to remark that in this case the Baroness must desire\r\ngreatly to bring about a marriage between Mr. Clifford Wentworth and\r\nMiss Elizabeth Acton; but he resolved, on the whole, to suppress this\r\nobservation.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX\r\n\r\n\r\nIt seemed to Robert Acton, after Eugenia had come to his house, that\r\nsomething had passed between them which made them a good deal more\r\nintimate. It was hard to say exactly what, except her telling him that\r\nshe had taken her resolution with regard to the Prince Adolf; for\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s visit had made no difference in their relations. He\r\ncame to see her very often; but he had come to see her very often\r\nbefore. It was agreeable to him to find himself in her little\r\ndrawing-room; but this was not a new discovery. There was a change,\r\nhowever, in this sense: that if the Baroness had been a great deal in\r\nActon\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts before, she was now never out of them. From the first\r\nshe had been personally fascinating; but the fascination now had become\r\nintellectual as well. He was constantly pondering her words and\r\nmotions; they were as interesting as the factors in an algebraic\r\nproblem. This is saying a good deal; for Acton was extremely fond of\r\nmathematics. He asked himself whether it could be that he was in love\r\nwith her, and then hoped he was not; hoped it not so much for his own\r\nsake as for that of the amatory passion itself. If this was love, love\r\nhad been overrated. Love was a poetic impulse, and his own state of\r\nfeeling with regard to the Baroness was largely characterized by that\r\neminently prosaic sentiment\xe2\x80\x94curiosity. It was true, as Acton with his\r\nquietly cogitative habit observed to himself, that curiosity, pushed to\r\na given point, might become a romantic passion; and he certainly\r\nthought enough about this charming woman to make him restless and even\r\na little melancholy. It puzzled and vexed him at times to feel that he\r\nwas not more ardent. He was not in the least bent upon remaining a\r\nbachelor. In his younger years he had been\xe2\x80\x94or he had tried to be\xe2\x80\x94of the\r\nopinion that it would be a good deal \xe2\x80\x9cjollier\xe2\x80\x9d not to marry, and he had\r\nflattered himself that his single condition was something of a citadel.\r\nIt was a citadel, at all events, of which he had long since leveled the\r\noutworks. He had removed the guns from the ramparts; he had lowered the\r\ndraw-bridge across the moat. The draw-bridge had swayed lightly under\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s step; why should he not cause it to be raised again,\r\nso that she might be kept prisoner? He had an idea that she would\r\nbecome\xe2\x80\x94in time at least, and on learning the conveniences of the place\r\nfor making a lady comfortable\xe2\x80\x94a tolerably patient captive. But the\r\ndraw-bridge was never raised, and Acton\xe2\x80\x99s brilliant visitor was as free\r\nto depart as she had been to come. It was part of his curiosity to know\r\nwhy the deuce so susceptible a man was _not_ in love with so charming a\r\nwoman. If her various graces were, as I have said, the factors in an\r\nalgebraic problem, the answer to this question was the indispensable\r\nunknown quantity. The pursuit of the unknown quantity was extremely\r\nabsorbing; for the present it taxed all Acton\xe2\x80\x99s faculties.\r\n\r\nToward the middle of August he was obliged to leave home for some days;\r\nan old friend, with whom he had been associated in China, had begged\r\nhim to come to Newport, where he lay extremely ill. His friend got\r\nbetter, and at the end of a week Acton was released. I use the word\r\n\xe2\x80\x9creleased\xe2\x80\x9d advisedly; for in spite of his attachment to his Chinese\r\ncomrade he had been but a half-hearted visitor. He felt as if he had\r\nbeen called away from the theatre during the progress of a remarkably\r\ninteresting drama. The curtain was up all this time, and he was losing\r\nthe fourth act; that fourth act which would have been so essential to a\r\njust appreciation of the fifth. In other words, he was thinking about\r\nthe Baroness, who, seen at this distance, seemed a truly brilliant\r\nfigure. He saw at Newport a great many pretty women, who certainly were\r\nfigures as brilliant as beautiful light dresses could make them; but\r\nthough they talked a great deal\xe2\x80\x94and the Baroness\xe2\x80\x99s strong point was\r\nperhaps also her conversation\xe2\x80\x94Madame M\xc3\xbcnster appeared to lose nothing\r\nby the comparison. He wished she had come to Newport too. Would it not\r\nbe possible to make up, as they said, a party for visiting the famous\r\nwatering-place and invite Eugenia to join it? It was true that the\r\ncomplete satisfaction would be to spend a fortnight at Newport with\r\nEugenia alone. It would be a great pleasure to see her, in society,\r\ncarry everything before her, as he was sure she would do. When Acton\r\ncaught himself thinking these thoughts he began to walk up and down,\r\nwith his hands in his pockets, frowning a little and looking at the\r\nfloor. What did it prove\xe2\x80\x94for it certainly proved something\xe2\x80\x94this lively\r\ndisposition to be \xe2\x80\x9coff\xe2\x80\x9d somewhere with Madame M\xc3\xbcnster, away from all\r\nthe rest of them? Such a vision, certainly, seemed a refined\r\nimplication of matrimony, after the Baroness should have formally got\r\nrid of her informal husband. At any rate, Acton, with his\r\ncharacteristic discretion, forbore to give expression to whatever else\r\nit might imply, and the narrator of these incidents is not obliged to\r\nbe more definite.\r\n\r\nHe returned home rapidly, and, arriving in the afternoon, lost as\r\nlittle time as possible in joining the familiar circle at Mr.\r\nWentworth\xe2\x80\x99s. On reaching the house, however, he found the piazzas\r\nempty. The doors and windows were open, and their emptiness was made\r\nclear by the shafts of lamp-light from the parlors. Entering the house,\r\nhe found Mr. Wentworth sitting alone in one of these apartments,\r\nengaged in the perusal of the _North American Review_. After they had\r\nexchanged greetings and his cousin had made discreet inquiry about his\r\njourney, Acton asked what had become of Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s companions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are scattered about, amusing themselves as usual,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old\r\nman. \xe2\x80\x9cI saw Charlotte, a short time since, seated, with Mr. Brand, upon\r\nthe piazza. They were conversing with their customary animation. I\r\nsuppose they have joined her sister, who, for the hundredth time, was\r\ndoing the honors of the garden to her foreign cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you mean Felix,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. And on Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nassenting, he said, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd the others?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour sister has not come this evening. You must have seen her at\r\nhome,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. I proposed to her to come. She declined.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLizzie, I suppose, was expecting a visitor,\xe2\x80\x9d said the old man, with a\r\nkind of solemn slyness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf she was expecting Clifford, he had not turned up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth, at this intelligence, closed the _North American Review_\r\nand remarked that he had understood Clifford to say that he was going\r\nto see his cousin. Privately, he reflected that if Lizzie Acton had had\r\nno news of his son, Clifford must have gone to Boston for the evening:\r\nan unnatural course of a summer night, especially when accompanied with\r\ndisingenuous representations.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must remember that he has two cousins,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, laughing. And\r\nthen, coming to the point, \xe2\x80\x9cIf Lizzie is not here,\xe2\x80\x9d he added, \xe2\x80\x9cneither\r\napparently is the Baroness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth stared a moment, and remembered that queer proposition of\r\nFelix\xe2\x80\x99s. For a moment he did not know whether it was not to be wished\r\nthat Clifford, after all, might have gone to Boston. \xe2\x80\x9cThe Baroness has\r\nnot honored us tonight,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has not come over for three\r\ndays.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs she ill?\xe2\x80\x9d Acton asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo; I have been to see her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter with her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, \xe2\x80\x9cI infer she has tired of us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon pretended to sit down, but he was restless; he found it\r\nimpossible to talk with Mr. Wentworth. At the end of ten minutes he\r\ntook up his hat and said that he thought he would \xe2\x80\x9cgo off.\xe2\x80\x9d It was very\r\nlate; it was ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock.\r\n\r\nHis quiet-faced kinsman looked at him a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cAre you going home?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nhe asked.\r\n\r\nActon hesitated, and then answered that he had proposed to go over and\r\ntake a look at the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, you are honest, at least,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, sadly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo are you, if you come to that!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Acton, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\r\nshouldn\xe2\x80\x99t I be honest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man opened the _North American_ again, and read a few lines.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf we have ever had any virtue among us, we had better keep hold of it\r\nnow,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. He was not quoting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have a Baroness among us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s what we must keep\r\nhold of!\xe2\x80\x9d He was too impatient to see Madame M\xc3\xbcnster again to wonder\r\nwhat Mr. Wentworth was talking about. Nevertheless, after he had passed\r\nout of the house and traversed the garden and the little piece of road\r\nthat separated him from Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s provisional residence, he stopped a\r\nmoment outside. He stood in her little garden; the long window of her\r\nparlor was open, and he could see the white curtains, with the\r\nlamp-light shining through them, swaying softly to and fro in the warm\r\nnight wind. There was a sort of excitement in the idea of seeing Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster again; he became aware that his heart was beating rather faster\r\nthan usual. It was this that made him stop, with a half-amused\r\nsurprise. But in a moment he went along the piazza, and, approaching\r\nthe open window, tapped upon its lintel with his stick. He could see\r\nthe Baroness within; she was standing in the middle of the room. She\r\ncame to the window and pulled aside the curtain; then she stood looking\r\nat him a moment. She was not smiling; she seemed serious.\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cMais entrez donc!\xe2\x80\x9d_ she said at last. Acton passed in across the\r\nwindow-sill; he wondered, for an instant, what was the matter with her.\r\nBut the next moment she had begun to smile and had put out her hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBetter late than never,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is very kind of you to come at\r\nthis hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have just returned from my journey,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, very kind, very kind,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated, looking about her where to\r\nsit.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI went first to the other house,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton continued. \xe2\x80\x9cI expected to find\r\nyou there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had sunk into her usual chair; but she got up again, and began to\r\nmove about the room. Acton had laid down his hat and stick; he was\r\nlooking at her, conscious that there was in fact a great charm in\r\nseeing her again. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know whether I ought to tell you to sit\r\ndown,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is too late to begin a visit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s too early to end one,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton declared; \xe2\x80\x9cand we needn\xe2\x80\x99t mind the\r\nbeginning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe looked at him again, and, after a moment, dropped once more into\r\nher low chair, while he took a place near her. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are in the middle,\r\nthen?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked. \xe2\x80\x9cWas that where we were when you went away? No, I\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t been to the other house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot yesterday, nor the day before, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know how many days it is.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are tired of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton.\r\n\r\nShe leaned back in her chair; her arms were folded. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is a terrible\r\naccusation, but I have not the courage to defend myself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not attacking you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cI expected something of this\r\nkind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a proof of extreme intelligence. I hope you enjoyed your\r\njourney.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot at all,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton declared. \xe2\x80\x9cI would much rather have been here with\r\nyou.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow you _are_ attacking me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are contrasting\r\nmy inconstancy with your own fidelity.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI confess I never get tired of people I like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you are not a poor wicked foreign woman, with irritable nerves and\r\na sophisticated mind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSomething has happened to you since I went away,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, changing\r\nhis place.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour going away\xe2\x80\x94that is what has happened to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you mean to say that you have missed me?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I had meant to say it, it would not be worth your making a note of.\r\nI am very dishonest and my compliments are worthless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon was silent for some moments. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have broken down,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at\r\nlast.\r\n\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster left her chair, and began to move about.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly for a moment. I shall pull myself together again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better not take it too hard. If you are bored, you needn\xe2\x80\x99t be\r\nafraid to say so\xe2\x80\x94to me at least.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t say such things as that,\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness answered. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nshould encourage me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI admire your patience; that is encouraging.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t even say that. When you talk of my patience you are\r\ndisloyal to your own people. Patience implies suffering; and what have\r\nI had to suffer?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, not hunger, not unkindness, certainly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, laughing.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNevertheless, we all admire your patience.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou all detest me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness, with a sudden vehemence,\r\nturning her back toward him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou make it hard,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, getting up, \xe2\x80\x9cfor a man to say something\r\ntender to you.\xe2\x80\x9d This evening there was something particularly striking\r\nand touching about her; an unwonted softness and a look of suppressed\r\nemotion. He felt himself suddenly appreciating the fact that she had\r\nbehaved very well. She had come to this quiet corner of the world under\r\nthe weight of a cruel indignity, and she had been so gracefully,\r\nmodestly thankful for the rest she found there. She had joined that\r\nsimple circle over the way; she had mingled in its plain, provincial\r\ntalk; she had shared its meagre and savorless pleasures. She had set\r\nherself a task, and she had rigidly performed it. She had conformed to\r\nthe angular conditions of New England life, and she had had the tact\r\nand pluck to carry it off as if she liked them. Acton felt a more\r\ndownright need than he had ever felt before to tell her that he admired\r\nher and that she struck him as a very superior woman. All along,\r\nhitherto, he had been on his guard with her; he had been cautious,\r\nobservant, suspicious. But now a certain light tumult in his blood\r\nseemed to tell him that a finer degree of confidence in this charming\r\nwoman would be its own reward. \xe2\x80\x9cWe don\xe2\x80\x99t detest you,\xe2\x80\x9d he went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what you mean. Really, it would give me a sort of pleasure to\r\nhear you say so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia had been looking at the door on the other side of the room; now\r\nshe slowly turned her eyes toward Robert Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat can be the\r\nmotive,\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, \xe2\x80\x9cof a man like you\xe2\x80\x94an honest man, a _galant\r\nhomme_\xe2\x80\x94in saying so base a thing as that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoes it sound very base?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton, candidly. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose it does,\r\nand I thank you for telling me so. Of course, I don\xe2\x80\x99t mean it\r\nliterally.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness stood looking at him. \xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean it?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\nThis question was difficult to answer, and Acton, feeling the least bit\r\nfoolish, walked to the open window and looked out. He stood there,\r\nthinking a moment, and then he turned back. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know that document\r\nthat you were to send to Germany,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou called it your\r\n\xe2\x80\x98renunciation.\xe2\x80\x99 Did you ever send it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s eyes expanded; she looked very grave. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat a singular\r\nanswer to my question!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it isn\xe2\x80\x99t an answer,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cI have wished to ask you, many\r\ntimes. There were at any rate offers enough\r\nhe could make; perhaps he had hitherto not been sufficiently explicit\r\nin doing so. \xe2\x80\x9cI wish you would ask something of me,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently said.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs there nothing I can do for you? Over the top of the\r\nfan her eyes were fixed on him. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are very strange tonight,\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nsaid, with a little laugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will do anything in the world,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined, standing in front of\r\nher. \xe2\x80\x9cShouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you like to travel about and see something of the\r\ncountry? You ought to see Niagara, you know.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith you, do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should be delighted to take you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon looked at her, smiling, and yet with a serious air. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, yes;\r\nwe might go alone,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you were not what you are,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered, \xe2\x80\x9cI should feel insulted.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you mean\xe2\x80\x94what I am?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you were one of the gentlemen I have been used to all my life.
