But I have left\r\nthe candle!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia turned away. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are very stupid, my poor boy. You had better\r\ngo home.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, \xe2\x80\x9cgood-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you a word to throw to a man when he has safely returned from\r\na dangerous journey?\xe2\x80\x9d Acton asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow do you do?\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cI thought\xe2\x80\x94I thought you were\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d and he\r\npaused, looking at the Baroness again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou thought I was at Newport, eh? So I was\xe2\x80\x94this morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-night, clever child!\xe2\x80\x9d said Madame M\xc3\xbcnster, over her shoulder.\r\n\r\nClifford stared at her\xe2\x80\x94not at all like a clever child; and then, with\r\none of his little facetious growls, took his departure.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the matter with him?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton, when he was gone. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\nseemed rather in a muddle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia, who was near the window, glanced out, listening a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nmatter\xe2\x80\x94the matter\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you don\xe2\x80\x99t say such things here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf you mean that he had been drinking a little, you can say that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe doesn\xe2\x80\x99t drink any more. I have cured him. And in return\xe2\x80\x94he\xe2\x80\x99s in\r\nlove with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was Acton\xe2\x80\x99s turn to stare. He instantly thought of his sister; but\r\nhe said nothing about her. He began to laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t wonder at his\r\npassion! But I wonder at his forsaking your society for that of your\r\nbrother\xe2\x80\x99s paint-brushes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia was silent a little. \xe2\x80\x9cHe had not been in the studio. I invented\r\nthat at the moment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cInvented it? For what purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has an idea of being romantic. He has adopted the habit of coming\r\nto see me at midnight\xe2\x80\x94passing only through the orchard and through\r\nFelix\xe2\x80\x99s painting-room, which has a door opening that way. It seems to\r\namuse him,\xe2\x80\x9d added Eugenia, with a little laugh.\r\n\r\nActon felt more surprise than he confessed to, for this was a new view\r\nof Clifford, whose irregularities had hitherto been quite without the\r\nromantic element. He tried to laugh again, but he felt rather too\r\nserious, and after a moment\xe2\x80\x99s hesitation his seriousness explained\r\nitself. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope you don\xe2\x80\x99t encourage him,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cHe must not be\r\ninconstant to poor Lizzie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo your sister?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou know they are decidedly intimate,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Eugenia, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9chas she\xe2\x80\x94has she\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton interrupted, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat she has. But I always supposed\r\nthat Clifford had a desire to make himself agreeable to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, _par exemple!_\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness went on. \xe2\x80\x9cThe little monster! The\r\nnext time he becomes sentimental I will him tell that he ought to be\r\nashamed of himself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon was silent a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cYou had better say nothing about it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had told him as much already, on general grounds,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\nBaroness. \xe2\x80\x9cBut in this country, you know, the relations of young people\r\nare so extraordinary that one is quite at sea. They are not engaged\r\nwhen you would quite say they ought to be. Take Charlotte Wentworth,\r\nfor instance, and that young ecclesiastic. If I were her father I\r\nshould insist upon his marrying her; but it appears to be thought there\r\nis no urgency. On the other hand, you suddenly learn that a boy of\r\ntwenty and a little girl who is still with her governess\xe2\x80\x94your sister\r\nhas no governess? Well, then, who is never away from her mamma\xe2\x80\x94a young\r\ncouple, in short, between whom you have noticed nothing beyond an\r\nexchange of the childish pleasantries characteristic of their age, are\r\non the point of setting up as man and wife.\xe2\x80\x9d The Baroness spoke with a\r\ncertain exaggerated volubility which was in contrast with the languid\r\ngrace that had characterized her manner before Clifford made his\r\nappearance. It seemed to Acton that there was a spark of irritation in\r\nher eye\xe2\x80\x94a note of irony (as when she spoke of Lizzie being never away\r\nfrom her mother) in her voice. If Madame M\xc3\xbcnster was irritated, Robert\r\nActon was vaguely mystified; she began to move about the room again,\r\nand he looked at her without saying anything. Presently she took out\r\nher watch, and, glancing at it, declared that it was three o\xe2\x80\x99clock in\r\nthe morning and that he must go.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not been here an hour,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cand they are still sitting up\r\nat the other house. You can see the lights. Your brother has not come\r\nin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, at the other house,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Eugenia, \xe2\x80\x9cthey are terrible people! I\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t know what they may do over there. I am a quiet little humdrum\r\nwoman; I have rigid rules and I keep them. One of them is not to have\r\nvisitors in the small hours\xe2\x80\x94especially clever men like you. So\r\ngood-night!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nDecidedly, the Baroness was incisive; and though Acton bade her\r\ngood-night and departed, he was still a good deal mystified.\r\n\r\nThe next day Clifford Wentworth came to see Lizzie, and Acton, who was\r\nat home and saw him pass through the garden, took note of the\r\ncircumstance. He had a natural desire to make it tally with Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s account of Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s disaffection; but his ingenuity,\r\nfinding itself unequal to the task, resolved at last to ask help of the\r\nyoung man\xe2\x80\x99s candor. He waited till he saw him going away, and then he\r\nwent out and overtook him in the grounds.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wish very much you would answer me a question,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton said. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\r\nwere you doing, last night, at Madame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford began to laugh and to blush, by no means like a young man with\r\na romantic secret. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat did she tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is exactly what I don\xe2\x80\x99t want to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I want to tell you the same,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford; \xe2\x80\x9cand unless I know\r\nit perhaps I can\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey had stopped in a garden path; Acton looked hard at his rosy young\r\nkinsman. \xe2\x80\x9cShe said she couldn\xe2\x80\x99t fancy what had got into you; you\r\nappeared to have taken a violent dislike to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford stared, looking a little alarmed. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, come,\xe2\x80\x9d he growled, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t mean that!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd that when\xe2\x80\x94for common civility\xe2\x80\x99s sake\xe2\x80\x94you came occasionally to the\r\nhouse you left her alone and spent your time in Felix\xe2\x80\x99s studio, under\r\npretext of looking at his sketches.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come!\xe2\x80\x9d growled Clifford, again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you ever know me to tell an untruth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, lots of them!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, seeing an opening, out of the\r\ndiscussion, for his sarcastic powers. \xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently added, \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthought you were my father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou knew someone was there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe heard you coming in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon meditated. \xe2\x80\x9cYou had been with the Baroness, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was in the parlor. We heard your step outside. I thought it was my\r\nfather.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd on that,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton, \xe2\x80\x9cyou ran away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe told me to go\xe2\x80\x94to go out by the studio.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon meditated more intensely; if there had been a chair at hand he\r\nwould have sat down. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should she wish you not to meet your father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, \xe2\x80\x9cfather doesn\xe2\x80\x99t like to see me there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon looked askance at his companion and forbore to make any comment\r\nupon this assertion. \xe2\x80\x9cHas he said so,\xe2\x80\x9d he asked, \xe2\x80\x9cto the Baroness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I hope not,\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford. \xe2\x80\x9cHe hasn\xe2\x80\x99t said so\xe2\x80\x94in so many\r\nwords\xe2\x80\x94to me. But I know it worries him; and I want to stop worrying\r\nhim. The Baroness knows it, and she wants me to stop, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo stop coming to see her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know about that; but to stop worrying father. Eugenia knows\r\neverything,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford added, with an air of knowingness of his own.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, interrogatively, \xe2\x80\x9cEugenia knows everything?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe knew it was not father coming in.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen why did you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nClifford blushed and laughed afresh. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I was afraid it was. And\r\nbesides, she told me to go, at any rate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid she think it was I?\xe2\x80\x9d Acton asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe didn\xe2\x80\x99t say so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Robert Acton reflected. \xe2\x80\x9cBut you didn\xe2\x80\x99t go,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently said;\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cyou came back.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI couldn\xe2\x80\x99t get out of the studio,\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cThe door was\r\nlocked, and Felix has nailed some planks across the lower half of the\r\nconfounded windows to make the light come in from above. So they were\r\nno use. I waited there a good while, and then, suddenly, I felt\r\nashamed. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t want to be hiding away from my own father. I couldn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nstand it any longer. I bolted out, and when I found it was you I was a\r\nlittle flurried. But Eugenia carried it off, didn\xe2\x80\x99t she?\xe2\x80\x9d Clifford\r\nadded, in the tone of a young humorist whose perception had not been\r\npermanently clouded by the sense of his own discomfort.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBeautifully!\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cEspecially,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen one\r\nremembers that you were very imprudent and that she must have been a\r\ngood deal annoyed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Clifford, with the indifference of a young man who feels\r\nthat however he may have failed of felicity in behavior he is extremely\r\njust in his impressions, \xe2\x80\x9cEugenia doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care for anything!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon hesitated a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cThank you for telling me this,\xe2\x80\x9d he said at\r\nlast. And then, laying his hand on Clifford\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, he added, \xe2\x80\x9cTell\r\nme one thing more: are you by chance a little in love with the\r\nBaroness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said Clifford, almost shaking off his hand.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X\r\n\r\n\r\nThe first sunday that followed Robert Acton\xe2\x80\x99s return from Newport\r\nwitnessed a change in the brilliant weather that had long prevailed.\r\nThe rain began to fall and the day was cold and dreary. Mr. Wentworth\r\nand his daughters put on overshoes and went to church, and Felix Young,\r\nwithout overshoes, went also, holding an umbrella over Gertrude. It is\r\nto be feared that, in the whole observance, this was the privilege he\r\nmost highly valued. The Baroness remained at home; she was in neither a\r\ncheerful nor a devotional mood. She had, however, never been, during\r\nher residence in the United States, what is called a regular attendant\r\nat divine service; and on this particular Sunday morning of which I\r\nbegan with speaking she stood at the window of her little drawing-room,\r\nwatching the long arm of a rose tree that was attached to her piazza,\r\nbut a portion of which had disengaged itself, sway to and fro, shake\r\nand gesticulate, against the dusky drizzle of the sky. Every now and\r\nthen, in a gust of wind, the rose tree scattered a shower of\r\nwater-drops against the window-pane; it appeared to have a kind of\r\nhuman movement\xe2\x80\x94a menacing, warning intention. The room was very cold;\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster put on a shawl and walked about. Then she determined to\r\nhave some fire; and summoning her ancient negress, the contrast of\r\nwhose polished ebony and whose crimson turban had been at first a\r\nsource of satisfaction to her, she made arrangements for the production\r\nof a crackling flame. This old woman\xe2\x80\x99s name was Azarina. The Baroness\r\nhad begun by thinking that there would be a savory wildness in her\r\ntalk, and, for amusement, she had encouraged her to chatter. But\r\nAzarina was dry and prim; her conversation was anything but African;\r\nshe reminded Eugenia of the tiresome old ladies she met in society. She\r\nknew, however, how to make a fire; so that after she had laid the logs,\r\nEugenia, who was terribly bored, found a quarter of an hour\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nentertainment in sitting and watching them blaze and sputter. She had\r\nthought it very likely Robert Acton would come and see her; she had not\r\nmet him since that infelicitous evening. But the morning waned without\r\nhis coming; several times she thought she heard his step on the piazza;\r\nbut it was only a window-shutter shaking in a rain-gust. The Baroness,\r\nsince the beginning of that episode in her career of which a slight\r\nsketch has been attempted in these pages, had had many moments of\r\nirritation. But today her irritation had a peculiar keenness; it\r\nappeared to feed upon itself. It urged her to do something; but it\r\nsuggested no particularly profitable line of action. If she could have\r\ndone something at the moment, on the spot, she would have stepped upon\r\na European steamer and turned her back, with a kind of rapture, upon\r\nthat profoundly mortifying failure, her visit to her American\r\nrelations. It is not exactly apparent why she should have termed this\r\nenterprise a failure, inasmuch as she had been treated with the highest\r\ndistinction for which allowance had been made in American institutions.\r\nHer irritation came, at bottom, from the sense, which, always present,\r\nhad suddenly grown acute, that the social soil on this big, vague\r\ncontinent was somehow not adapted for growing those plants whose\r\nfragrance she especially inclined to inhale and by which she liked to\r\nsee herself surrounded\xe2\x80\x94a species of vegetation for which she carried a\r\ncollection of seedlings, as we may say, in her pocket. She found her\r\nchief happiness in the sense of exerting a certain power and making a\r\ncertain impression; and now she felt the annoyance of a rather wearied\r\nswimmer who, on nearing shore, to land, finds a smooth straight wall of\r\nrock when he had counted upon a clean firm beach. Her power, in the\r\nAmerican air, seemed to have lost its prehensile attributes; the smooth\r\nwall of rock was insurmountable. _\xe2\x80\x9cSurely je n\xe2\x80\x99en suis pas l\xc3\xa0,\xe2\x80\x9d_ she\r\nsaid to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I let it make me uncomfortable that a Mr. Robert\r\nActon shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t honor me with a visit!\xe2\x80\x9d Yet she was vexed that he had\r\nnot come; and she was vexed at her vexation.\r\n\r\nHer brother, at least, came in, stamping in the hall and shaking the\r\nwet from his coat. In a moment he entered the room, with a glow in his\r\ncheek and half-a-dozen rain-drops glistening on his moustache. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, you\r\nhave a fire,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cLes beaux jours sont pass\xc3\xa9s,\xe2\x80\x9d_ replied the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever, never! They have only begun,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix declared, planting himself\r\nbefore the hearth. He turned his back to the fire, placed his hands\r\nbehind him, extended his legs and looked away through the window with\r\nan expression of face which seemed to denote the perception of\r\nrose-color even in the tints of a wet Sunday.\r\n\r\nHis sister, from her chair, looked up at him, watching him; and what\r\nshe saw in his face was not grateful to her present mood. She was\r\npuzzled by many things, but her brother\xe2\x80\x99s disposition was a frequent\r\nsource of wonder to her. I say frequent and not constant, for there\r\nwere long periods during which she gave her attention to other\r\nproblems. Sometimes she had said to herself that his happy temper, his\r\neternal gaiety, was an affectation, a _pose_; but she was vaguely\r\nconscious that during the present summer he had been a highly\r\nsuccessful comedian. They had never yet had an explanation; she had not\r\nknown the need of one. Felix was presumably following the bent of his\r\ndisinterested genius, and she felt that she had no advice to give him\r\nthat he would understand. With this, there was always a certain element\r\nof comfort about Felix\xe2\x80\x94the assurance that he would not interfere. He\r\nwas very delicate, this pure-minded Felix; in effect, he was her\r\nbrother, and Madame M\xc3\xbcnster felt that there was a great propriety,\r\nevery way, in that. It is true that Felix was delicate; he was not fond\r\nof explanations with his sister; this was one of the very few things in\r\nthe world about which he was uncomfortable. But now he was not thinking\r\nof anything uncomfortable.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia at last, \xe2\x80\x9cdo stop making _les yeux doux_\r\nat the rain.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith pleasure. I will make them at you!\xe2\x80\x9d answered Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow much longer,\xe2\x80\x9d asked Eugenia, in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cdo you propose to\r\nremain in this lovely spot?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix stared. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you want to go away\xe2\x80\x94already?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Already\xe2\x80\x99 is delicious. I am not so happy as you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix dropped into a chair, looking at the fire. \xe2\x80\x9cThe fact is I _am_\r\nhappy,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in his light, clear tone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd do you propose to spend your life in making love to Gertrude\r\nWentworth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, smiling sidewise at his sister.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness returned his glance, much more gravely; and then, \xe2\x80\x9cDo you\r\nlike her?\xe2\x80\x9d she asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix demanded.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness was silent a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cI will answer you in the words of\r\nthe gentleman who was asked if he liked music: _\xe2\x80\x98Je ne la crains\r\npas!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x99_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe admires you immensely,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t care for that. Other women should not admire one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey should dislike you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAgain Madame M\xc3\xbcnster hesitated. \xe2\x80\x9cThey should hate me! It\xe2\x80\x99s a measure of\r\nthe time I have been losing here that they don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo time is lost in which one has been happy!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, with a\r\nbright sententiousness which may well have been a little irritating.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd in which,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined his sister, with a harsher laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cone has\r\nsecured the affections of a young lady with a fortune!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix explained, very candidly and seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cI have secured\r\nGertrude\xe2\x80\x99s affection, but I am by no means sure that I have secured her\r\nfortune. That may come\xe2\x80\x94or it may not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, well, it _may!_ That\xe2\x80\x99s the great point.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt depends upon her father. He doesn\xe2\x80\x99t smile upon our union. You know\r\nhe wants her to marry Mr. Brand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know nothing about it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cPlease to put on a\r\nlog.\xe2\x80\x9d Felix complied with her request and sat watching the quickening\r\nof the flame. Presently his sister added, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd you propose to elope\r\nwith mademoiselle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy no means. I don\xe2\x80\x99t wish to do anything that\xe2\x80\x99s disagreeable to Mr.\r\nWentworth. He has been far too kind to us.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you must choose between pleasing yourself and pleasing him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to please everyone!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Felix, joyously. \xe2\x80\x9cI have a good\r\nconscience. I made up my mind at the outset that it was not my place to\r\nmake love to Gertrude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, to simplify matters, she made love to you!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix looked at his sister with sudden gravity. \xe2\x80\x9cYou say you are not\r\nafraid of her,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut perhaps you ought to be\xe2\x80\x94a little. She\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nvery clever person.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI begin to see it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the Baroness. Her brother, making no\r\nrejoinder, leaned back in his chair, and there was a long silence. At\r\nlast, with an altered accent, Madame M\xc3\xbcnster put another question. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nexpect, at any rate, to marry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be greatly disappointed if we don\xe2\x80\x99t.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA disappointment or two will do you good!\xe2\x80\x9d the Baroness declared.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, afterwards, do you mean to turn American?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me I am a very good American already. But we shall go to\r\nEurope. Gertrude wants extremely to see the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, like me, when I came here!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, with a little\r\nlaugh.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, not like you,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined, looking at his sister with a\r\ncertain gentle seriousness. While he looked at her she rose from her\r\nchair, and he also got up. \xe2\x80\x9cGertrude is not at all like you,\xe2\x80\x9d he went\r\non; \xe2\x80\x9cbut in her own way she is almost as clever.\xe2\x80\x9d He paused a moment;\r\nhis soul was full of an agreeable feeling and of a lively disposition\r\nto express it. His sister, to his spiritual vision, was always like the\r\nlunar disk when only a part of it is lighted. The shadow on this bright\r\nsurface seemed to him to expand and to contract; but whatever its\r\nproportions, he always appreciated the moonlight. He looked at the\r\nBaroness, and then he kissed her. \xe2\x80\x9cI am very much in love with\r\nGertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. Eugenia turned away and walked about the room, and\r\nFelix continued. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is very interesting, and very different from what\r\nshe seems. She has never had a chance. She is very brilliant. We will\r\ngo to Europe and amuse ourselves.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness had gone to the window, where she stood looking out. The\r\nday was drearier than ever; the rain was doggedly falling. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, to\r\namuse yourselves,\xe2\x80\x9d she said at last, \xe2\x80\x9cyou had decidedly better go to\r\nEurope!\xe2\x80\x9d Then she turned round, looking at her brother. A chair stood\r\nnear her; she leaned her hands upon the back of it. \xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you think it\r\nis very good of me,\xe2\x80\x9d she asked, \xe2\x80\x9cto come all this way with you simply\r\nto see you properly married\xe2\x80\x94if properly it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it will be properly!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, with light eagerness.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness gave a little laugh. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are thinking only of yourself,\r\nand you don\xe2\x80\x99t answer my question. While you are amusing yourself\xe2\x80\x94with\r\nthe brilliant Gertrude\xe2\x80\x94what shall I be doing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n_\xe2\x80\x9cVous serez de la partie!\xe2\x80\x9d_ cried Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you: I should spoil it.\xe2\x80\x9d The Baroness dropped her eyes for some\r\nmoments. \xe2\x80\x9cDo you propose, however, to leave me here?\xe2\x80\x9d she inquired.\r\n\r\nFelix smiled at her. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest sister, where you are concerned I\r\nnever propose. I execute your commands.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia, slowly, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you are the most heartless\r\nperson living. Don\xe2\x80\x99t you see that I am in trouble?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw that you were not cheerful, and I gave you some good news.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, let me give you some news,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cYou probably\r\nwill not have discovered it for yourself. Robert Acton wants to marry\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I had not discovered that. But I quite understand it. Why does it\r\nmake you unhappy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I can\xe2\x80\x99t decide.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccept him, accept him!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, joyously. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is the best fellow\r\nin the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is immensely in love with me,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he has a large fortune. Permit me in turn to remind you of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I am perfectly aware of it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a great item in\r\nhis favor. I am terribly candid.\xe2\x80\x9d And she left her place and came\r\nnearer her brother, looking at him hard. He was turning over several\r\nthings; she was wondering in what manner he really understood her.\r\n\r\nThere were several ways of understanding her: there was what she said,\r\nand there was what she meant, and there was something, between the two,\r\nthat was neither. It is probable that, in the last analysis, what she\r\nmeant was that Felix should spare her the necessity of stating the case\r\nmore exactly and should hold himself commissioned to assist her by all\r\nhonorable means to marry the best fellow in the world. But in all this\r\nit was never discovered what Felix understood.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnce you have your liberty, what are your objections?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I don\xe2\x80\x99t particularly like him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, try a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am trying now,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cI should succeed better if he didn\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nlive here. I could never live here.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMake him go to Europe,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix suggested.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, there you speak of happiness based upon violent effort,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nBaroness rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cThat is not what I am looking for. He would never\r\nlive in Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe would live anywhere, with you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, gallantly.\r\n\r\nHis sister looked at him still, with a ray of penetration in her\r\ncharming eyes; then she turned away again. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, at all events,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nshe presently went on, \xe2\x80\x9cthat if it had been said of me that I had come\r\nover here to seek my fortune it would have to be added that I have\r\nfound it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t leave it lying!\xe2\x80\x9d urged Felix, with smiling solemnity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am much obliged to you for your interest,\xe2\x80\x9d his sister declared,\r\nafter a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cBut promise me one thing: _pas de z\xc3\xa8le!_ If Mr. Acton\r\nshould ask you to plead his cause, excuse yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall certainly have the excuse,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I have a cause\r\nof my own to plead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf he should talk of me\xe2\x80\x94favorably,\xe2\x80\x9d Eugenia continued, \xe2\x80\x9cwarn him\r\nagainst dangerous illusions. I detest importunities; I want to decide\r\nat my leisure, with my eyes open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be discreet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cexcept to you. To you I will say,\r\nAccept him outright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had advanced to the open doorway, and she stood looking at him. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwill go and dress and think of it,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; and he heard her moving\r\nslowly to her apartments.\r\n\r\nLate in the afternoon the rain stopped, and just afterwards there was a\r\ngreat flaming, flickering, trickling sunset. Felix sat in his\r\npainting-room and did some work; but at last, as the light, which had\r\nnot been brilliant, began to fade, he laid down his brushes and came\r\nout to the little piazza of the cottage. Here he walked up and down for\r\nsome time, looking at the splendid blaze of the western sky and saying,\r\nas he had often said before, that this was certainly the country of\r\nsunsets. There was something in these glorious deeps of fire that\r\nquickened his imagination; he always found images and promises in the\r\nwestern sky. He thought of a good many things\xe2\x80\x94of roaming about the\r\nworld with Gertrude Wentworth; he seemed to see their possible\r\nadventures, in a glowing frieze, between the cloud-bars; then of what\r\nEugenia had just been telling him. He wished very much that Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster would make a comfortable and honorable marriage. Presently, as\r\nthe sunset expanded and deepened, the fancy took him of making a note\r\nof so magnificent a piece of coloring. He returned to his studio and\r\nfetched out a small panel, with his palette and brushes, and, placing\r\nthe panel against a window-sill, he began to daub with great gusto.\r\nWhile he was so occupied he saw Mr. Brand, in the distance, slowly come\r\ndown from Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s house, nursing a large folded umbrella. He\r\nwalked with a joyless, meditative tread, and his eyes were bent upon\r\nthe ground. Felix poised his brush for a moment, watching him; then, by\r\na sudden impulse, as he drew nearer, advanced to the garden-gate and\r\nsignaled to him\xe2\x80\x94the palette and bunch of brushes contributing to this\r\neffect.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand stopped and started; then he appeared to decide to accept\r\nFelix\xe2\x80\x99s invitation. He came out of Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s gate and passed\r\nalong the road; after which he entered the little garden of the\r\ncottage. Felix had gone back to his sunset; but he made his visitor\r\nwelcome while he rapidly brushed it in.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI wanted so much to speak to you that I thought I would call you,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid, in the friendliest tone. \xe2\x80\x9cAll the more that you have been to see\r\nme so little. You have come to see my sister; I know that. But you\r\nhaven\xe2\x80\x99t come to see me\xe2\x80\x94the celebrated artist. Artists are very\r\nsensitive, you know; they notice those things.\xe2\x80\x9d And Felix turned round,\r\nsmiling, with a brush in his mouth.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand stood there with a certain blank, candid majesty, pulling\r\ntogether the large flaps of his umbrella. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I come to see\r\nyou?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked. \xe2\x80\x9cI know nothing of Art.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt would sound very conceited, I suppose,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cif I were to\r\nsay that it would be a good little chance for you to learn something.\r\nYou would ask me why you should learn; and I should have no answer to\r\nthat. I suppose a minister has no need for Art, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe has need for good temper, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand, with decision.\r\n\r\nFelix jumped up, with his palette on his thumb and a movement of the\r\nliveliest deprecation. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s because I keep you standing there while\r\nI splash my red paint! I beg a thousand pardons! You see what bad\r\nmanners Art gives a man; and how right you are to let it alone. I\r\ndidn\xe2\x80\x99t mean you should stand, either. The piazza, as you see, is\r\nornamented with rustic chairs; though indeed I ought to warn you that\r\nthey have nails in the wrong places. I was just making a note of that\r\nsunset. I never saw such a blaze of different reds. It looks as if the\r\nCelestial City were in flames, eh? If that were really the case I\r\nsuppose it would be the business of you theologians to put out the\r\nfire. Fancy me\xe2\x80\x94an ungodly artist\xe2\x80\x94quietly sitting down to paint it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand had always credited Felix Young with a certain impudence, but\r\nit appeared to him that on this occasion his impudence was so great as\r\nto make a special explanation\xe2\x80\x94or even an apology\xe2\x80\x94necessary. And the\r\nimpression, it must be added, was sufficiently natural. Felix had at\r\nall times a brilliant assurance of manner which was simply the vehicle\r\nof his good spirits and his good will; but at present he had a special\r\ndesign, and as he would have admitted that the design was audacious, so\r\nhe was conscious of having summoned all the arts of conversation to his\r\naid. But he was so far from desiring to offend his visitor that he was\r\nrapidly asking himself what personal compliment he could pay the young\r\nclergyman that would gratify him most. If he could think of it, he was\r\nprepared to pay it down. \xe2\x80\x9cHave you been preaching one of your beautiful\r\nsermons today?\xe2\x80\x9d he suddenly asked, laying down his palette. This was\r\nnot what Felix had been trying to think of, but it was a tolerable\r\nstop-gap.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand frowned\xe2\x80\x94as much as a man can frown who has very fair, soft\r\neyebrows, and, beneath them, very gentle, tranquil eyes. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, I have\r\nnot preached any sermon today. Did you bring me over here for the\r\npurpose of making that inquiry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix saw that he was irritated, and he regretted it immensely; but he\r\nhad no fear of not being, in the end, agreeable to Mr. Brand. He looked\r\nat him, smiling and laying his hand on his arm. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, no, not for\r\nthat\xe2\x80\x94not for that. I wanted to ask you something; I wanted to tell you\r\nsomething. I am sure it will interest you very much. Only\xe2\x80\x94as it is\r\nsomething rather private\xe2\x80\x94we had better come into my little studio. I\r\nhave a western window; we can still see the sunset. _Andiamo!_\xe2\x80\x9d And he\r\ngave a little pat to his companion\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\nHe led the way in; Mr. Brand stiffly and softly followed. The twilight\r\nhad thickened in the little studio; but the wall opposite the western\r\nwindow was covered with a deep pink flush. There were a great many\r\nsketches and half-finished canvasses suspended in this rosy glow, and\r\nthe corners of the room were vague and dusky. Felix begged Mr. Brand to\r\nsit down; then glancing round him, \xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove, how pretty it looks!\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ncried. But Mr. Brand would not sit down; he went and leaned against the\r\nwindow; he wondered what Felix wanted of him. In the shadow, on the\r\ndarker parts of the wall, he saw the gleam of three or four pictures\r\nthat looked fantastic and surprising. They seemed to represent naked\r\nfigures. Felix stood there, with his head a little bent and his eyes\r\nfixed upon his visitor, smiling intensely, pulling his moustache. Mr.\r\nBrand felt vaguely uneasy. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is very delicate\xe2\x80\x94what I want to say,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nFelix began. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have been thinking of it for some time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease to say it as quickly as possible,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s because you are a clergyman, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix went on. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nthink I should venture to say it to a common man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand was silent a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cIf it is a question of yielding to a\r\nweakness, of resenting an injury, I am afraid I am a very common man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dearest friend,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cthis is not an injury; it\xe2\x80\x99s a\r\nbenefit\xe2\x80\x94a great service! You will like it extremely. Only it\xe2\x80\x99s so\r\ndelicate!\xe2\x80\x9d And, in the dim light, he continued to smile intensely. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nknow I take a great interest in my cousins\xe2\x80\x94in Charlotte and Gertrude\r\nWentworth. That\xe2\x80\x99s very evident from my having traveled some five\r\nthousand miles to see them.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand said nothing and Felix\r\nproceeded. \xe2\x80\x9cComing into their society as a perfect stranger I received\r\nof course a great many new impressions, and my impressions had a great\r\nfreshness, a great keenness. Do you know what I mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that I do; but I should like you to continue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think my impressions have always a good deal of freshness,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nBrand\xe2\x80\x99s entertainer; \xe2\x80\x9cbut on this occasion it was perhaps particularly\r\nnatural that\xe2\x80\x94coming in, as I say, from outside\xe2\x80\x94I should be struck with\r\nthings that passed unnoticed among yourselves. And then I had my sister\r\nto help me; and she is simply the most observant woman in the world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not surprised,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand, \xe2\x80\x9cthat in our little circle two\r\nintelligent persons should have found food for observation. I am sure\r\nthat, of late, I have found it myself!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, but I shall surprise you yet!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cBoth my\r\nsister and I took a great fancy to my cousin Charlotte.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour cousin Charlotte?\xe2\x80\x9d repeated Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe fell in love with her from the first!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou fell in love with Charlotte?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Dame!_\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cshe\xe2\x80\x99s a very charming person; and Eugenia\r\nwas especially smitten.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand stood staring, and he pursued,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAffection, you know, opens one\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and we noticed something.\r\nCharlotte is not happy! Charlotte is in love.\xe2\x80\x9d And Felix, drawing\r\nnearer, laid his hand again upon his companion\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\nThere was something akin to an acknowledgment of fascination in the way\r\nMr. Brand looked at him; but the young clergyman retained as yet quite\r\nenough self-possession to be able to say, with a good deal of\r\nsolemnity, \xe2\x80\x9cShe is not in love with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix gave a light laugh, and rejoined with the alacrity of a maritime\r\nadventurer who feels a puff of wind in his sail. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, no; if she were\r\nin love with me I should know it! I am not so blind as you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sir, you are stone blind. Poor Charlotte is dead in love with\r\n_you!_\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand said nothing for a moment; he breathed a little heavily. \xe2\x80\x9cIs\r\nthat what you wanted to say to me?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have wanted to say it these three weeks. Because of late she has\r\nbeen worse. I told you,\xe2\x80\x9d added Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cit was very delicate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, sir\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94Mr. Brand began; \xe2\x80\x9cwell, sir\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was sure you didn\xe2\x80\x99t know it,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix continued. \xe2\x80\x9cBut don\xe2\x80\x99t you see\xe2\x80\x94as\r\nsoon as I mention it\xe2\x80\x94how everything is explained?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand answered\r\nnothing; he looked for a chair and softly sat down. Felix could see\r\nthat he was blushing; he had looked straight at his host hitherto, but\r\nnow he looked away. The foremost effect of what he had heard had been a\r\nsort of irritation of his modesty. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, \xe2\x80\x9cI suggest\r\nnothing; it would be very presumptuous in me to advise you. But I think\r\nthere is no doubt about the fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand looked hard at the floor for some moments; he was oppressed\r\nwith a mixture of sensations. Felix, standing there, was very sure that\r\none of them was profound surprise. The innocent young man had been\r\ncompletely unsuspicious of poor Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s hidden flame. This gave\r\nFelix great hope; he was sure that Mr. Brand would be flattered. Felix\r\nthought him very transparent, and indeed he was so; he could neither\r\nsimulate nor dissimulate. \xe2\x80\x9cI scarcely know what to make of this,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid at last, without looking up; and Felix was struck with the fact\r\nthat he offered no protest or contradiction. Evidently Felix had\r\nkindled a train of memories\xe2\x80\x94a retrospective illumination. It was\r\nmaking, to Mr. Brand\xe2\x80\x99s astonished eyes, a very pretty blaze; his second\r\nemotion had been a gratification of vanity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank me for telling you,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a good thing to\r\nknow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure of that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, don\xe2\x80\x99t let her languish!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix murmured, lightly and softly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou _do_ advise me, then?\xe2\x80\x9d And Mr. Brand looked up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI congratulate you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, smiling. He had thought at first his\r\nvisitor was simply appealing; but he saw he was a little ironical.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is in your interest; you have interfered with me,\xe2\x80\x9d the young\r\nclergyman went on.\r\n\r\nFelix still stood and smiled. The little room had grown darker, and the\r\ncrimson glow had faded; but Mr. Brand could see the brilliant\r\nexpression of his face. \xe2\x80\x9cI won\xe2\x80\x99t pretend not to know what you mean,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid Felix at last. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I have not really interfered with you. Of what\r\nyou had to lose\xe2\x80\x94with another person\xe2\x80\x94you have lost nothing. And think\r\nwhat you have gained!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me I am the proper judge, on each side,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand\r\ndeclared. He got up, holding the brim of his hat against his mouth and\r\nstaring at Felix through the dusk.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have lost an illusion!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you call an illusion?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe belief that you really know\xe2\x80\x94that you have ever really\r\nknown\xe2\x80\x94Gertrude Wentworth. Depend upon that,\xe2\x80\x9d pursued Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nknow her yet; but I have no illusions; I don\xe2\x80\x99t pretend to.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand kept gazing, over his hat. \xe2\x80\x9cShe has always been a lucid,\r\nlimpid nature,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, solemnly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has always been a dormant nature. She was waiting for a\r\ntouchstone. But now she is beginning to awaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t praise her to me!\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand, with a little quaver in his\r\nvoice. \xe2\x80\x9cIf you have the advantage of me that is not generous.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sir, I am melting with generosity!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am\r\nnot praising my cousin. I am simply attempting a scientific definition\r\nof her. She doesn\xe2\x80\x99t care for abstractions. Now I think the contrary is\r\nwhat you have always fancied\xe2\x80\x94is the basis on which you have been\r\nbuilding. She is extremely preoccupied with the concrete. I care for\r\nthe concrete, too. But Gertrude is stronger than I; she whirls me\r\nalong!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand looked for a moment into the crown of his hat. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s a most\r\ninteresting nature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo it is,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cBut it pulls\xe2\x80\x94it pulls\xe2\x80\x94like a runaway horse.\r\nNow I like the feeling of a runaway horse; and if I am thrown out of\r\nthe vehicle it is no great matter. But if _you_ should be thrown, Mr.\r\nBrand\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94and Felix paused a moment\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9canother person also would suffer from\r\nthe accident.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat other person?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCharlotte Wentworth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand looked at Felix for a moment sidewise, mistrustfully; then\r\nhis eyes slowly wandered over the ceiling. Felix was sure he was\r\nsecretly struck with the romance of the situation. \xe2\x80\x9cI think this is\r\nnone of our business,\xe2\x80\x9d the young minister murmured.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone of mine, perhaps; but surely yours!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand lingered still, looking at the ceiling; there was evidently\r\nsomething he wanted to say. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat do you mean by Miss Gertrude being\r\nstrong?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked abruptly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix meditatively, \xe2\x80\x9cI mean that she has had a great deal\r\nof self-possession. She was waiting\xe2\x80\x94for years; even when she seemed,\r\nperhaps, to be living in the present. She knew how to wait; she had a\r\npurpose. That\xe2\x80\x99s what I mean by her being strong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut what do you mean by her purpose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell\xe2\x80\x94the purpose to see the world!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand eyed his strange informant askance again; but he said\r\nnothing. At last he turned away, as if to take leave. He seemed\r\nbewildered, however; for instead of going to the door he moved toward\r\nthe opposite corner of the room. Felix stood and watched him for a\r\nmoment\xe2\x80\x94almost groping about in the dusk; then he led him to the door,\r\nwith a tender, almost fraternal movement. \xe2\x80\x9cIs that all you have to\r\nsay?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Brand.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it\xe2\x80\x99s all\xe2\x80\x94but it will bear a good deal of thinking of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix went with him to the garden-gate, and watched him slowly walk\r\naway into the thickening twilight with a relaxed rigidity that tried to\r\nrectify itself. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is offended, excited, bewildered, perplexed\xe2\x80\x94and\r\nenchanted!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix said to himself. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a capital mixture.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI\r\n\r\n\r\nSince that visit paid by the Baroness M\xc3\xbcnster to Mrs. Acton, of which\r\nsome account was given at an earlier stage of this narrative, the\r\nintercourse between these two ladies had been neither frequent nor\r\nintimate. It was not that Mrs. Acton had failed to appreciate Madame\r\nM\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s charms; on the contrary, her perception of the graces of\r\nmanner and conversation of her brilliant visitor had been only too\r\nacute. Mrs. Acton was, as they said in Boston, very \xe2\x80\x9cintense,\xe2\x80\x9d and her\r\nimpressions were apt to be too many for her. The state of her health\r\nrequired the restriction of emotion; and this is why, receiving, as she\r\nsat in her eternal arm-chair, very few visitors, even of the soberest\r\nlocal type, she had been obliged to limit the number of her interviews\r\nwith a lady whose costume and manner recalled to her imagination\xe2\x80\x94Mrs.\r\nActon\xe2\x80\x99s imagination was a marvel\xe2\x80\x94all that she had ever read of the most\r\nstirring historical periods. But she had sent the Baroness a great many\r\nquaintly-worded messages and a great many nosegays from her garden and\r\nbaskets of beautiful fruit. Felix had eaten the fruit, and the Baroness\r\nhad arranged the flowers and returned the baskets and the messages. On\r\nthe day that followed that rainy Sunday of which mention has been made,\r\nEugenia determined to go and pay the beneficent invalid a _\xe2\x80\x9cvisite\r\nd\xe2\x80\x99adieux\xe2\x80\x9d_; so it was that, to herself, she qualified her enterprise.\r\nIt may be noted that neither on the Sunday evening nor on the Monday\r\nmorning had she received that expected visit from Robert Acton. To his\r\nown consciousness, evidently he was \xe2\x80\x9ckeeping away;\xe2\x80\x9d and as the\r\nBaroness, on her side, was keeping away from her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s, whither, for\r\nseveral days, Felix had been the unembarrassed bearer of apologies and\r\nregrets for absence, chance had not taken the cards from the hands of\r\ndesign. Mr. Wentworth and his daughters had respected Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nseclusion; certain intervals of mysterious retirement appeared to them,\r\nvaguely, a natural part of the graceful, rhythmic movement of so\r\nremarkable a life. Gertrude especially held these periods in honor; she\r\nwondered what Madame M\xc3\xbcnster did at such times, but she would not have\r\npermitted herself to inquire too curiously.\r\n\r\nThe long rain had freshened the air, and twelve hours\xe2\x80\x99 brilliant\r\nsunshine had dried the roads; so that the Baroness, in the late\r\nafternoon, proposing to walk to Mrs. Acton\xe2\x80\x99s, exposed herself to no\r\ngreat discomfort. As with her charming undulating step she moved along\r\nthe clean, grassy margin of the road, beneath the thickly-hanging\r\nboughs of the orchards, through the quiet of the hour and place and the\r\nrich maturity of the summer, she was even conscious of a sort of\r\nluxurious melancholy. The Baroness had the amiable weakness of\r\nattaching herself to places\xe2\x80\x94even when she had begun with a little\r\naversion; and now, with the prospect of departure, she felt tenderly\r\ntoward this well-wooded corner of the Western world, where the sunsets\r\nwere so beautiful and one\xe2\x80\x99s ambitions were so pure. Mrs. Acton was able\r\nto receive her; but on entering this lady\xe2\x80\x99s large, freshly-scented room\r\nthe Baroness saw that she was looking very ill. She was wonderfully\r\nwhite and transparent, and, in her flowered arm-chair, she made no\r\nattempt to move. But she flushed a little\xe2\x80\x94like a young girl, the\r\nBaroness thought\xe2\x80\x94and she rested her clear, smiling eyes upon those of\r\nher visitor. Her voice was low and monotonous, like a voice that had\r\nnever expressed any human passions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have come to bid you good-bye,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cI shall soon be going\r\naway.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen are you going away?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery soon\xe2\x80\x94any day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am very sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cI hoped you would stay\xe2\x80\x94always.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways?\xe2\x80\x9d Eugenia demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I mean a long time,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Acton, in her sweet, feeble tone.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey tell me you are so comfortable\xe2\x80\x94that you have got such a beautiful\r\nlittle house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia stared\xe2\x80\x94that is, she smiled; she thought of her poor little\r\nchalet and she wondered whether her hostess were jesting. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, my\r\nhouse is exquisite,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cthough not to be compared to yours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd my son is so fond of going to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Acton added. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nafraid my son will miss you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, dear madam,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia, with a little laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cI can\xe2\x80\x99t stay in\r\nAmerica for your son!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDon\xe2\x80\x99t you like America?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness looked at the front of her dress. \xe2\x80\x9cIf I liked it\xe2\x80\x94that\r\nwould not be staying for your son!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Acton gazed at her with her grave, tender eyes, as if she had not\r\nquite understood. The Baroness at last found something irritating in\r\nthe sweet, soft stare of her hostess; and if one were not bound to be\r\nmerciful to great invalids she would almost have taken the liberty of\r\npronouncing her, mentally, a fool. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid, then, I shall never\r\nsee you again,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mrs. Acton. \xe2\x80\x9cYou know I am dying.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, dear madam,\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Eugenia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI want to leave my children cheerful and happy. My daughter will\r\nprobably marry her cousin.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTwo such interesting young people,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, vaguely. She\r\nwas not thinking of Clifford Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel so tranquil about my end,\xe2\x80\x9d Mrs. Acton went on. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is coming so\r\neasily, so surely.\xe2\x80\x9d And she paused, with her mild gaze always on\r\nEugenia\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness hated to be reminded of death; but even in its imminence,\r\nso far as Mrs. Acton was concerned, she preserved her good manners.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, madam, you are too charming an invalid,\xe2\x80\x9d she rejoined.\r\n\r\nBut the delicacy of this rejoinder was apparently lost upon her\r\nhostess, who went on in her low, reasonable voice. \xe2\x80\x9cI want to leave my\r\nchildren bright and comfortable. You seem to me all so happy here\xe2\x80\x94just\r\nas you are. So I wish you could stay. It would be so pleasant for\r\nRobert.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEugenia wondered what she meant by its being pleasant for Robert; but\r\nshe felt that she would never know what such a woman as that meant. She\r\ngot up; she was afraid Mrs. Acton would tell her again that she was\r\ndying. \xe2\x80\x9cGood-bye, dear madam,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI must remember that your\r\nstrength is precious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMrs. Acton took her hand and held it a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, you _have_ been\r\nhappy here, haven\xe2\x80\x99t you? And you like us all, don\xe2\x80\x99t you? I wish you\r\nwould stay,\xe2\x80\x9d she added, \xe2\x80\x9cin your beautiful little house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had told Eugenia that her waiting-woman would be in the hall, to\r\nshow her downstairs; but the large landing outside her door was empty,\r\nand Eugenia stood there looking about. She felt irritated; the dying\r\nlady had not _\xe2\x80\x9cla main heureuse.\xe2\x80\x9d_ She passed slowly downstairs, still\r\nlooking about. The broad staircase made a great bend, and in the angle\r\nwas a high window, looking westward, with a deep bench, covered with a\r\nrow of flowering plants in curious old pots of blue china-ware. The\r\nyellow afternoon light came in through the flowers and flickered a\r\nlittle on the white wainscots. Eugenia paused a moment; the house was\r\nperfectly still, save for the ticking, somewhere, of a great clock. The\r\nlower hall stretched away at the foot of the stairs, half covered over\r\nwith a large Oriental rug. Eugenia lingered a little, noticing a great\r\nmany things. _\xe2\x80\x9cComme c\xe2\x80\x99est bien!\xe2\x80\x9d_ she said to herself; such a large,\r\nsolid, irreproachable basis of existence the place seemed to her to\r\nindicate. And then she reflected that Mrs. Acton was soon to withdraw\r\nfrom it. The reflection accompanied her the rest of the way downstairs,\r\nwhere she paused again, making more observations. The hall was\r\nextremely broad, and on either side of the front door was a wide,\r\ndeeply-set window, which threw the shadows of everything back into the\r\nhouse. There were high-backed chairs along the wall and big Eastern\r\nvases upon tables, and, on either side, a large cabinet with a glass\r\nfront and little curiosities within, dimly gleaming. The doors were\r\nopen\xe2\x80\x94into the darkened parlor, the library, the dining-room. All these\r\nrooms seemed empty. Eugenia passed along, and stopped a moment on the\r\nthreshold of each. _\xe2\x80\x9cComme c\xe2\x80\x99est bien!\xe2\x80\x9d_ she murmured again; she had\r\nthought of just such a house as this when she decided to come to\r\nAmerica. She opened the front door for herself\xe2\x80\x94her light tread had\r\nsummoned none of the servants\xe2\x80\x94and on the threshold she gave a last\r\nlook. Outside, she was still in the humor for curious contemplation; so\r\ninstead of going directly down the little drive, to the gate, she\r\nwandered away towards the garden, which lay to the right of the house.\r\nShe had not gone many yards over the grass before she paused quickly;\r\nshe perceived a gentleman stretched upon the level verdure, beneath a\r\ntree. He had not heard her coming, and he lay motionless, flat on his\r\nback, with his hands clasped under his head, staring up at the sky; so\r\nthat the Baroness was able to reflect, at her leisure, upon the\r\nquestion of his identity. It was that of a person who had lately been\r\nmuch in her thoughts; but her first impulse, nevertheless, was to turn\r\naway; the last thing she desired was to have the air of coming in quest\r\nof Robert Acton. The gentleman on the grass, however, gave her no time\r\nto decide; he could not long remain unconscious of so agreeable a\r\npresence. He rolled back his eyes, stared, gave an exclamation, and\r\nthen jumped up. He stood an instant, looking at her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse my ridiculous position,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have just now no sense of the ridiculous. But, in case you have,\r\ndon\xe2\x80\x99t imagine I came to see you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake care,\xe2\x80\x9d rejoined Acton, \xe2\x80\x9chow you put it into my head! I was\r\nthinking of you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe occupation of extreme leisure!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cTo think of a\r\nwoman when you are in that position is no compliment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t say I was thinking well!\xe2\x80\x9d Acton affirmed, smiling.\r\n\r\nShe looked at him, and then she turned away.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThough I didn\xe2\x80\x99t come to see you,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9cremember at least that I\r\nam within your gates.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am delighted\xe2\x80\x94I am honored! Won\xe2\x80\x99t you come into the house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have just come out of it. I have been calling upon your mother. I\r\nhave been bidding her farewell.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFarewell?\xe2\x80\x9d Acton demanded.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am going away,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. And she turned away again, as if\r\nto illustrate her meaning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton, standing a moment in his place. But\r\nthe Baroness made no answer, and he followed her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI came this way to look at your garden,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, walking back to the\r\ngate, over the grass. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I must go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me at least go with you.\xe2\x80\x9d He went with her, and they said nothing\r\ntill they reached the gate. It was open, and they looked down the road\r\nwhich was darkened over with long bosky shadows. \xe2\x80\x9cMust you go straight\r\nhome?\xe2\x80\x9d Acton asked.\r\n\r\nBut she made no answer. She said, after a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy have you not\r\nbeen to see me?\xe2\x80\x9d He said nothing, and then she went on, \xe2\x80\x9cWhy don\xe2\x80\x99t you\r\nanswer me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am trying to invent an answer,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton confessed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you none ready?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone that I can tell you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut let me walk with you now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou may do as you like.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe moved slowly along the road, and Acton went with her. Presently he\r\nsaid, \xe2\x80\x9cIf I had done as I liked I would have come to see you several\r\ntimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that invented?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Eugenia.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, that is natural. I stayed away because\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, here comes the reason, then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause I wanted to think about you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you wanted to lie down!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cI have seen you\r\nlie down\xe2\x80\x94almost\xe2\x80\x94in my drawing-room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon stopped in the road, with a movement which seemed to beg her to\r\nlinger a little. She paused, and he looked at her awhile; he thought\r\nher very charming. \xe2\x80\x9cYou are jesting,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cbut if you are really\r\ngoing away it is very serious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I stay,\xe2\x80\x9d and she gave a little laugh, \xe2\x80\x9cit is more serious still!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen shall you go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs soon as possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd why?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I stay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause we all admire you so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is not a reason. I am admired also in Europe.\xe2\x80\x9d And she began to\r\nwalk homeward again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat could I say to keep you?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Acton. He wanted to keep her, and\r\nit was a fact that he had been thinking of her for a week. He was in\r\nlove with her now; he was conscious of that, or he thought he was; and\r\nthe only question with him was whether he could trust her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat you can say to keep me?\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9cAs I want very much to go\r\nit is not in my interest to tell you. Besides, I can\xe2\x80\x99t imagine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe went on with her in silence; he was much more affected by what she\r\nhad told him than appeared. Ever since that evening of his return from\r\nNewport her image had had a terrible power to trouble him. What\r\nClifford Wentworth had told him\xe2\x80\x94that had affected him, too, in an\r\nadverse sense; but it had not liberated him from the discomfort of a\r\ncharm of which his intelligence was impatient. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is not honest, she\r\nis not honest,\xe2\x80\x9d he kept murmuring to himself. That is what he had been\r\nsaying to the summer sky, ten minutes before. Unfortunately, he was\r\nunable to say it finally, definitively; and now that he was near her it\r\nseemed to matter wonderfully little. \xe2\x80\x9cShe is a woman who will lie,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nhad said to himself. He\r\nalmost wished he could make her lie and then convict her of it, so that\r\nhe might see how he should like that. He kept thinking of this as he\r\nwalked by her side, while she moved forward with her light, graceful\r\ndignity. He had sat with her before; he had driven with her; but he had\r\nnever walked with her.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy Jove, how _comme il faut_ she is!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, as he observed her\r\nsidewise. But he\r\nsaw her again that evening, for the Baroness reappeared at her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s.\r\nHe had little talk with her, however; two gentlemen had driven out from\r\nBoston, in a buggy, to call upon Mr. Wentworth and his daughters, and\r\nMadame M\xc3\xbcnster was an object of absorbing interest to both of the\r\nvisitors. One of them, indeed, said nothing to her; he only sat and\r\nwatched with intense gravity, and leaned forward solemnly, presenting\r\nhis ear (a very large one), as if he were deaf, whenever she dropped an\r\nobservation. He had evidently been impressed with the idea of her\r\nmisfortunes and reverses: he never smiled. His companion adopted a\r\nlighter, easier style; sat as near as possible to Madame M\xc3\xbcnster;\r\nattempted to draw her out, and proposed every few moments a new topic\r\nof conversation. Eugenia was less vividly responsive than usual and had\r\nless to say than, from her brilliant reputation, her interlocutor\r\nexpected, upon the relative merits of European and American\r\ninstitutions; but she was inaccessible to Robert Acton, who roamed\r\nabout the piazza with his hands in his pockets, listening for the\r\ngrating sound of the buggy from Boston, as it should be brought round\r\nto the side-door. But he listened in vain, and at last he lost\r\npatience. His sister came to him and begged him to take her home, and\r\nhe presently went off with her. Eugenia observed him leaving the house\r\nwith Lizzie; in her present mood the fact seemed a contribution to her\r\nirritated conviction that he had several precious qualities. \xe2\x80\x9cEven that\r\n_mal-\xc3\xa9lev\xc3\xa9e_ little girl,\xe2\x80\x9d she reflected, \xe2\x80\x9cmakes him do what she\r\nwishes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nShe had been sitting just within one of the long windows that opened\r\nupon the piazza; but very soon after Acton had gone away she got up\r\nabruptly, just when the talkative gentleman from Boston was asking her\r\nwhat she thought of the \xe2\x80\x9cmoral tone\xe2\x80\x9d of that city. And then, in an instant, \xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s the matter\r\nwith that buggy?\xe2\x80\x9d His practiced ear had apparently detected an\r\nunnatural creak in the wheels of the light vehicle which had been\r\nbrought to the portico, and he hurried away to investigate so grave an\r\nanomaly.\r\n\r\nThe Baroness walked homeward, alone, in the starlight, asking herself a\r\nquestion. Was she to have gained nothing\xe2\x80\x94was she to have gained\r\nnothing?\r\n\r\nGertrude Wentworth had held a silent place in the little circle\r\ngathered about the two gentlemen from Boston. She was not interested in\r\nthe visitors; she was watching Madame M\xc3\xbcnster, as she constantly\r\nwatched her. She knew that Eugenia also was not interested\xe2\x80\x94that she was\r\nbored; and Gertrude was absorbed in study of the problem how, in spite\r\nof her indifference and her absent attention, she managed to have such\r\na charming manner. That was the manner Gertrude would have liked to\r\nhave; she determined to cultivate it, and she wished that\xe2\x80\x94to give her\r\nthe charm\xe2\x80\x94she might in future very often be bored. While she was\r\nengaged in these researches, Felix Young was looking for Charlotte, to\r\nwhom he had something to say. For some time, now, he had had something\r\nto say to Charlotte, and this evening his sense of the propriety of\r\nholding some special conversation with her had reached the\r\nmotive-point\xe2\x80\x94resolved itself into acute and delightful desire. He\r\nwandered through the empty rooms on the large ground-floor of the\r\nhouse, and found her at last in a small apartment denominated, for\r\nreasons not immediately apparent, Mr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x9coffice:\xe2\x80\x9d an\r\nextremely neat and well-dusted room, with an array of law-books, in\r\ntime-darkened sheep-skin, on one of the walls; a large map of the\r\nUnited States on the other, flanked on either side by an old steel\r\nengraving of one of Raphael\xe2\x80\x99s Madonnas; and on the third several glass\r\ncases containing specimens of butterflies and beetles. Charlotte was\r\nsitting by a lamp, embroidering a slipper. Felix did not ask for whom\r\nthe slipper was destined; he saw it was very large.\r\n\r\nHe moved a chair toward her and sat down, smiling as usual, but, at\r\nfirst, not speaking. There was something in Felix\xe2\x80\x99s manner that quickened her modesty,\r\nher self-consciousness; if absolute choice had been given her she would\r\nhave preferred never to find herself alone with him; and in fact,\r\nthough she thought him a most brilliant, distinguished, and\r\nwell-meaning person, she had exercised a much larger amount of\r\ntremulous tact than he had ever suspected, to circumvent the accident\r\nof _t\xc3\xaate-\xc3\xa0-t\xc3\xaate_.
