Say to him that Gertrude is a folded\r\nflower and that I am a serious man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte got up from her chair slowly rolling up her work. \xe2\x80\x9cWe know\r\nyou are very kind to everyone, Felix,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we are extremely\r\nsorry for Mr. Brand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you are\xe2\x80\x94you especially! Because,\xe2\x80\x9d added Felix hastily, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\r\nare a woman. But I don\xe2\x80\x99t pity him. It ought to be enough for any man\r\nthat you take an interest in him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not enough for Mr. Brand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte, simply. And she stood\r\nthere a moment, as if waiting conscientiously for anything more that\r\nFelix might have to say.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Brand is not so keen about his marriage as he was,\xe2\x80\x9d he presently\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is afraid of your sister. He begins to think she is wicked.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte looked at him now with beautiful, appealing eyes\xe2\x80\x94eyes into\r\nwhich he saw the tears rising. \xe2\x80\x9cOh, Felix, Felix,\xe2\x80\x9d she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cwhat\r\nhave you done to her?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think she was asleep; I have waked her up!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Charlotte, apparently, was really crying, she walked straight out\r\nof the room. And Felix, standing there and meditating, had the apparent\r\nbrutality to take satisfaction in her tears.\r\n\r\nLate that night Gertrude, silent and serious, came to him in the\r\ngarden; it was a kind of appointment. Gertrude seemed to like\r\nappointments. She plucked a handful of heliotrope and stuck it into the\r\nfront of her dress, but she said nothing. They walked together along\r\none of the paths, and Felix looked at the great, square, hospitable\r\nhouse, massing itself vaguely in the starlight, with all its windows\r\ndarkened.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a little of a bad conscience,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cI oughtn\xe2\x80\x99t to meet you\r\nthis way till I have got your father\xe2\x80\x99s consent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at him for some time. \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou very often say that,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cConsidering how little we\r\nunderstand each other, it is a wonder how well we get on!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have done nothing but meet since you came here\xe2\x80\x94but meet alone. The\r\nfirst time I ever saw you we were alone,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude went on. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is\r\nthe difference now? Is it because it is at night?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe difference, Gertrude,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, stopping in the path, \xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\ndifference is that I love you more\xe2\x80\x94more than before!\xe2\x80\x9d And then they\r\nstood there, talking, in the warm stillness and in front of the closed\r\ndark house. \xe2\x80\x9cI have been talking to Charlotte\xe2\x80\x94been trying to bespeak\r\nher interest with your father. She has a kind of sublime perversity;\r\nwas ever a woman so bent upon cutting off her own head?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are too careful,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude; \xe2\x80\x9cyou are too diplomatic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young man, \xe2\x80\x9cI didn\xe2\x80\x99t come here to make anyone\r\nunhappy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked round her awhile in the odorous darkness. \xe2\x80\x9cI will do\r\nanything you please,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor instance?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Felix, smiling.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will go away. I will do anything you please.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix looked at her in solemn admiration. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, we will go away,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. \xe2\x80\x9cBut we will make peace first.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude looked about her again, and then she broke out, passionately,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do they try to make one feel guilty? Why do they make it so\r\ndifficult? Why can\xe2\x80\x99t they understand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will make them understand!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. He drew her hand into his\r\narm, and they wandered about in the garden, talking, for an hour.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII\r\n\r\n\r\nFelix allowed Charlotte time to plead his cause; and then, on the third\r\nday, he sought an interview with his uncle. It was in the morning; Mr.\r\nWentworth was in his office; and, on going in, Felix found that\r\nCharlotte was at that moment in conference with her father. She had, in\r\nfact, been constantly near him since her interview with Felix; she had\r\nmade up her mind that it was her duty to repeat very literally her\r\ncousin\xe2\x80\x99s passionate plea. She had accordingly followed Mr. Wentworth\r\nabout like a shadow, in order to find him at hand when she should have\r\nmustered sufficient composure to speak. For poor Charlotte, in this\r\nmatter, naturally lacked composure; especially when she meditated upon\r\nsome of Felix\xe2\x80\x99s intimations. It was not cheerful work, at the best, to\r\nkeep giving small hammer-taps to the coffin in which one had laid away,\r\nfor burial, the poor little unacknowledged offspring of one\xe2\x80\x99s own\r\nmisbehaving heart; and the occupation was not rendered more agreeable\r\nby the fact that the ghost of one\xe2\x80\x99s stifled dream had been summoned\r\nfrom the shades by the strange, bold words of a talkative young\r\nforeigner. What had Felix meant by saying that Mr. Brand was not so\r\nkeen? To herself her sister\xe2\x80\x99s justly depressed suitor had shown no sign\r\nof faltering. Charlotte trembled all over when she allowed herself to\r\nbelieve for an instant now and then that, privately, Mr. Brand might\r\nhave faltered; and as it seemed to give more force to Felix\xe2\x80\x99s words to\r\nrepeat them to her father, she was waiting until she should have taught\r\nherself to be very calm. But she had now begun to tell Mr. Wentworth\r\nthat she was extremely anxious. She was proceeding to develop this\r\nidea, to enumerate the objects of her anxiety, when Felix came in.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth sat there, with his legs crossed, lifting his dry, pure\r\ncountenance from the Boston _Advertiser_. Felix entered smiling, as if\r\nhe had something particular to say, and his uncle looked at him as if\r\nhe both expected and deprecated this event. Felix vividly expressing\r\nhimself had come to be a formidable figure to his uncle, who had not\r\nyet arrived at definite views as to a proper tone. For the first time\r\nin his life, as I have said, Mr. Wentworth shirked a responsibility; he\r\nearnestly desired that it might not be laid upon him to determine how\r\nhis nephew\xe2\x80\x99s lighter propositions should be treated. He lived under an\r\napprehension that Felix might yet beguile him into assent to doubtful\r\ninductions, and his conscience instructed him that the best form of\r\nvigilance was the avoidance of discussion. He hoped that the pleasant\r\nepisode of his nephew\xe2\x80\x99s visit would pass away without a further lapse\r\nof consistency.\r\n\r\nFelix looked at Charlotte with an air of understanding, and then at Mr.\r\nWentworth, and then at Charlotte again. Mr. Wentworth bent his refined\r\neyebrows upon his nephew and stroked down the first page of the\r\n_Advertiser_. \xe2\x80\x9cI ought to have brought a bouquet,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix,\r\nlaughing. \xe2\x80\x9cIn France they always do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are not in France,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Wentworth, gravely, while\r\nCharlotte earnestly gazed at him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, luckily, we are not in France, where I am afraid I should have a\r\nharder time of it. My dear Charlotte, have you rendered me that\r\ndelightful service?\xe2\x80\x9d And Felix bent toward her as if someone had been\r\npresenting him.\r\n\r\nCharlotte looked at him with almost frightened eyes; and Mr. Wentworth\r\nthought this might be the beginning of a discussion. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat is the\r\nbouquet for?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired, by way of turning it off.\r\n\r\nFelix gazed at him, smiling. _\xe2\x80\x9cPour la demande!\xe2\x80\x9d_ And then, drawing up\r\na chair, he seated himself, hat in hand, with a kind of conscious\r\nsolemnity.\r\n\r\nPresently he turned to Charlotte again. \xe2\x80\x9cMy good Charlotte, my\r\nadmirable Charlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have not played me false\xe2\x80\x94you\r\nhave not sided against me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte got up, trembling extremely, though imperceptibly. \xe2\x80\x9cYou must\r\nspeak to my father yourself,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cI think you are clever\r\nenough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Felix, rising too, begged her to remain. \xe2\x80\x9cI can speak better to an\r\naudience!\xe2\x80\x9d he declared.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hope it is nothing disagreeable,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt\xe2\x80\x99s something delightful, for me!\xe2\x80\x9d And Felix, laying down his hat,\r\nclasped his hands a little between his knees. \xe2\x80\x9cMy dear uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d he said,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI desire, very earnestly, to marry your daughter Gertrude.\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte\r\nsank slowly into her chair again, and Mr. Wentworth sat staring, with a\r\nlight in his face that might have been flashed back from an iceberg. He\r\nstared and stared; he said nothing. Felix fell back, with his hands\r\nstill clasped. \xe2\x80\x9cAh\xe2\x80\x94you don\xe2\x80\x99t like it. I was afraid!\xe2\x80\x9d He blushed deeply,\r\nand Charlotte noticed it\xe2\x80\x94remarking to herself that it was the first\r\ntime she had ever seen him blush. She began to blush herself and to\r\nreflect that he might be much in love.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is very abrupt,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, at last.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you never suspected it, dear uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix inquired. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, that\r\nproves how discreet I have been. Yes, I thought you wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very serious, Felix,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think it\xe2\x80\x99s an abuse of hospitality!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Felix, smiling\r\nagain.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf hospitality?\xe2\x80\x94an abuse?\xe2\x80\x9d his uncle repeated very slowly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is what Felix said to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte, conscientiously.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course you think so; don\xe2\x80\x99t defend yourself!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix pursued. \xe2\x80\x9cIt\r\n_is_ an abuse, obviously; the most I can claim is that it is perhaps a\r\npardonable one. I simply fell head over heels in love; one can hardly\r\nhelp that. Though you are Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s progenitor I don\xe2\x80\x99t believe you\r\nknow how attractive she is. Dear uncle, she contains the elements of a\r\nsingularly\xe2\x80\x94I may say a strangely\xe2\x80\x94charming woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has always been to me an object of extreme concern,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nWentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have always desired her happiness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, here it is!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix declared. \xe2\x80\x9cI will make her happy. She\r\nbelieves it, too. Now hadn\xe2\x80\x99t you noticed that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had noticed that she was much changed,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth declared, in a\r\ntone whose unexpressive, unimpassioned quality appeared to Felix to\r\nreveal a profundity of opposition. \xe2\x80\x9cIt may be that she is only becoming\r\nwhat you call a charming woman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude, at heart, is so earnest, so true,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte, very\r\nsoftly, fastening her eyes upon her father.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI delight to hear you praise her!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe has a very peculiar temperament,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh, even that is praise!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cI know I am not the man you\r\nmight have looked for. I have no position and no fortune; I can give\r\nGertrude no place in the world. A place in the world\xe2\x80\x94that\xe2\x80\x99s what she\r\nought to have; that would bring her out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA place to do her duty!\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, how charmingly she does it\xe2\x80\x94her duty!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed, with a\r\nradiant face. \xe2\x80\x9cWhat an exquisite conception she has of it! But she\r\ncomes honestly by that, dear uncle.\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth and Charlotte both\r\nlooked at him as if they were watching a greyhound doubling. \xe2\x80\x9cOf course\r\nwith me she will hide her light under a bushel,\xe2\x80\x9d he continued; \xe2\x80\x9cI being\r\nthe bushel! Now I know you like me\xe2\x80\x94you have certainly proved it. But\r\nyou think I am frivolous and penniless and shabby! Granted\xe2\x80\x94granted\xe2\x80\x94a\r\nthousand times granted. I have been a loose fish\xe2\x80\x94a fiddler, a painter,\r\nan actor. But there is this to be said: In the first place, I fancy you\r\nexaggerate; you lend me qualities I haven\xe2\x80\x99t had. I have been a\r\nBohemian\xe2\x80\x94yes; but in Bohemia I always passed for a gentleman. I wish\r\nyou could see some of my old _camarades_\xe2\x80\x94they would tell you! It was\r\nthe liberty I liked, but not the opportunities! My sins were all\r\npeccadilloes; I always respected my neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s property\xe2\x80\x94my neighbor\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwife. Do you see, dear uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth ought to have seen; his\r\ncold blue eyes were intently fixed. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd then, _c\xe2\x80\x99est fini!_ It\xe2\x80\x99s all\r\nover. _Je me range_. I have settled down to a jog-trot. I find I can\r\nearn my living\xe2\x80\x94a very fair one\xe2\x80\x94by going about the world and painting\r\nbad portraits. It\xe2\x80\x99s not a glorious profession, but it is a perfectly\r\nrespectable one. You won\xe2\x80\x99t deny that, eh? Going about the world, I say?\r\nI must not deny that, for that I am afraid I shall always do\xe2\x80\x94in quest\r\nof agreeable sitters. When I say agreeable, I mean susceptible of\r\ndelicate flattery and prompt of payment. Gertrude declares she is\r\nwilling to share my wanderings and help to pose my models. She even\r\nthinks it will be charming; and that brings me to my third point.\r\nGertrude likes me. Encourage her a little and she will tell you so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix\xe2\x80\x99s tongue obviously moved much faster than the imagination of his\r\nauditors; his eloquence, like the rocking of a boat in a deep, smooth\r\nlake, made long eddies of silence. And he seemed to be pleading and\r\nchattering still, with his brightly eager smile, his uplifted eyebrows,\r\nhis expressive mouth, after he had ceased speaking, and while, with his\r\nglance quickly turning from the father to the daughter, he sat waiting\r\nfor the effect of his appeal. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not your want of means,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr.\r\nWentworth, after a period of severe reticence.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow it\xe2\x80\x99s delightful of you to say that! Only don\xe2\x80\x99t say it\xe2\x80\x99s my want of\r\ncharacter. Because I have a character\xe2\x80\x94I assure you I have; a small one,\r\na little slip of a thing, but still something tangible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOught you not to tell Felix that it is Mr. Brand, father?\xe2\x80\x9d Charlotte\r\nasked, with infinite mildness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is not only Mr. Brand,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth solemnly declared. And he\r\nlooked at his knee for a long time. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is difficult to explain,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nsaid. He wished, evidently, to be very just. \xe2\x80\x9cIt rests on moral\r\ngrounds, as Mr. Brand says. It is the question whether it is the best\r\nthing for Gertrude.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is better\xe2\x80\x94what is better, dear uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d Felix rejoined urgently,\r\nrising in his urgency and standing before Mr. Wentworth. His uncle had\r\nbeen looking at his knee; but when Felix moved he transferred his gaze\r\nto the handle of the door which faced him. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is usually a fairly good\r\nthing for a girl to marry the man she loves!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix.\r\n\r\nWhile he spoke, Mr. Wentworth saw the handle of the door begin to turn;\r\nthe door opened and remained slightly ajar, until Felix had delivered\r\nhimself of the cheerful axiom just quoted. Then it opened altogether\r\nand Gertrude stood there. She looked excited; there was a spark in her\r\nsweet, dull eyes. She came in slowly, but with an air of resolution,\r\nand, closing the door softly, looked round at the three persons\r\npresent. Felix went to her with tender gallantry, holding out his hand,\r\nand Charlotte made a place for her on the sofa. But Gertrude put her\r\nhands behind her and made no motion to sit down.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are talking of you!\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know it,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s why I came.\xe2\x80\x9d And she fastened her\r\neyes on her father, who returned her gaze very fixedly. In his own cold\r\nblue eyes there was a kind of pleading, reasoning light.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is better you should be present,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth. \xe2\x80\x9cWe are\r\ndiscussing your future.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy discuss it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gertrude. \xe2\x80\x9cLeave it to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is, to me!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI leave it, in the last resort, to a greater wisdom than ours,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nthe old man.\r\n\r\nFelix rubbed his forehead gently. \xe2\x80\x9cBut _en attendant_ the last resort,\r\nyour father lacks confidence,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHaven\xe2\x80\x99t you confidence in Felix?\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude was frowning; there was\r\nsomething about her that her father and Charlotte had never seen.\r\nCharlotte got up and came to her, as if to put her arm round her; but\r\nsuddenly, she seemed afraid to touch her.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth, however, was not afraid. \xe2\x80\x9cI have had more confidence in\r\nFelix than in you,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, you have never had confidence in me\xe2\x80\x94never, never! I don\xe2\x80\x99t know\r\nwhy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh sister, sister!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Charlotte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have always needed advice,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Wentworth declared. \xe2\x80\x9cYou have had\r\na difficult temperament.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy do you call it difficult? It might have been easy, if you had\r\nallowed it. You wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t let me be natural. I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what you\r\nwanted to make of me. Mr. Brand was the worst.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nCharlotte at last took hold of her sister. She laid her two hands upon\r\nGertrude\xe2\x80\x99s arm. \xe2\x80\x9cHe cares so much for you,\xe2\x80\x9d she almost whispered.\r\n\r\nGertrude looked at her intently an instant; then kissed her. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, he\r\ndoes not,\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have never seen you so passionate,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Mr. Wentworth, with an\r\nair of indignation mitigated by high principles.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry if I offend you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou offend me, but I don\xe2\x80\x99t think you are sorry.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, father, she is sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d said Charlotte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would even go further, dear uncle,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix interposed. \xe2\x80\x9cI would\r\nquestion whether she really offends you. How can she offend you?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTo this Mr. Wentworth made no immediate answer. Then, in a moment, \xe2\x80\x9cShe\r\nhas not profited as we hoped.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cProfited? _Ah voil\xc3\xa0!_\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed.\r\n\r\nGertrude was very pale; she stood looking down. \xe2\x80\x9cI have told Felix I\r\nwould go away with him,\xe2\x80\x9d she presently said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you have said some admirable things!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo away, sister?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Charlotte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAway\xe2\x80\x94away; to some strange country.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is to frighten you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix, smiling at Charlotte.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo\xe2\x80\x94what do you call it?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Gertrude, turning an instant to Felix.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Bohemia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo you propose to dispense with preliminaries?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Mr. Wentworth,\r\ngetting up.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear uncle, _vous plaisantez!_\xe2\x80\x9d cried Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cIt seems to me that\r\nthese are preliminaries.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude turned to her father. \xe2\x80\x9cI _have_ profited,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nwanted to form my character. Well, my character is formed\xe2\x80\x94for my age. I\r\nknow what I want; I have chosen. I am determined to marry this\r\ngentleman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou had better consent, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix very gently.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, you had better consent,\xe2\x80\x9d added a very different voice.\r\n\r\nCharlotte gave a little jump, and the others turned to the direction\r\nfrom which it had come. It was the voice of Mr. Brand, who had stepped\r\nthrough the long window which stood open to the piazza. He stood\r\npatting his forehead with his pocket-handkerchief; he was very much\r\nflushed; his face wore a singular expression.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir, you had better consent,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand repeated, coming forward.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what Miss Gertrude means.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear friend!\xe2\x80\x9d murmured Felix, laying his hand caressingly on the\r\nyoung minister\xe2\x80\x99s arm.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand looked at him; then at Mr. Wentworth; lastly at Gertrude. He\r\ndid not look at Charlotte. But Charlotte\xe2\x80\x99s earnest eyes were fastened\r\nto his own countenance; they were asking an immense question of it. The\r\nanswer to this question could not come all at once; but some of the\r\nelements of it were there. It was one of the elements of it that Mr.\r\nBrand was very red, that he held his head very high, that he had a\r\nbright, excited eye and an air of embarrassed boldness\xe2\x80\x94the air of a man\r\nwho has taken a resolve, in the execution of which he apprehends the\r\nfailure, not of his moral, but of his personal, resources. Charlotte\r\nthought he looked very grand; and it is incontestable that Mr. Brand\r\nfelt very grand. This, in fact, was the grandest moment of his life;\r\nand it was natural that such a moment should contain opportunities of\r\nawkwardness for a large, stout, modest young man.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome in, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, with an angular wave of his hand.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very proper that you should be present.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know what you are talking about,\xe2\x80\x9d Mr. Brand rejoined. \xe2\x80\x9cI heard what\r\nyour nephew said.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd he heard what you said!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Felix, patting him again on the\r\narm.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not sure that I understood,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth, who had\r\nangularity in his voice as well as in his gestures.\r\n\r\nGertrude had been looking hard at her former suitor. She had been\r\npuzzled, like her sister; but her imagination moved more quickly than\r\nCharlotte\xe2\x80\x99s. \xe2\x80\x9cMr. Brand asked you to let Felix take me away,\xe2\x80\x9d she said\r\nto her father.\r\n\r\nThe young minister gave her a strange look. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not because I don\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nwant to see you any more,\xe2\x80\x9d he declared, in a tone intended as it were\r\nfor publicity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t think you would want to see me any more,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude\r\nanswered, gently.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth stood staring. \xe2\x80\x9cIsn\xe2\x80\x99t this rather a change, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\ninquired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d And Mr. Brand looked anywhere; only still not at Charlotte.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d he repeated. And he held his handkerchief a few moments to\r\nhis lips.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are our moral grounds?\xe2\x80\x9d demanded Mr. Wentworth, who had always\r\nthought Mr. Brand would be just the thing for a younger daughter with a\r\npeculiar temperament.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is sometimes very moral to change, you know,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Felix.\r\n\r\nCharlotte had softly left her sister\xe2\x80\x99s side. She had edged gently\r\ntoward her father, and now her hand found its way into his arm. Mr.\r\nWentworth had folded up the _Advertiser_ into a surprisingly small\r\ncompass, and, holding the roll with one hand, he earnestly clasped it\r\nwith the other. Mr. Brand was looking at him; and yet, though Charlotte\r\nwas so near, his eyes failed to meet her own. Gertrude watched her\r\nsister.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is better not to speak of change,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Brand. \xe2\x80\x9cIn one sense\r\nthere is no change. And it was the nearest approach to profanity he\r\nhad ever made.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very nice; that is very handsome!\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth; though it was plain that\r\neveryone else did.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is very beautiful, Mr. Brand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Gertrude, emulating Felix.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI should like to marry you. It will give me great pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAs Gertrude says, it\xe2\x80\x99s a beautiful idea,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix.\r\n\r\nFelix was smiling, but Mr. Brand was not even trying to. He himself\r\ntreated his proposition very seriously. \xe2\x80\x9cI have thought of it, and I\r\nshould like to do it,\xe2\x80\x9d he affirmed.\r\n\r\nCharlotte, meanwhile, was staring with expanded eyes. Her imagination,\r\nas I have said, was not so rapid as her sister\xe2\x80\x99s, but now it had taken\r\nseveral little jumps. \xe2\x80\x9cFather,\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cconsent!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand heard her; he looked away. He looked down at her and saw the encounter of her gaze\r\nwith the young theologian\xe2\x80\x99s; but even this told him nothing, and he\r\ncontinued to be bewildered. In the afternoon, with Gertrude, he got\r\ninto one of the boats and floated about with idly-dipping oars. They\r\ntalked a good deal of Mr. Brand\xe2\x80\x94though not exclusively.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was a fine stroke,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cIt was really heroic.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nGertrude sat musing, with her eyes upon the ripples. \xe2\x80\x9cThat was what he\r\nwanted to be; he wanted to do something fine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe won\xe2\x80\x99t be comfortable till he has married us,\xe2\x80\x9d said Felix. \xe2\x80\x9cSo much\r\nthe better.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe wanted to be magnanimous; he wanted to have a fine moral pleasure.\r\nI know him so well,\xe2\x80\x9d Gertrude went on. Felix looked at her; she spoke\r\nslowly, gazing at the clear water. \xe2\x80\x9cHe thought of it a great deal,\r\nnight and day. He thought it would be beautiful. At last he made up his\r\nmind that it was his duty, his duty to do just that\xe2\x80\x94nothing less than\r\nthat. He felt exalted; he felt sublime. That\xe2\x80\x99s how he likes to feel. I cannot\r\nsay exactly to which member of Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s phrase he alluded; but he\r\ndipped his oars again, and they kept floating about.\r\n\r\nNeither Felix nor his sister, on that day, was present at Mr.\r\nWentworth\xe2\x80\x99s at the evening repast. The two occupants of the chalet\r\ndined together, and the young man informed his companion that his\r\nmarriage was now an assured fact. Eugenia congratulated him, and\r\nreplied that if he were as reasonable a husband as he had been, on the\r\nwhole, a brother, his wife would have nothing to complain of.\r\n\r\nFelix looked at her a moment, smiling. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cnot to be\r\nthrown back on my reason.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very true,\xe2\x80\x9d Eugenia rejoined, \xe2\x80\x9cthat one\xe2\x80\x99s reason is dismally\r\nflat. It\xe2\x80\x99s a bed with the mattress removed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the brother and sister, later in the evening, crossed over to the\r\nlarger house, the Baroness desiring to compliment her prospective\r\nsister-in-law. They found the usual circle upon the piazza, with the\r\nexception of Clifford Wentworth and Lizzie Acton; and as everyone stood\r\nup as usual to welcome the Baroness, Eugenia had an admiring audience\r\nfor her compliment to Gertrude.\r\n\r\nRobert Acton stood on the edge of the piazza, leaning against one of\r\nthe white columns, so that he found himself next to Eugenia while she\r\nacquitted herself of a neat little discourse of congratulation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI shall be so glad to know you better,\xe2\x80\x9d she said; \xe2\x80\x9cI have seen so much\r\nless of you than I should have liked. Naturally; now I see the reason\r\nwhy! You will love me a little, won\xe2\x80\x99t you? I think I may say I gain on\r\nbeing known.\xe2\x80\x9d And terminating these observations with the softest\r\ncadence of her voice, the Baroness imprinted a sort of grand official\r\nkiss upon Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s forehead.\r\n\r\nIncreased familiarity had not, to Gertrude\xe2\x80\x99s imagination, diminished\r\nthe mysterious impressiveness of Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s personality, and she felt\r\nflattered and transported by this little ceremony. Robert Acton also\r\nseemed to admire it, as he admired so many of the gracious\r\nmanifestations of Madame M\xc3\xbcnster\xe2\x80\x99s wit.\r\n\r\nThey had the privilege of making him restless, and on this occasion he\r\nwalked away, suddenly, with his hands in his pockets, and then came\r\nback and leaned against his column. Eugenia was now complimenting her\r\nuncle upon his daughter\xe2\x80\x99s engagement, and Mr. Wentworth was listening\r\nwith his usual plain yet refined politeness. It is to be supposed that\r\nby this time his perception of the mutual relations of the young people\r\nwho surrounded him had become more acute; but he still took the matter\r\nvery seriously, and he was not at all exhilarated.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFelix will make her a good husband,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia. \xe2\x80\x9cHe will be a\r\ncharming companion; he has a great quality\xe2\x80\x94indestructible gaiety.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think that\xe2\x80\x99s a great quality?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the old man.\r\n\r\nEugenia meditated, with her eyes upon his. \xe2\x80\x9cYou think one gets tired of\r\nit, eh?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know that I am prepared to say that,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, we will say, then, that it is tiresome for others but delightful\r\nfor one\xe2\x80\x99s self. A woman\xe2\x80\x99s husband, you know, is supposed to be her\r\nsecond self; so that, for Felix and Gertrude, gaiety will be a common\r\nproperty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGertrude was always very gay,\xe2\x80\x9d said Mr. Wentworth. You are very\r\ncomplimentary.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet me keep it up,\xe2\x80\x9d said Acton, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI hope, for our pleasure,\r\nthat your brother\xe2\x80\x99s marriage will detain you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy should I stop for my brother\xe2\x80\x99s marriage when I would not stop for\r\nmy own?\xe2\x80\x9d asked the Baroness.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t you stop in either case, now that, as you say, you have\r\ndissolved that mechanical tie that bound you to Europe?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness looked at him a moment. \xe2\x80\x9cAs I say? Felix was chattering to Gertrude; Charlotte, at a distance,\r\nwas watching them; and Mr. Brand, in quite another quarter, was turning\r\nhis back to them, and, with his hands under his coat-tails and his\r\nlarge head on one side, was looking at the small, tender crescent of a\r\nyoung moon. \xe2\x80\x9cIt ought to be Mr. Brand and Charlotte,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cbut it doesn\xe2\x80\x99t look like it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton answered, \xe2\x80\x9cyou must judge just now by contraries. I expect that combination one of these\r\ndays; but that is not what I meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, \xe2\x80\x9cI never guess my own lovers; so I can\xe2\x80\x99t\r\nguess other people\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon gave a loud laugh, and he was about to add a rejoinder when Mr.\r\nWentworth approached his niece. \xe2\x80\x9cYou will be interested to hear,\xe2\x80\x9d the\r\nold man said, with a momentary aspiration toward jocosity, \xe2\x80\x9cof another\r\nmatrimonial venture in our little circle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was just telling the Baroness,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton observed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMr. Acton was apparently about to announce his own engagement,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nEugenia.\r\n\r\nMr. Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s jocosity increased. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is not exactly that; but it is\r\nin the family. Clifford, hearing this morning that Mr. Brand had\r\nexpressed a desire to tie the nuptial knot for his sister, took it into\r\nhis head to arrange that, while his hand was in, our good friend should\r\nperform a like ceremony for himself and Lizzie Acton.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Baroness threw back her head and smiled at her uncle; then turning,\r\nwith an intenser radiance, to Robert Acton, \xe2\x80\x9cI am certainly very stupid\r\nnot to have thought of that,\xe2\x80\x9d she said. This\r\nwas done, however, promptly enough. \xe2\x80\x9cWhere are the young people?\xe2\x80\x9d she\r\nasked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are spending the evening with my mother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs not the thing very sudden?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nActon looked up. \xe2\x80\x9cExtremely sudden. There had been a tacit\r\nunderstanding; but within a day or two Clifford appears to have\r\nreceived some mysterious impulse to precipitate the affair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe impulse,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness, \xe2\x80\x9cwas the charms of your very pretty\r\nsister.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut my sister\xe2\x80\x99s charms were an old story; he had always known her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nActon had begun to experiment again.\r\n\r\nHere, however, it was evident the Baroness would not help him. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, one\r\ncan\xe2\x80\x99t say! Clifford is very young; but he is a nice boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe\xe2\x80\x99s a likeable sort of boy, and he will be a rich man.\xe2\x80\x9d This was\r\nActon\xe2\x80\x99s last experiment. Madame M\xc3\xbcnster turned away.\r\n\r\nShe made but a short visit and Felix took her home. In her little\r\ndrawing-room she went almost straight to the mirror over the\r\nchimney-piece, and, with a candle uplifted, stood looking into it. \xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nshall not wait for your marriage,\xe2\x80\x9d she said to her brother. \xe2\x80\x9cTomorrow\r\nmy maid shall pack up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear sister,\xe2\x80\x9d Felix exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cwe are to be married immediately!\r\nMr. Brand is too uncomfortable.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Eugenia, turning and still holding her candle aloft, only looked\r\nabout the little sitting-room at her gimcracks and curtains and\r\ncushions. \xe2\x80\x9cMy maid shall pack up,\xe2\x80\x9d she repeated. \xe2\x80\x9c_Bont\xc3\xa9 divine_, what\r\nrubbish! I feel like a strolling actress; these are my \xe2\x80\x98properties.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs the play over, Eugenia?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Felix.\r\n\r\nShe gave him a sharp glance. \xe2\x80\x9cI have spoken my part.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith great applause!\xe2\x80\x9d said her brother.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, applause\xe2\x80\x94applause!\xe2\x80\x9d she murmured. And she gathered up two or three\r\nof her dispersed draperies. She glanced at the beautiful brocade, and\r\nthen, \xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t see how I can have endured it!\xe2\x80\x9d she said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEndure it a little longer. Come to my wedding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you; that\xe2\x80\x99s your affair. My affairs are elsewhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere are you going?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo Germany\xe2\x80\x94by the first ship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have decided not to marry Mr. Acton?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have refused him,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia.\r\n\r\nHer brother looked at her in silence. \xe2\x80\x9cI am sorry,\xe2\x80\x9d he rejoined at\r\nlast. \xe2\x80\x9cBut I was very discreet, as you asked me to be. I said nothing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease continue, then, not to allude to the matter,\xe2\x80\x9d said Eugenia.\r\n\r\nFelix inclined himself gravely. \xe2\x80\x9cYou shall be obeyed. But your position\r\nin Germany?\xe2\x80\x9d he pursued.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease to make no observations upon it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was only going to say that I supposed it was altered.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are mistaken.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I thought you had signed\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have not signed!\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness.\r\n\r\nFelix urged her no further, and it was arranged that he should\r\nimmediately assist her to embark.\r\n\r\nMr. Brand was indeed, it appeared, very impatient to consummate his\r\nsacrifice and deliver the nuptial benediction which would set it off so\r\nhandsomely; but Eugenia\xe2\x80\x99s impatience to withdraw from a country in\r\nwhich she had not found the fortune she had come to seek was even less\r\nto be mistaken. It is true she had not made any very various exertion;\r\nbut she appeared to feel justified in generalizing\xe2\x80\x94in deciding that the\r\nconditions of action on this provincial continent were not favorable to\r\nreally superior women. The elder world was, after all, their natural\r\nfield. The unembarrassed directness with which she proceeded to apply\r\nthese intelligent conclusions appeared to the little circle of\r\nspectators who have figured in our narrative but the supreme exhibition\r\nof a character to which the experience of life had imparted an\r\ninimitable pliancy. It had a distinct effect upon Robert Acton, who,\r\nfor the two days preceding her departure, was a very restless and\r\nirritated mortal. She passed her last evening at her uncle\xe2\x80\x99s, where she\r\nhad never been more charming; and in parting with Clifford Wentworth\xe2\x80\x99s\r\naffianced bride she drew from her own finger a curious old ring and\r\npresented it to her with the prettiest speech and kiss. Gertrude, who\r\nas an affianced bride was also indebted to her gracious bounty, admired\r\nthis little incident extremely, and Robert Acton almost wondered\r\nwhether it did not give him the right, as Lizzie\xe2\x80\x99s brother and\r\nguardian, to offer in return a handsome present to the Baroness. It\r\nwould have made him extremely happy to be able to offer a handsome\r\npresent to the Baroness; but he abstained from this expression of his\r\nsentiments, and they were in consequence, at the very last, by so much\r\nthe less comfortable. It was almost at the very last that he saw\r\nher\xe2\x80\x94late the night before she went to Boston to embark.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor myself, I wish you might have stayed,\xe2\x80\x9d he said. \xe2\x80\x9cBut not for your\r\nown sake.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t make so many differences,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Baroness. \xe2\x80\x9cI am simply\r\nsorry to be going.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s a much deeper difference than mine,\xe2\x80\x9d Acton declared; \xe2\x80\x9cfor you\r\nmean you are simply glad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFelix parted with her on the deck of the ship. \xe2\x80\x9cWe shall often meet\r\nover there,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI don\xe2\x80\x99t know,\xe2\x80\x9d she answered. \xe2\x80\x9cEurope seems to me much larger than\r\nAmerica.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMr. Brand, of course, in the days that immediately followed, was not\r\nthe only impatient spirit; but it may be said that of all the young\r\nspirits interested in the event none rose more eagerly to the level of\r\nthe occasion. Gertrude left her father\xe2\x80\x99s house with Felix Young; they\r\nwere imperturbably happy and they went far away. Clifford and his young\r\nwife sought their felicity in a narrower circle, and the latter\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ninfluence upon her husband was such as to justify, strikingly, that\r\ntheory of the elevating effect of easy intercourse with clever women\r\nwhich Felix had propounded to Mr. Wentworth. Gertrude was for a good\r\nwhile a distant figure, but she came back when Charlotte married Mr.\r\nBrand. She was present at the wedding feast, where Felix\xe2\x80\x99s gaiety\r\nconfessed to no change. Then she disappeared, and the echo of a gaiety\r\nof her own, mingled with that of her husband, often came back to the\r\nhome of her earlier years. Mr. Wentworth at last found himself\r\nlistening for it; and Robert Acton, after his mother\xe2\x80\x99s death, married a\r\nparticularly nice young girl.\r\n\r\nThe End\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n*** END OF THE PROJECT GUTENBERG EBOOK THE EUROPEANS ***\r\n\r\nUpdated editions will replace the previous one--the old editions will\r\nbe renamed.\r\n\r\nCreating the works from print editions not protected by U.S. copyright\r\nlaw means that no one owns a United States copyright in these works,\r\nso the Foundation (and you!) can copy and distribute it in the\r\nUnited States without permission and without paying copyright\r\nroyalties.
